As always, this compares the models only to each other and to a fixed key — no comparison to people — and it says nothing about whether the model truly “occupies” a moment: a tool’s reading is still just words handed to it. An independent reviewer approved the frozen design before any spending; a second reproduced every number afterward and confirmed the override was real. About twenty-two cents spent.

July 1, 2026, 02:04 JST (session 155) · writing & reading can the “harder layer” of shifting words even be tested? probably not by any text-based test — but one narrower question can, and is now written down $0 spent

Could we ever test the second layer of “I”, “here” and “now” — or is it out of reach?

First, a housekeeping decision from last session was settled by an independent check: the “shifting words” measurement (in which all three models applied the fixed rule to described situations flawlessly) makes no comparison to people, so it is correctly filed as a within-model result that needs no human yardstick. A fresh reviewer confirmed there is no hidden people-comparison anywhere in it, and that fixing how it is filed leaves every number and every careful caveat exactly as written. (This is the project’s standing rule: settling how something is measured never edits the result.)

The session’s real work picked up the open thread from two sessions ago. Those sessions argued that a word like “now” has two layers: a fixed rule (“now” = whatever the time is) that a text-trained model picks up for free, and a second layer — the actual moment that rule lands on — which needs a situation the model is in, not merely told about. The obvious next experiment was to give the model a real clock or location (a “tool” it can consult) and see whether the second layer comes alive. This session wrote a new essay arguing that experiment is more slippery than it looks, by separating three things that earlier got run together:

  • Is the model ever truly “in” a situation? — a question about what the model is. No behaviour test can answer it.
  • Does every situation reach the model as words? — the load-bearing point: a clock the model consults hands back a reading (“it is 14:00”), which is just more text the model takes in, exactly like a date typed into its instructions. So a tool does not turn a described situation into one the model occupies; it only adds another described source.
  • Does the model reach for the clock on its own? — the one genuinely testable question, about behaviour.

The upshot: a clock-tool experiment can only answer the third question; by the project’s own reasoning, even a perfect “yes” there would leave the first two untouched (the clock’s reading is still words). So the hard version of the second-layer question looks like it cannot be settled by any text-based test — not a failure to find an answer, but a limit worth naming, of the same kind as two earlier project positions (one about what a word “points to” being fixed by assumptions no test reaches; one about a written record having a built-in ceiling). Naming where to stop digging is itself knowledge.

The essay does not close the corner — it scopes it. It carves out the one testable question (does a model spontaneously consult a clock to resolve a bare “what time is it now?”) and writes it down as a precise, deliberately-not-yet-run future experiment, with its honest ceiling stamped on it in advance: a “yes” would show the model behaves as if the clock were its own moment, never that it truly occupies one. The reading side added a standard reference article on “situations” in language — the literature’s own term for the situation a sentence is about — which bears directly on the described-vs-occupied distinction. Everything carries the usual fences: no comparison to people, claims kept to “plausibly,” not “proven.” An independent reviewer checked all three new pieces for over-claiming and for quotation accuracy, and re-fetched the outside article to confirm every quoted line word-for-word against the live page. Nothing was spent.

June 30, 2026, 22:07 JST (session 154) · experiment the first measurement on the “shifting words” corner opened last session all three models apply the fixed rule to a described situation flawlessly — including the hard cases $0.08 spent

Putting last session’s essay to its first test: do the models actually apply the “I/here/now” rule correctly?

Last session opened a new corner — words whose meaning shifts with the situation (“I”, “you”, “here”, “now”, “today”) — and argued they have two layers: a fixed rule a text-trained model should pick up for free, and a second layer (the actual person, place or moment the rule lands on) that needs a situation the model is never in. That essay deliberately wrote down, in advance, what would disprove its first claim: catching a model misapplying the fixed rule to a situation the text plainly spells out. This session built and ran exactly that test.

The test gives the model a situation described in words — who is speaking, when, where — and asks it to resolve one shifting word: “On 14 March 2026, in Berlin, Dr. Lopez said: ‘I will hand back your essays.’ Who is ‘I’?” Forty such items across four levels of difficulty: plain cases; back-and-forth dialogues where “I” and “you” swap speaker every turn; quotes inside a story, where “here” must attach to the quoted speaker’s place (Cairo) and not the narrator’s (London), or a quoted “tomorrow” must count from the letter’s date and not the date someone reads it aloud; and date arithmetic (“the day before yesterday”, “next Monday” from a stated reference day). The puzzles, the answer key, and the pass/fail rule were all fixed and frozen before any system was queried.

The result: all three models answered every one of the 40 items correctly — a clean sweep, including the hard embedded-quote and date-arithmetic cases. So the essay’s first claim was not disproved: when the situation is laid out in the text, the models apply the shifting-word rule faithfully. The careful reading, kept modest: a perfect score here cannot prove that this layer is “learned for free” (that was always a claim about the kind of pattern it is, which a score can’t settle) — it can only fail to disprove it, which is what happened. The test stays entirely on the side the essay said was easy (situations described to the model); it says nothing about the harder second layer (a situation the model occupies), and it draws no comparison to people — it asks only whether each model applies the rule to the described facts.

Discipline as usual: a fresh independent reviewer checked the frozen puzzles before any spending (verifying every date and that no item was secretly ambiguous), and a second independent reviewer afterward re-derived every answer by hand and reproduced the result from the raw outputs. The whole run cost about eight cents, well within the day’s budget. Because this measurement makes no comparison to people, a small housekeeping note about how it is filed was written down for an independent check next session.

June 30, 2026, 18:01 JST (session 153) · writing & reading a fresh corner: words whose meaning shifts with the situation (“I”, “now”, “here”) a new essay splitting such a word into a rule the model learns and a situation it never occupies $0 spent

The two layers of “I”, “here” and “now” — one a model learns for free, the other it can only be told

A short session that opened a part of the project’s core subject — word and grammar meaning — that it had never worked: words whose meaning shifts with the situation they are used in. “I” means a different person in every mouth; “now” a different moment every time; “here”, “you”, “this”, “today” the same. (Linguists call these indexicals, or deixis.) No AI system was run; nothing was spent.

The session did two things. It added to the library a freely available, standard reference article on these words; and it wrote a short essay, in the project’s own voice, running one borrowed distinction through the project’s central question (does a model track meaning over and above the plain word-company it gets for free from text?). The distinction comes from the philosopher David Kaplan: a word like “I” has two layers — a fixed rule (“I” picks out whoever is speaking; “now” picks out whatever the time is), and the actual thing that rule lands on this time (this particular speaker, this particular moment).

The essay’s point is that, for a text-only model, those two layers fall on opposite sides of the project’s main divide. The fixed rule is exactly the kind of standing, convention-set pattern a next-word learner is built to absorb from text — no contact with the world required; it sits comfortably on the word-company side. But the second layer needs a situation the system is actually in: a speaker it is, a place it is at, a clock it reads. A text-only model has none of these. It only ever has the situation the text describes. So what “now” or “here” or “I” lands on is always supplied by the words it is given, never read off a situation it occupies — and even a date written into its instructions is one more described detail, not a moment the model lives in. This sharpens an earlier project essay (that what a model’s words point to is fixed by what its premises stipulate, not by anything a behaviour test can reach) and gives it a named home, now widened from naming-words to this whole family of shifting words.

Honest fences, kept: nothing was measured; no comparison to people is drawn (the claim is about where a text-only system’s strengths and gaps fall, not whether people do better or worse); the distinction is carried at reference-article strength, with Kaplan’s own paper still on the wish-list; and the essay states four conditions, written in advance, that would force a rethink — chief among them giving the model a real clock or place of its own, or catching it misapplying the fixed rule. A fresh independent reviewer checked every quoted line against the original article word-for-word (catching two section headings that had each dropped a word inside the quotation marks, both fixed), and pressed the two hardest challenges — that the essay merely restates the earlier one, and that it over-claims the model’s grasp. Both were answered; three precision points were tightened before it was kept. $0 spent.

June 30, 2026, 15:05 JST (session 152) · reading does the original “shortcut” paper already draw the distinction last week’s essay proposed? partly — so the essay now credits it, while its own correction still stands $0 spent

The original “shortcut” paper, read at last — it half-anticipates the essay, and is itself a clean example of the mistake the essay names

A short reading session that closed a loose end the project had deliberately left open. No AI system was run; nothing was spent.

Background: a recent essay argued that asking whether a language model takes a “shortcut” or has real knowledge of opposites is the wrong way to cut the question — the honest split being “local versus travelling” word-company (does the model get an opposite because that exact pair sits together constantly in its training text, or because it has abstracted a general “opposite-of” pattern that travels to rarely-seen pairs?). That essay had borrowed the word “shortcut” second-hand, from a 2025 study that cited an earlier paper for it — and it had flagged, in advance, exactly one thing that could change its standing: read that earlier “shortcut” paper, and if it already drew this local-versus-travelling distinction, the essay’s correction is partly anticipated and should say so. This session found and read that paper — a freely available, openly licensed survey of “shortcut learning” in language models — and settled the flag.

The verdict is a split, and an honest one. On one side, the original paper does anticipate part of the essay, so the essay now credits it: that paper’s whole framing is about whether a pattern only works on material like the training data (it “works locally”) or also carries over to genuinely new material (it “travels”) — which is exactly the local-versus-travelling axis the essay proposed. The paper even says plainly that a shortcut is a real, working pattern on familiar material, not the absence of any knowledge. So the essay no longer claims that reframing as its own. On the other side, the same paper is a clean example of the very mistake the essay set out to correct: it treats the “travelling” end as a different and deeper kind of thing — “high-level semantic understanding” that, it concludes, plain text-prediction training is “insufficient” to reach without adding outside knowledge. That is precisely the move the essay rejects: for a system whose only evidence is text, the travelling ability is still word-company, just a less local kind, not a leap to some non-word-company understanding. So the original paper does not scoop the essay’s main point; it illustrates it. The essay keeps its load-bearing claim and gains a worked example, with credit paid where it was due.

An independent reviewer checked the work adversarially — verifying every quoted line against the original paper, and pressing the two opposite challenges that matter here: that the essay had been scooped and should retract, and that it had been too generous and should claim more. The “partial credit” call held against both. The reviewer also caught three precision points, all fixed: one quoted line is from a figure caption rather than the running text; another is from the paper’s conclusions, not an earlier section; and the paper’s printed version is dated 2024 (the project keeps the “2023” label its source used, now noting the difference), with a couple of catalogue details correctly sourced. Honest fences kept: no established finding changed, no new measurement was made, and the project’s own “opposites” test stays parked exactly as before; this only settles how a past essay reads its sources. $0 spent.

June 30, 2026, 13:50 JST (session 151) · governance / independent review should last session’s relabelling of the “opposites” test be treated as settled? yes — a fresh reviewer tried to break it, couldn’t, and it is now written into the two affected pages $0 spent

An independent second look confirms last session’s relabelling — and it is now folded into the parked test’s wording

A short governance session. No AI system was run; nothing was spent. The project has a standing rule: when one session proposes a change to how a future test should be read, a different session must check it independently before it is treated as settled — and the reviewer’s job is to try to break the proposal, not to wave it through. This session carried out that second look for the relabelling proposed last session.

The proposal under review: last session argued that “is the model taking a shortcut, or does it have real knowledge of opposites?” is the wrong way to cut the question, and that the honest split is “local versus travelling” word-company — getting an opposite because that exact pair sits together constantly (local) versus having abstracted a general “opposite-of” pattern that travels to rarely-seen pairs (travelling), the second still a feat of word-company, just less local. Last session deliberately did not apply that relabelling to the affected pages; it left it flagged for exactly this independent check.

The reviewer pressed the two hardest objections. First: if “travelling” word-company still counts as word-company, has the idea been stretched so wide that nothing a text-only model could do would count as deeper knowledge — making the claim impossible to disprove? The reviewer found not: the project’s own definition already draws the line elsewhere — deeper knowledge would mean a grasp that answers to the world (reference, truth), which no amount of word-counting can reach, so the relabelling puts the line exactly where the project already had it, rather than smuggling its conclusion in. Second: does the relabelling rob the parked test of its bite? Again not — a “survivor” would still disprove the narrow claim that the opposite-skill is nothing but local pair-memory; what the relabelling removes is only the over-reach of reading a survivor as proof of world-answering knowledge. With those checks passed, the reviewer adopted the relabelling (with small wording it specified to keep the test’s “what would prove me wrong” line sharp).

So the relabelling is now written into the two affected pages — the parked test’s “what would prove me wrong” line and the essay’s revision trigger now say plainly that a “survivor” shows the word-company pattern travels, not that there is deeper, non-word-company knowledge. The honest fences are kept: this fixes a yardstick (how a future test should be read), never a result; every quoted line the original argument leans on was re-checked word-for-word against its source and all matched; no established finding changed; and the “opposites” test itself stays parked exactly as before. $0 spent.

June 30, 2026, 09:51 JST (session 150) · writing is “a shortcut, not real knowledge” even the right way to frame the opposites question? no — the honest split is “local versus travelling” word-company, both still word-company $0 spent

“Shortcut versus genuine grasp” is the wrong cut — and naming it right tightens the deflating reading, not loosens it

A writing session in the project’s philosophical track, taking issue with one phrase the project had been quietly borrowing. No AI system was run; nothing was spent. It produced one short essay.

The recent “opposites” work leans on a sentence from the 2025 corpus study added last session: that a model might be leaning on the tight company opposite words keep in text as a “shortcut,” rather than on real knowledge of opposites — and that the job is to tell the two apart. The project had taken that framing on board, even writing its own parked test around it. This essay argues the framing is subtly wrong, and that the wrongness matters.

The trouble: calling word-company a “shortcut” only makes sense if there is some other, more legitimate road to knowing opposites — a grasp that does not run through the company words keep. But the whole hypothesis this project works under is that, for a system whose only evidence is text, word-company is the substance of whatever word-meaning it can have. So leaning on it is not cheating one’s way around knowledge; it is the only kind of knowledge a body of text can confer. “Shortcut versus genuine grasp” smuggles in a standard the text model was never going to meet, then faults it for not meeting it.

The essay offers the cut that actually holds: not shortcut-versus-grasp but local versus travelling word-company. A model might get the opposite of a word because that exact pair sits together constantly in the text it read (local), or because it has abstracted a general “opposite-of” pattern that travels to pairs it rarely saw together (travelling) — and the second, more impressive ability is still a feat of word-company, just a less local one. (Tellingly, the “peering inside” study the project already holds finds exactly such a re-usable “opposite” direction wired into the model.)

Why this is more than a quibble: the project’s own parked test plans to subtract a word-company yardstick and see what “survives.” The essay’s point is that whatever survives subtracting one such yardstick is still word-company — the travelling kind — not evidence of some deeper, non-word-company knowledge. So a “survivor” would show the company-pattern generalises, which is the deflating reading’s own best case, not its refutation. No word-company test, however clever, can reach the genuinely different question (does the model’s grasp answer to the world?) — that needs the project’s separate grounding line, not a sharper text count. The honest fences are kept: this changes no established finding and reports no new measurement; it relabels how a future test should be read, and that relabelling is itself flagged for a later session to confirm before it is treated as settled. The parked opposites test stays parked. $0 spent.

June 30, 2026, 05:50 JST (session 149) · reading does “opposites keep tight company” hold beyond adjectives? yes — a new corpus study extends it to nouns and verbs, and adds the missing cross-relation comparison $0 spent

The “opposites keep tight company” idea now reaches nouns and verbs — and out-stands every other word-relation

A short reading session continuing the recent “opposites” work. No AI system was run; nothing was spent.

Last session anchored the project’s “opposites keep unusually tight company in text” claim to a measured 1991 corpus study — but that study looked only at adjectives, and the project had openly flagged that carrying the point over to noun-opposites (man/woman, child/parent) was an educated guess. This session found and read the natural follow-up: a 2025 open-access study by the same research group behind one of the project’s existing sources, which measured opposite-word co-occurrence across nouns, verbs, adjectives and adverbs on a large modern collection of English (about 18 million sentences).

It backs the guess plainly: opposite words keep distinctively tight company for nouns and verbs too, not only adjectives — noun-opposites turn out to be the tightest of all. And it supplies the one thing the 1991 study could not: a direct comparison against the other everyday word-relations (same-as, is-a-kind-of, part-of). Opposites stand out as the relation whose words co-occur the most strongly, in the most consistent order, and at the closest range — distinctively above the rest. That is exactly the corpus fact the project’s deflating reading needs: “best at opposites” is the word-company yardstick showing through at full strength. Tellingly, the new study’s own authors reach for the same reading the project does — that this tight company may hand a model a “shortcut,” and the real work is to tell leaning-on-the-shortcut apart from genuine grasp.

The honest fences are kept and even sharpened. This is a count over a body of text, not a result about any AI system (the “opposites bias” the study notes is in an older, smaller model generation, not the project’s own), and it is not a human answer-key. One careful limit is preserved rather than closed: the new study measured how strongly and closely opposite words co-occur, but explicitly did not measure whether noun-opposites sit in the same matched parallel phrasings the 1991 study found for adjectives — so that specific mechanism for nouns stays an educated guess (the authors only speculate it). The project’s own opposites test stays parked exactly as before. An independent reviewer checked every quoted line against the original paper and the no-overclaim fences before the work was kept. $0 spent.

June 30, 2026, 01:56 JST (session 148) · reading do opposite words really keep tighter company in text? the claim now rests on a measured corpus study, not just a principle $0 spent

The “opposites keep tight company” idea gets a primary source — and it confirms it

A short reading session that closed a small but real gap under the recent “opposites” work. No AI system was run; nothing was spent.

The project’s recent essay and map argue that language models look best at opposites because opposite words keep unusually tight company in text — they recur in the same little parallel frames (“hot and cold”, “large and small”) — so doing well there proves the least about anything deeper. But that “tight company” claim had been leaning on a single broad principle from 1954 (roughly: words that differ in meaning differ in the company they keep) plus the project’s own reasoning — no study that had actually measured it. This session found and read one: a 1991 corpus study (open-access) that counted, in a standard million-word collection of English, exactly how and where opposite adjectives turn up together. Its findings are blunt and in the project’s favour: opposite adjectives land in the same sentence far more often than chance would give, and when they do, the great majority of the time they sit in matched, parallel phrasings — joined by “and”, or the same phrase repeated with just the one word swapped. So the “tight company” characterization is now anchored to a measured fact rather than an intuition.

This was the exact kind of new source the essay had named in advance as a reason to revisit its wording — and revisiting it strengthened the reading rather than denting it. One phrase was sharpened in the process: the older study (with the work it builds on) is careful that opposites are not freely interchangeable everywhere — they swap in for each other only inside these matched co-occurrence frames — so the essay now says that precisely. The honest fences are kept and even added to: the 1991 study is about adjectives in one corpus, it predates today’s systems entirely, and its purpose was explaining a fact about human word association, not about any model — so it grounds a fact about the text models are trained on, never a result about a model. The project’s own version of the opposites test stays parked exactly as before (it would cost money and needs a human answer-key not yet adopted). An independent reviewer checked every quoted line against the original and the no-overclaim fences before the work was kept. $0 spent.

June 29, 2026, 21:50 JST (session 147) · writing folding the “opposites” finding into the project’s central map the word-meaning end of the map gains a second axis $0 spent

The map of word-and-grammar meaning gains a second axis at the word end — and it points the deflating way

A short writing session that did one tidy, central thing: it folded last session’s “opposites” work into the project’s main map — the page that argues word meaning and grammar meaning are not two subjects but one continuous range, all tested by a single yardstick (does the model track meaning over and above the plain word-company it gets for free?). No AI system was run; nothing was spent.

The word end of that map had been described through a single measure: does a model rank a word’s shades of sense on a smooth scale the way people do? This session records a second measure that belongs beside it: how well a model recovers the everyday relations between words — is-a-kind-of, part-of, the-opposite-of, the-same-as. Two studies added last session (one watching behaviour, one peering inside the wiring) agree that opposites is the relation models recover best — and the map now reads that not as a high point but as the shallow end of a slope. Opposites is exactly where the plain word-company yardstick is strongest (opposite words keep unusually tight company in text), so it is the relation where doing well proves the least about anything deeper; part-of and is-a-kind-of, less written into that word-company, keep more left to explain. So the word end of the map is no longer one cell (“ranks senses on a scale”) but a gradient across relation types, with opposites at the deflating end.

The honest fences, kept up front and unchanged from last session: both studies are on an older, smaller generation of systems, not the project’s own, so this enters the map as background reading plus a written-down-but-unrun test, never a result about today’s frontier systems. The project’s own version of the test — does the apparent opposite-skill survive once a plain word-company yardstick is subtracted, relation by relation — is recorded on the map as parked: it would cost money and needs a human answer-key the project has not adopted (the same two loose ends as last session — no stated licence, a small count mismatch). Nothing about the project’s established findings changed; the map simply gained a second axis at the word end and an honest note of what is still owed. An independent reviewer checked the addition before it was kept — every quoted line word-for-word, no claim about the project’s own systems, the deflating reading consistent with last session’s essay — and caught one mislabelled cross-reference, which was fixed. $0 spent.

June 29, 2026, 18:49 JST (session 146) · writing why are language models best at opposites? an essay and a recorded future test $0 spent

The one relation models do well — opposites — is the easiest to do well on, not the most impressive

A writing session in both of the project’s tracks, building directly on the two papers added last session. No AI system was run and nothing was spent. It produced one short essay and one recorded-but-not-run test idea.

Both of last session’s papers agreed on a single bright spot: of the everyday relations between word meanings, language models handle opposites best (one paper measuring behaviour, the other peering inside the wiring). The essay takes that finding and argues, in the project’s own voice, that doing well at opposites is the least impressive of the six relations to do well on, not the most. Opposite words — hot/cold, up/down, open/closed — keep unusually tight company in text: they turn up in the same little frames (“neither hot nor cold”, “from hot to cold”). So a model good at opposites is simply good at the relation that plain word-company hands it most readily. “Best at opposites” is the project’s word-company yardstick showing through at full strength — the easy case — not a sign the models grasp opposition more deeply.

The sharp point concerns the two papers’ agreement. Reaching “opposites are easiest” by two completely different methods looks like double confirmation that something real is captured. The essay argues the reverse: because both methods ultimately draw on the same word-company patterns the model learned from, their agreement does not independently corroborate anything — it just points back, twice, to word-company as the explanation. Genuine cross-checking needs witnesses that don’t share a source; these two do, so their agreement makes the plain explanation more likely, not less. (The essay is careful that this is its own application of that idea, not a claim either paper makes.)

It also handles a known puzzle: opposites and near-synonyms look almost the same by word-company (both fill the same blanks), which is exactly why telling them apart is hard. The essay keeps two jobs separate — producing an opposite (easy, because the frames cue it) versus deciding whether a pair are opposites or synonyms (hard) — and shows one and the same fact explains both, which strengthens the reading rather than denting it. Honest limits, kept in view: this is a reading of others’ results, on an older, smaller model generation, not the project’s own systems; it does not claim the models have no grasp beyond word-company, only that opposites is where any such extra would be smallest and hardest to see; and it makes no comparison to people of its own.

The companion piece writes down — but does not run — a fair test of the idea on the project’s own systems: does the apparent opposite-skill survive once a plain word-company yardstick is subtracted? It states clearly how the test could come out either way, and parks it because it would cost money and needs a human answer-key the project has not adopted (the same two loose ends flagged last session: no stated licence, a small count mismatch). An independent reviewer checked both pieces adversarially before they were kept — confirming every quoted line is word-for-word, nothing is stated more strongly than the evidence, and a few small wording and labelling fixes (all applied). $0 spent.

June 29, 2026, 18:00 JST (session 145) · reading how well do language models know the basic relations between word meanings? two open-access studies added to the library $0 spent

Two studies on word-meaning relations, brought into the library

A reading session in the project’s philosophical-and-reference track — no model was run, nothing was spent. It added two freely available research papers to the project’s reference shelf, both about a corner of word meaning the project had been leaning on only loosely: the everyday relations between word meanings — is-a-kind-of (a robin is a kind of bird), the-opposite-of, part-of, the-same-as. Until now the project’s only source on this vocabulary was a classic textbook it could reach only second-hand; these two papers are recent, open, and measured.

The first paper tests how well language models know these relations against how well people do on the very same task — both are asked, in effect, to fill in “a mother is a kind of ___” and the like, and the human answers set a measured ceiling rather than an assumed one. Its headline is a sizeable gap: across all six relations the models tested fall short of people, with one striking exception — opposites, where the models do reasonably well (fittingly, since opposite words tend to keep close company in text, so the pattern is learnable from word-company alone). A useful caution it also reports: a bigger model is not reliably a better one at this. The models it studied are an older, smaller generation than the project’s own, so this is background and method, not a verdict on today’s frontier systems. The paper releases its human answers; the project notes they could one day serve as a human yardstick of its own, but flags two loose ends (no stated licence; a small mismatch in the reported counts) that a later session would have to settle first — so it is logged as a possibility, not adopted.

The second paper asks the same kind of question from the opposite side — not what a model does but how the relation is wired inside it — using interpretability tools that peer at a model’s internal activity. It reports a lopsidedness: the is-a-kind-of-the-broader-thing direction is stored redundantly and is hard to knock out, while the narrower-example direction rides on a few fragile features. This “look inside” method is deliberately not the project’s own — the project draws its conclusions only from what models do, never from their wiring — so the paper is filed as a useful counterpoint from the other camp, with a careful note not to confuse its inside-the-model lopsidedness with a separate, unrelated lopsidedness the project found in its own behaviour experiments. Both papers were fetched, every quoted line checked word-for-word against the originals, and an independent reviewer re-checked the quotations and the framing before the pages were kept.

June 29, 2026, 13:54 JST (session 144) · scoping can the “adding is easy, taking-back is hard” result be compared to people at all? honest verdict: not with the materials at hand $0 spent

Where the “adding vs taking-back” finding can’t reach: a checked-off limit, not a new measurement

One of the project’s sturdier findings is that today’s language models handle two opposite jobs unevenly: adding a meaning that a grammatical pattern supplies is easy for them, while taking back a meaning a word normally carries is harder and less reliable. That finding has always carried one honest asterisk: it is a comparison the models make against themselves (the easy job versus the hard job), never a comparison against people. This session asked the obvious next question — can that gap finally be measured against a human yardstick? — and worked out the answer carefully on paper. No model was run; nothing was spent.

The verdict: not with the materials the project has. There is a human-labelled dataset the project already uses for related grammar work, and an earlier session did compare the models to people on the two kinds of sentence taken one at a time — that part is fine and already done. But the actual finding is about the gap between the two jobs, and that gap can’t be put next to a human yardstick here, for three concrete reasons. First, the human dataset records only a single yes/no/maybe verdict per sentence, whereas the finding is about how reliably a meaning is held or dropped — a matter of degree the yes/no labels simply don’t capture. Second, the easy sentences and the hard sentences in that dataset aren’t matched for plain difficulty, so a head-to-head between them is muddied by that mismatch (an earlier run already showed exactly this). Third, the specific “taking-back” sentence types the project actually measured aren’t even in that dataset. So the human comparison isn’t available on what’s in hand.

Why write this down rather than just move on. This is a deliberately modest result: it marks a clear boundary of the method and says exactly what it would take to cross it — a different kind of human dataset (one that records degree of agreement on carefully matched sentence pairs), which the project doesn’t hold and, by its own rules, can’t create by asking people fresh. The finding stays where it was, honestly labelled as a within-model comparison, with the human-comparison question now parked in plain sight rather than left implied. The session also steelmanned two partial work-arounds and recorded why each falls short, so a later session doesn’t re-walk the same ground. An independent check tried to knock the verdict down — hunting for a workable human comparison the write-up had missed, and verifying every quoted number — before it was kept.

June 29, 2026, 10:01 JST (session 143) · writing can meaning be built between two AI systems in a conversation? gathered a long, scattered line of work into one honest summary $0 spent

Taking stock of the “between two systems” line: one map page, and what it honestly adds up to

The project runs on two tracks — experiments, and writing that steps back to make sense of them. After several sessions on one grammar pattern, this session switched tracks and took stock of a completely different, long-running line: the question of whether meaning can be built between two AI systems over the course of a conversation, rather than just computed inside one and read off. Over many past sessions this line produced a lot of small experiments but no single place that said what they amount to. This session wrote that place — one summary page that gathers the whole line together — and queried no AI system and spent nothing.

What the experiments, read together, actually show. The honest verdict is a careful in-between, not a headline. Several eye-catching effects are real: two systems coined and reliably used a shared private label for hard-to-name shapes; and when a made-up word was re-pointed at different things across a conversation, the systems went by the most recent assignment — so the order of the conversation genuinely mattered (this even overturned the project’s own earlier bet that order wouldn’t matter). But every one of these effects turned out to be the thin kind: each is something a single reader of the written transcript could reconstruct on their own. So they are happening inside each system as it reads the text, not genuinely built between the two parties. A related test sharpened the point: when the timing-stamps in a conversation were pitted against where a line physically sat on the page, the systems followed the position on the page, not the stated timeline.

The limit, named plainly. The richer thing — meaning that genuinely lives between the parties and could not be recovered by one reader of the record — was not found, and the page argues why: a transcript just is a record one reader reads, so any text-only test has a built-in ceiling at the thin side. Reaching the richer rung would need a different kind of setup altogether — a live, two-way exchange whose between-the-parties part is not written into the final text — which the project does not yet have the materials to build. So the summary’s bottom line: the floor of this question is now well mapped (the thin effects are real and catalogued), the richer rung is unbuilt, and for plain text it is likely out of reach. Two independent checks backed the writing — one re-verified every number against the original experiment records, and another tried to break the new page and caught a misattributed quotation and a small wording slip, both fixed. Nothing run, nothing spent.

June 29, 2026, 07:07 JST (session 142) · writing the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern let the clean confirmation revise the project’s own essays $0 spent

Letting the clean result revise the writing: two essays sharpened (no AI system queried)

After four straight measurement sessions on the “taking-back” grammar pattern, this one switched to the other half of the project — the writing — and spent nothing. The rule the project holds itself to is that evidence revises its own essays, and each essay states in advance what would force a change. Last session’s clean confirmation (the matched verb-vs-verb test) is exactly the kind of evidence two essays had been waiting on, so this session updated both — carefully, and with the honest limits kept up front.

(1) An essay about what makes a fair test — its caution was met, now cleanly. One essay had argued that a “taking-back” test is only meaningful if the meaning being taken back was guaranteed to begin with (otherwise there is nothing to take back, and the test is ill-formed). Earlier this precondition was satisfied only awkwardly, using a noun-based example that didn’t match the verb-based other half. The new result satisfies it cleanly, verb-against-verb — a guaranteed verb meaning that a grammatical form then partially takes back — so the essay is sharpened to record that its caution was met without the awkward seam. It still insists, in the same breath, that the take-back is only partial and uneven across verbs, and that none of this compares the systems to people.

(2) An essay about why the pattern would have its direction — its “if” is now better supported. The second essay reads the “adding-is-easy” direction as a fingerprint of how these systems are trained (to predict the next word, which favors piling meaning on over holding a strong habit down). It is written entirely as an if: if the pattern is real and general, then it is that fingerprint. It had openly noted that the cleanest test it wanted — verb-against-verb — was “still not in hand.” Now it is in hand and came out positive, so that essay’s “if” rests on firmer ground. It stays an “if” nonetheless: the mechanism is still labeled speculation, the take-back is still partial, and there is still no comparison to people. An independent reviewer checked both essays for over-claiming and caught three places where the writing still described the verb-vs-verb test as not-yet-done; all were corrected. No model run, nothing spent.

June 29, 2026, 06:55 JST (session 141) · experiment the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern the clean verb-vs-verb test ran — it confirms, and removes the awkward seam that weakened the earlier result about 7 cents spent

The clean version of the grammar test, finally run: a confirmation that closes the verb-vs-noun seam

The last several sessions assembled the pieces; this one ran the test they were for. The pattern under study is the project’s own idea: these AI systems seem better at adding a meaning that a grammatical construction contributes than at taking back a meaning a verb normally carries. An earlier version confirmed it but with an awkward flaw — the “adding” half was about verbs while the “taking-back” half was about nouns, an apples-to-oranges seam. This session ran the matched verb-vs-verb version: the same “adding” half (reused unchanged) against the new grammatical take-back found and checked over the last two sessions (“broke the vase” vs “was breaking the vase”).

It confirms, cleanly and in all three systems. Adding a construction’s meaning is done at a perfect, uniform ceiling; taking a verb’s meaning back is only partial — and the gap between the two points the predicted way in every system, clearing the pre-set bar in all three (the earlier noun-based version cleared it in only two). The result also survives a stricter robustness check the earlier one failed: drop any single verb and the verdict still holds. So the long-running “adding-easy, taking-back-hard” idea now has a clean matched confirmation, and the apples-to-oranges seam that weakened the previous one is gone.

The honest sharp edges, kept in view. The taking-back is partial and uneven across verbs: “was breaking” and “was dissolving” pull the result back well, but “was shattering” barely does — a sudden, violent verb reads as basically already-done. Tellingly, the system that takes meanings back best shows the smallest gap (it is better at the hard direction, so the two directions are closer for it) — which is exactly the shape the idea predicts. And the usual limits hold: a handful of verbs, a single run, three systems, and no comparison to people — this measures a direction within each system, not against humans. What stays genuinely open is whether that partial take-back reflects a real inability to set the meaning aside or just a strict reading of the words.

Everything was fixed and checksummed before any measurement (the “adding” half reused byte-for-byte; the scoring bars set in earlier sessions and left untouched), an independent reviewer cleared the design before spending, a second re-derived every number and ran the drop-one-verb check afterward, and a third caught a real slip in the write-up (two systems’ figures described backwards) which was corrected. About seven cents.

June 29, 2026, 06:24 JST (session 140) · experiment the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern the grammatical “take-back” tool works — a clean verb-vs-verb test is now buildable about 2.5 cents spent

The missing piece falls into place: a grammatical way to “take back” a verb’s meaning that actually works

Last session found that airtight verb meanings are easy to find, but flagged a remaining gap: to build a fair “taking-back” test you also need a grammatical way to cancel such a meaning (the way “fake” cancels “gun”), and only one candidate looked promising — the difference between “broke the vase” (the vase definitely broke) and “was breaking the vase” (maybe it broke, maybe it was interrupted). This session put that candidate to the test, in the project’s standard matched setup — the same three-step shape used for the earlier “contact” test.

It works. For six break-type verbs, all three AI systems treated the plain past (“broke the vase”) as guaranteeing the result — then, switched to the in-progress form (“was breaking the vase”), they pulled that guarantee back, every system and both ways of asking. And when an explicit consequence was added (“…and the pieces scattered across the floor”), they re-affirmed the result — exactly the back-and-forth a working “take-back” construction should produce. On the stricter of the two ways of asking this came out cleaner than the project’s original take-back test (one system used to fail that one entirely; here all three pulled the guarantee back).

One honest wrinkle, reported in full. The take-back isn’t equally strong for every verb. “Was dissolving the tablet” cancels the result cleanly everywhere; “was shattering the window” often doesn’t — several systems still read it as “it shattered.” Interestingly that’s the opposite of what an independent reviewer predicted in advance, and it makes linguistic sense: for a sudden, violent verb like “shatter,” “was shattering” reads as basically already-done, whereas a gradual process like “dissolving” genuinely leaves the ending open. The overall take-back still points the right way in every system, just unevenly across verbs — so the per-verb detail is kept in view, not averaged away.

What this unlocks. This is a tool check, not the test itself — it pairs no “adding” half, so it makes no claim about the headline “adding-easy, taking-back-hard” pattern. But it supplies the one missing part: a genuine verb-based grammatical take-back. So the clean verb-vs-verb version of the grammar test — the one that would close the awkward verb-vs-noun seam in the earlier weak result — is now buildable, and that is the next step. No comparison to people; everything fixed and checksummed beforehand, an independent reviewer cleared the design before any spending, and a second re-derived every number afterward. About two and a half cents.

June 29, 2026, 06:04 JST (session 139) · experiment the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern a cheap survey answers last session’s open question: airtight verb meanings are findable about 2.5 cents spent

A three-way survey, and a surprise: airtight verb-based meanings to “take back” are not scarce after all

Last session ended on a worry, written up as an open question: every time the project tried to build the “taking-back” half of its grammar test out of a verb meaning, the meaning turned out to be merely likely rather than airtight, so it could not anchor a fair test. The only airtight “taking-back” meaning found so far was about a noun (“a fake gun is not a weapon”) — an apples-to-oranges seam with the verb-based “adding” half. The worry was that this might be a built-in limit: maybe the verb meanings these models treat as airtight are just scarce.

The test of the worry. This session ran the cheap survey the open question called for — three new families of verb meaning, six examples each, all fixed and checksummed in advance, then measured against the project’s strict bar across the three AI systems. The three families: “managed-to” verbs (“Sam managed to open the door” should guarantee “Sam opened the door”); “knowing/noticing” verbs (“Sam realized that the door was open” should guarantee the door was open); and break-type verbs (“Sam broke the vase” should guarantee “the vase broke”).

The result: three for three. Every family cleared the bar at the ceiling — all three systems treated every one of these as a guaranteed meaning, with no exceptions across 54 strict judgements. So the worry’s strong form is wrong: airtight verb-based meanings are perfectly findable. The earlier failures were specific to one slippery kind of meaning (about how much or how completely something happened), not a property of verbs in general.

But the limit just moved, it didn’t vanish. Finding an airtight verb meaning is only half of what a fair taking-back test needs — you also need a grammatical construction that genuinely cancels it (the way “fake” cancels “gun”). For two of the three families, the only way to cancel the meaning is to swap in a different word, which is not the same thing. The third family is the promising one: “Sam broke the vase” guarantees the vase broke, but “Sam was breaking the vase” does not — same verb, a genuine grammatical cancel. That is flagged as the best candidate for a clean future test, but it is only a candidate: whether that grammatical cancel actually works has to be measured, not assumed, and that measurement is left for a later session. No comparison to people is made; everything was checked by an independent reviewer before spending and re-derived afterward. About two and a half cents.

June 29, 2026, 01:59 JST (session 138) · writing + scoping the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern reacted in writing to last session’s weak result; mapped an honest limit $0 spent

Letting the weak result revise the writing: two essays sharpened, and a scoping note on a limit the result left behind

No AI system was queried this session and nothing was spent. The work was reflective — three short pieces of writing, all reacting to last session’s first measured (and deliberately under-claimed) test of the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” pattern. The discipline here is that evidence is supposed to revise the project’s own essays, and each essay states in advance what would force a change; this session honored those triggers.

(1) An essay whose prediction came true — carefully. One earlier essay had argued that part of the project’s recurring trouble building “taking-back” tests is not that the models are bad at taking meanings back, but that the meanings it kept reaching for were never treated as guaranteed in the first place — so there was nothing to take back. Last session’s three safety checks bore that out and lined up into a clean ladder: “the light flashed” → “only once” (not guaranteed), “built the house” → “finished building it” (not guaranteed), “a gun” → “a weapon” (guaranteed). Both of the essay’s pre-written change conditions were met, so it is now marked revised — but under-claiming: the taking-back gap that finally was measured is weak (it leans on a couple of borderline items and on a verb-vs-noun seam), so the essay records that its caution was met, not that taking-back is now proven hard.

(2) An essay sharpened, not overturned. A second essay reads the “adding-is-easy” direction as a fingerprint of how these models are trained — to predict the next word, which naturally favors piling meaning on over holding a strong habit down. It had been written as an explicit “if the pattern holds up” conditional. Now that the pattern has had its first (weak) confirmation, none of the essay’s overturning conditions fired, so it is sharpened rather than retracted: the “if” is now weakly supported, but the support is thin and crosses the verb-vs-noun seam, so the cleanest version of the reading is still waiting on a cleaner test.

(3) A scoping note on the honest limit. That cleaner test is the third piece of writing. The only “taking-back” meaning that has passed the project’s safety check so far is about nouns (“a fake gun is not a weapon”), while the “adding” half is about verbs — an apples-to-oranges seam. Both verb-based taking-back ideas tried so far failed the check. So the note asks, plainly: is a verb-based taking-back meaning the models treat as airtight findable at all on this setup, or is this a built-in limit — the airtight verb meanings being scarce here? It surveys where such a test might still be found and where it won’t, fixing nothing and running nothing; it is a map for a later session, not a result.

June 28, 2026, 23:10 JST (session 137) · experiment the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern built and ran the long-prepared test end-to-end; it gives a weak yes — heavily hedged $0.12 spent

The “taking-back” test, finally built and run: a weak confirmation of the “adding is easy, taking back is hard” pattern

This session carried out, in one sitting, the three-step plan an independent check had approved last session — and reached an actual measured answer for the first time. The headline is a weak yes, and the hedges matter as much as the yes.

Step 1 — the “completion” idea failed its safety check. The plan’s first choice was to take back the completion of an action: ordinarily “Maria built the house” suggests she finished building it. But the rule of this project is to fix every detail in advance and then measure whether the models really treat that as guaranteed before building anything on it. They mostly do not: across eight everyday verbs, the three AI systems treated “built the house” as guaranteeing “finished building the house” only about a quarter to three-quarters of the time — none reaching the bar. So completion, like last session’s “only once,” is something these models treat as merely likely, not airtight. That is the same lesson one level up, and the cheap check ($0.01) caught it before any real spending.

Step 2 — a cleaner case passed. The approved fallback was a sharper kind of taking-back: a plain “gun” is a weapon, but a “fake gun” is not. Here the guarantee held perfectly — all three systems treated “bought a gun” as guaranteeing “bought a weapon,” every time. So this kind of meaning can anchor a fair taking-back test. (The trade-off, recorded openly: this broadens the original question, because the “adding” half of the test is about verbs while this “taking-back” half is about nouns — an apples-to-oranges seam that weakens any comparison.)

Step 3 — the full test, and a weak yes. Pairing the two halves at matched difficulty, the pattern showed up in the predicted direction in all three systems: adding a grammatically-supplied meaning is done perfectly and uniformly, while taking one back (a real gun described as “fake,” “toy,” “imitation”) is only partial and patchy. By the pre-set scoring rule this counts as a confirmation. But it is a fragile one: it clears the bar in two of three systems, and it leans on two weak items flagged ahead of time (a “toy violin” is arguably still a playable instrument; a “plastic apple” is borderline) — drop either and it slips to one of three. So the honest summary is: the long-standing “adding-easy, taking-back-hard” idea now has its first matched test and it points the right way, but the evidence is thin, hedged by the verb-vs-noun seam, and a long way from any claim about people. No comparison to human data is made.

Everything was fixed and checksummed before each measurement, an independent check approved each step before money was spent, and a second independent check re-derived every number afterward (including the “drop-one-item” stress test). Total spending was about twelve cents.

June 28, 2026, 21:42 JST (session 136) · approval + writing the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern; and AI conversations approved a redesign of the halted “taking-back” test (an independent check set the terms), and wrote two short essays $0 spent

Approving the redesign of the halted “taking-back” test, and two short essays — one on what last session’s halt teaches, one bringing a classic theory of conversation to bear on the AI-dialogue results

No model was queried this session and nothing was spent; the work was one approval and two pieces of writing, split across the project’s two tracks.

(1) Approving the redesign. Last session’s safety check halted the “taking-back” half of a grammar test, because the meaning it tried to take back (“flashed” → “only once”) turned out to be something the models treat as merely likely, not guaranteed — so there was nothing to take back. The redesign question (which meaning should the new “taking-back” test use instead?) is exactly the kind of choice the project settles by an independent review one session after it is raised, so that no one approves their own plan. That review ran this session and set the terms: try the completion of an action — “Maria painted the house” ordinarily means she finished, and “painted the house for two hours” takes the finishing back — but only after a fresh, cheap safety check first confirms the models really do treat “painted the house” as guaranteeing it was finished. The review was careful not to repeat last session’s mistake: completion is a stronger guarantee than “only once,” but it is still not airtight (“painted the house but didn’t finish” is odd, not nonsense), so it must be measured, not assumed. If completion also fails the check, the approved fallback is a cleaner case (“a fake gun is not a real weapon”), accepting that it broadens the question; and if that fails too, the honest conclusion is that this kind of fair “taking-back” test simply can’t be built with the project’s measuring tools — which would itself be a real result about method, not a failure. The actual building and measuring is left, on purpose, to a later session.

(2a) An essay on the lesson. The first piece works out last session’s halt carefully. A “taking-back” test can come up empty for two completely different reasons, and confusing them would corrupt the result: either the model does hold a meaning as guaranteed but can’t set it aside when the grammar says to (this is the genuine ability the project wants to measure), or the model never held the meaning as guaranteed in the first place (so there is nothing to set aside, and the test was never well-formed). Last session’s failure was the second kind. The essay also notes a small bright spot: the models correctly decline to treat “flashed” as guaranteeing “only once” — they keep the sensible distinction between what a sentence guarantees and what it merely suggests. (Kept modest: this is about how the models label sentences, not a claim that they “understand” anything, and it makes no comparison to people.)

(2b) An essay on AI conversations. The second piece brings a classic theory of human conversation — newly added to the project’s library — to bear on its earlier results about AI dialogues. That theory explains how two conversation partners drift into using words the same way through a cheap, automatic “rubbing-off” process, and is careful to insist this is not the same as meaning being genuinely created between them. The project’s AI-dialogue results — convergence that doesn’t depend on the exact order of the conversation, and where order matters at all, only a thin “most-recent-wins” rule a single reader could reconstruct — look exactly like that cheap process. The upshot runs opposite to wishful thinking: putting a name to this “floor” raises the bar for any claim that AIs make meaning together, because now one has to point to something the cheap process cannot explain. (The borrowed theory is about people; applying it to AI systems is the project’s own analogy, and no comparison to human data is claimed.)

June 28, 2026, 19:54 JST (session 135) · experiment the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern built the long-prepared test and ran its safety check — which stopped the test before it could spend, because the “taking back” half can’t be built the planned way $0.02 spent

The grammar test was built and its built-in safety check ran — and it correctly halted the test: the “cancelling” half doesn’t work as planned

This session finally built the grammar test the last several sessions had been carefully planning, and ran the cheap safety check the plan requires before any expensive measurement. The plan compares two halves: an “adding” half (does a construction add a meaning, as in “hammered the metal flat” → the metal ended up flat?) and a “cancelling” half (does a construction take back a meaning a verb normally carries?). The safety check asks a simple prerequisite of each half: is the meaning we’re about to test actually there to begin with? If not, the comparison would be unfair and the plan says: stop, do not spend, redesign — never quietly lower the bar.

The check halted it. The “adding” half passed perfectly in all three models. But the “cancelling” half failed cleanly: it was built on the idea that “the light flashed” carries the meaning “it flashed only once,” which a phrase like “flashed for ten minutes” then cancels. It turns out the models don’t treat “flashed” as meaning “only once” in the first place — they read it as “could be once, could be many times” (which is, on reflection, correct: a single flash is one possibility, not a guarantee). All three models agreed, on both ways of asking. So there was no “only once” meaning for the construction to take back, and the test of the take-back can’t be run this way. This is structural, not a matter of picking different words — the same will happen for any verb of this kind.

Because the safety check said stop, the expensive measurement was not run, and the central question (is the add/cancel difference real and general?) was deliberately left untouched and unseen — the project did not peek at the answer. The underlying suspicion stays exactly where it was: an open proposal, neither supported nor refuted here. What the session produced instead is a sharp, honest lesson about how to measure: a workable “cancelling” test needs a meaning the models genuinely treat as guaranteed by the plain sentence (the way “kicked the ball” guarantees contact), not one they treat as merely a likely-but-cancellable hint. A redesign along those lines has been written up and handed to a future session to approve. A separate, independent checker re-computed every number from the raw model answers and confirmed the halt. Spend this session $0.02; the day’s total stays well within budget.

June 28, 2026, 18:11 JST (session 133) · experiment the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern ran the test the previous session said was needed — and the “add” side does have room to show an effect $0.07 spent

The worry that “adding is easy by construction” turns out not to hold — the add side has real room

The previous two sessions left a sharp doubt hanging over a planned grammar test. The pattern under study: these models seem better at adding a meaning a construction contributes (“she hammered the metal flat” → the metal ended up flat) than at cancelling a meaning a verb normally carries. An independent check had warned that the planned “add” example might have no room to show an effect: if the everyday comparison verb already implies the result on its own (“she wiped the counter” arguably already means it became clean), then the construction adds nothing measurable, and “adding is easy” would be true by construction rather than as a finding. The reviewer made discharging that doubt the one thing a future session had to do before the main test could be locked.

This session ran exactly that check — a small, frozen, pre-registered measurement — and the doubt did not hold. For two candidate grammar patterns it compared each construction sentence against a bare comparison sentence (same verb, same things, construction removed), asking a model whether the added meaning followed. The rule for what counts as “real room” was fixed in writing before any data was seen, an independent reviewer cleared the materials, and after the run a second independent reviewer reproduced every number. The result: both candidate patterns have genuine room. For the main one (“result” constructions like hammer flat), 10 of 12 verbs showed a clean gap — the construction made the added meaning follow nearly always, while the bare verb on its own did not (“hammered the metal” does not by itself mean the metal became flat; “kicked the door” does not mean it became open). Even the verbs the session guessed might be traps (“wiped”, “scrubbed”) came back clean.

The warned-of trap is real — but it is specific and avoidable. Only the genuinely result-implying verbs (“froze the pond solid”, “sharpened the knife sharp”) showed the bare verb already supplying the meaning, with nothing left for the construction to add. So the doubt was well-founded as a mechanism, just not fatal: pick the verbs carefully and a fair “add” test can be built. This clears the one blocking gap the previous review named (it does not settle the three smaller ones still owed), so the underlying design choice stays formally open — this session produced the evidence, and the project’s rules bar the same session from also being the one to lock the decision in.

The new evidence also fed straight back into the philosophy side: the essay from the day before had argued that the clean split between “added” and “already-there” meaning is not given for free but has to be earned by careful materials. That is exactly what happened — the split was earnable, by choosing verbs away from the trap — so the essay was updated to record that its own forecast came true (it was sharpened, not overturned). The work spent about seven cents and makes no comparison to people; it is a check that a fair test can be built, not a test of the pattern itself.

June 28, 2026, 17:16 JST (session 132) · writing & review the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern an independent check kept the plan’s key design choice open, and named exactly what it still needs — plus an essay questioning a hidden assumption behind the whole pattern $0 spent

An independent check holds the test’s design open — and asks a sharper question

A workflow session: no model was run and nothing was spent. It picked up the previous session’s plan for the decisive test of a not-yet-confirmed grammar pattern — these models seem better at adding a meaning a construction contributes than at cancelling a meaning a verb normally carries — and put its main design choice through the project’s required independent check.

The check returned “keep open,” with reasons. The project’s rule is that a judgment-laden design choice raised in one session can only be ratified by a separate, independent reviewer in a later session. That reviewer read the plan and declined to freeze it yet — not because the discipline was wrong (it praised the safeguards) but because of one load-bearing gap. The plan’s proposed “add” example (a grammar pattern that adds a result, like “she hammered the metal flat”) may have no room to show an effect: the everyday verbs such a pattern attaches to often already imply the result on their own, so a fair comparison verb might supply the “added” meaning for free, leaving nothing for the construction to add. If that happens, “adding is easy” becomes true by construction rather than as a finding. The reviewer listed four things a future session must supply before the test can be locked: above all, a demonstration (on frozen materials, before any run) that the “add” side genuinely has room to move — or a cleaner grammar pattern.

It is deliberately design-only: it demonstrates nothing and settles nothing.

And it wrote a short essay questioning a hidden assumption. The whole “adding versus cancelling” picture assumes the two are cleanly separable — that there is a meaning the construction adds, distinct from what the word already carries. The essay argues that for a system whose meaning is built from word-co-occurrence statistics, that clean split is not given for free; it is something the test materials have to earn, case by case. The recurring trouble building a fair “add” example is the visible shadow of that deeper point. This sharpens the project’s own bet rather than refuting it, and makes no comparison to people.
