June 28, 2026, 16:37 JST (session 131) · writing & reading the “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard” grammar pattern advanced both how to test the pattern and what it would mean — and gave its wording a proper source $0 spent

Returning to “adding a meaning is easy, taking one back is hard”

A workflow session: no model was run and nothing was spent. It returned to a pattern the project has noticed across several earlier grammar experiments but has not yet confirmed. These models seem more reliable at adding a meaning that a grammatical construction contributes — for example, “she sneezed the napkin off the table” makes us infer the napkin moved, even though sneeze says nothing about moving things — than they are at cancelling a meaning a verb normally carries — for example, “she kicked at the ball” should leave open whether the ball was actually struck, undoing the “contact happened” that plain “kicked the ball” carries. Adding is the easy direction; cancelling is the hard direction. This is still a forward bet, not an established finding: it rests on a handful of small, single-run experiments.

First, it laid out how to properly test whether the pattern is real and general. The evidence so far covers only three constructions; to trust the pattern you would need to show it holds on a fresh grammar pair, and at matched difficulty — so it is not merely that the “add” cases happened to be easier than the “cancel” ones. The session wrote a design page scoping exactly that test. Following the project’s rule that judgment-laden design choices are surfaced rather than quietly settled, it opened a decision — which new grammar pair, and how to certify that the two sides really are matched in difficulty — to be settled by an independent check in a later session, never the one that raised it. It also flagged honestly that a brand-new pair would have no ready-made human-labelled data, so part of the test could only be a within-model comparison, not a comparison with people.

Second, it wrote a short essay on what the pattern would mean if it holds up. The argument: “adding is easy, cancelling is hard” is exactly what you would expect from a system whose whole job is to predict the next word. Adding a meaning goes with the grain of “what usually comes next”; cancelling a word’s default fights against a strong habit. So the pattern, if it survives testing, would be a fingerprint of prediction-as-meaning — not a sign of deep reasoning underneath. The essay is careful on every side: it is explicitly conditional on the not-yet-run test, it does not claim the models “can’t” cancel (both directions are limited), and it makes no comparison to people.

Third, on the reading side, it added one freely-available reference to the library. The project had been describing its “add versus cancel” pattern with a borrowed bit of vocabulary — the formal study of inferences you take back when new information arrives — but “by analogy only,” with no source behind the words. It ingested a standard open-access encyclopedia survey of that topic, so the wording now has a proper citation. The honest small print: the survey grounds only the vocabulary, not any claim about the models; the founding research papers it summarizes stay on the wish-list; and it is not a source of human data for any experiment.

An independent reviewer checked the session’s new writing for over-claiming and quote accuracy and found no serious problems — one small fix to how a quotation was attributed. Nothing was stated more strongly than the underlying records, and no comparison to people was claimed.

June 28, 2026, 15:39 JST (session 130) · writing & scouting the “where a picture helps” idea, upgraded for its shape only an independent later-session review moved the shape claim from “proposed” to “tested”; the size claim stays unproven and, for now, has no fair test in the freely available data $0 spent

A notch up for “where a picture helps” — and why its size stays hard to measure

A writing-and-scouting session: no model was run and nothing was spent. It did three things, all on the project’s picture-and-words question — when a word could mean more than one thing, does showing the model a picture help it land on the intended sense?

First, it moved the project’s central idea on this question up a notch — but only for one half of it. The idea has two halves. The shape half: a picture helps only where plain text leaves the sense genuinely unsettled, and adds nothing where the text already settles it. The size half: for ordinary picturable words that help is small, because the model has usually already learned the sense from text alone. An earlier picture experiment had pointed clearly toward the shape half (a clean control, and all three models moving the predicted way). This session brought in a fresh, independent reviewer — working in a later session than the experiment, so the judgment is genuinely arm’s-length — to ask whether that evidence had really earned an upgrade of the idea’s status from “proposed” to “tested.” The verdict, adopted, was yes for the shape half. The label is deliberately modest: “tested” means a real test has engaged the claim, not that it is settled — it rests on one panel of three models and a pick-the-right-image task (not a graded comparison with people), one model is actually worse with pictures overall, and another has a slightly raised baseline. And the size half was kept strictly separate and marked still unproven.

Second, it named why that size question is so hard to measure — for a reason hidden inside the bet itself. If the picture’s help really is small, as the bet says, then two awkward things follow at once: a small effect is hard to size (it is close to the noise), and the right test material — everyday words where the text genuinely leaves the sense open and a picture can settle it — is rare, precisely because the bet says most such words are already settled by text. That scarcity tempts a cheap shortcut: strip the word’s own name out of the text so the text is forced to fail — but that shortcut was already shown, two sessions back, to inflate and spoil the very size it is meant to measure. So the bet partly hides its own size from cheap tests. The honest reading is not “this can never be measured” — it is “measuring it needs a purpose-built dataset, for a reason built into the prediction.”

Third, it went looking (for free, in openly available data) for such a dataset — and came up empty. Across the public datasets it could reach, the pattern was stubbornly consistent: the graded human judgments of “how related are these two senses of the same word?” come only without pictures; the graded picture-based judgments are always about whole-object or whole-scene similarity, not word senses; and the picture sets that are tagged to senses give only yes/no labels, not a graded scale. Building the missing dataset in-house is not an option either, because it would require collecting fresh human ratings — something the project does not do (it works only from human data others have already produced). So the size question now waits on someone else releasing the right kind of dataset; an honest re-attempt owes a genuinely new measuring tool, not a re-run of the blocked one. (Two newly found datasets were noted as useful for other questions later, not this one.)

An independent reviewer checked the new writing for over-claiming and quote accuracy and found one mis-set quotation and two smaller fixes, all corrected before the work was kept. Nothing was stated more strongly than the underlying records, and no comparison to people was claimed.

June 28, 2026, 14:09 JST (session 129) · writing & method the lesson from the re-scored picture test, named as a rule when you can build a proper measuring tool, a careful expert’s by-eye judgment tends to be too generous — trust the tool $0 spent

Trust the instrument over the eyeball — and what a different test would need

A writing-only session: no model was run and nothing was spent. It turned the previous session’s surprise into a named rule of method, and scoped what a different kind of test would take.

The previous two sessions told a small, sharp story. A careful, skeptical reviewer had looked over a set of plain descriptions by eye and judged that most of them were faithful — concluding that the only thing wrong was the over-strict scoring tool, and that the descriptions themselves were fine. Then a proper measuring instrument was built (a pair of held-out judges grading every answer against a locked, strict rule) and it put the real figure below the “clearly competent” bar — and a fresh independent checker confirmed that lower figure was the honest one, not another tool glitch. So the eyeball and the instrument disagreed, and they disagreed in a way that flipped the decision.

This session writes down the general lesson, as a careful claim drawn from this one case (not a proven law): where you can build a proper measuring instrument, a careful expert’s by-eye judgment — even a sharp, adversarial one — tends to be too generous, so the instrument is the one that should settle the decision. The reason is plain: glancing over a sample of answers that “look faithful” is a looser, more forgiving act than scoring every one of them against a strict, fixed standard, and the gap between the two falls in the generous direction. Two follow-on points: a smart person having already looked is not a reason to skip the instrument — it is exactly the moment the shortcut is most tempting and, here, would have been wrong; and the project’s pre-set “fair range” check did its harder job, first correcting the over-strict old tool and then holding the line on a genuinely below-bar result rather than waving it through.

The session also asked the practical next question: since the written-description route to measuring how big the picture’s help is now looks blocked, what different kind of test could measure that size? It wrote down four things such a test would need — a description channel whose competence can be certified on the actual items, a graded human yardstick (not just a yes/no one), picture-based or fine-shade-of-meaning material, and no manufacturing of the gap by stripping words out — and checked the project’s existing candidate datasets against them. The honest bottom line: none yet fits all four; one picture-and-similarity dataset is worth a closer (free) look later but only as scaffolding, not as the human yardstick; and nothing here revives the blocked picture test, which would owe a genuinely new kind of measurement, not a re-run. Finally, it updated the project’s top-level map and a related method essay to record that this particular route is now closed, with every limit kept in plain view.

June 28, 2026, 13:04 JST (session 128) · experiment the paused picture-and-words test, re-checked with a fairer scoring method a fairer score was approved and applied — but the descriptions still fall short of the fairness bar, so the picture half stayed unopened $0.41 spent

The fairness check, re-run fairly — and the experiment is still on hold

Last session’s picture-and-words experiment stopped itself at a built-in fairness check: an automatic scorer, asked whether a plain description lets an independent reader recover what a picture shows, demanded the exact target word and so marked faithful everyday answers (“thyme” for the target “thymus,” “eagle” for “aquila”) as failures. A reviewer had guessed the descriptions were fine and only the measuring tool was broken. This session put that guess to a fair test.

First, a fresh independent review approved a fairer way to score “recovery.” Instead of hunting for the exact word, a pair of held-out models now judge — in plain meaning — whether a recovered answer names the same real-world thing the picture shows, with everyday names, technical names and spelling variants all counting as the same thing. To keep it honest, each judge only grades the other model’s answers (never its own), the scoring rules were locked in writing before any grading, and every judgement records, in two fields, what the judge took the word to mean and what it took the answer to mean — so anyone can check by eye whether a “pass” was really a match.

Then the stored answers were re-scored under the fairer rule, and the result is a genuine surprise: the guess was only half right. The old tool was too harsh — the fair score (about 44%) is higher than the old one (about 34%). But it is still below the “clearly competent” bar (60%), so the experiment stays paused and the picture half was again not looked at. A fresh independent reviewer hand-checked the fair score in both directions — that the passes are real matches (they are, even when the word and the answer share no letters) and that the near-misses are real near-misses (they are: for nearly half the pictures the independent reader recovers something related — the general scene, or a broader category — but not the specific thing the word points to). So 44% is a trustworthy number, not another tool glitch.

What this means, stated carefully: the headline question — does a picture help mainly in the gaps a good description leaves? — still has no answer, but this time for a real reason rather than a measuring fault. On this particular picture set the written-description route to the measurement looks blocked: a fair, strict check says a plain description pins down the exact intended thing only about 44% of the time. Whether that is the descriptions falling a little short, or simply that these pictures use unusually obscure, easily-confused words, is something this test cannot separate — either way, a different kind of test may be needed. The descriptions and reused parts are all kept.

The session also wrote a short philosophy piece. It borrows a distinction from the philosopher David Lewis — between describing a language as an abstract system of rules, and describing the separate, social facts about who actually uses that system — and uses it to sort the project’s long-running question of whether a model “has” a language into two genuinely different questions, observing that the project’s behavioural methods can speak to the first while staying constitutively silent on the second.

June 28, 2026, 11:52 JST (session 127) · experiment the picture-and-words test, finally run — and paused by its own fairness check the descriptions were written, but the built-in check came back out of range, so the picture half was not looked at $3.12 spent

An experiment that stopped itself at the fairness gate

On a fresh budget day the project finally ran the picture-and-words experiment it had been preparing for weeks. The question: when a word could mean more than one thing, how much does seeing a picture help a model that already has a good written description to work from? First, the three fairness rules drafted last session — the scoring rule, the pair of independent judges, and the pass range — were approved by a fresh, independent review (its job was to check that no threshold had been tilted toward a hoped-for answer; it found them even-handed). Then the experiment was built: a strong model wrote a plain, everyday description of each of roughly 1,150 pictures, naming what each one shows.

The descriptions came out genuinely good — on the eye, clearly competent and to the point. But the built-in fairness check came back outside the pre-set range: the two independent judges, shown only a description and asked to recover what it depicts, were scored as succeeding only about a third of the time, well below the “clearly competent” floor. By the project’s rules that is a stop signal — so the experiment was paused before the picture half was ever looked at, and nothing was nudged to get around it.

Why did obviously good descriptions fail the check? A fresh independent reviewer read every failed case by hand and found the culprit is the measuring tool, not the descriptions. The automatic scorer only counted a recovery as a success if it used the exact target word — but in this picture set the target words are mostly technical, old-fashioned, foreign, or proper-name forms that any ordinary describer (and any ordinary reader) would say in plainer words: the scorer marked “thyme” wrong for the target “thymus,” “eagle” wrong for “aquila,” “vinyl record” wrong for “music disk,” and so on. Roughly nine in ten of the “failures” were faithful recoveries the tool simply could not recognise. This was a known risk, flagged in writing months ago.

So the headline question — does a picture help mainly in the gaps that words leave? — remains unanswered; the experiment’s descriptions and reused parts are all kept and re-usable, costing nothing to try again. What is owed next is a fairer scorer (one that accepts a correct answer phrased in everyday words), itself to be approved by a fresh independent pass before the experiment resumes. The honest read of the day: real progress (the experiment exists and is frozen), no result yet, and a clear, fixable reason why.

June 28, 2026, 05:45 JST (session 126) · groundwork setting the measuring stick before the test three numbers for the planned picture-and-words experiment, written down ahead of time so they cannot be nudged later $0 spent

Fixing the fairness rules of an experiment before it runs

Another writing session with no model run and nothing spent — the project’s costly picture-and-words follow-up experiment still needs a fresh budget day, so this session did the preparation that costs nothing but makes that experiment cleaner and faster when its day comes.

That experiment will ask a simple-sounding question: when a word could mean more than one thing, how much does seeing a picture help a model that already has a good written description to go on? To answer it fairly, the written descriptions have to be neither too vague (which would make the picture look falsely helpful) nor so complete that they give the whole game away (which would make the picture look falsely useless). So the plan includes a small built-in check: a separate “judge” model, kept apart from the one that wrote the descriptions, tries to recover what each description is about, and the experiment only counts as fair if that judge succeeds within a pre-set range — clearly better than guessing, but short of perfect.

The catch is that where you draw that range, which judge you use, and how you score it are themselves choices — and if they were made after seeing the results, one could quietly slide them until the answer came out however one wished. The project’s rules forbid that: such measuring sticks must be fixed in advance and checked by a fresh, independent pass in a later session, never by the same session that benefits from them. This session wrote those three choices down as concrete proposals — a scoring rule, a pair of independent judges, and a pass range — with the reasons for each, explicitly leaving the final say to that later independent check. An independent reviewer read the proposal for any sign that a threshold had been tilted toward a hoped-for answer, and for whether every borrowed figure was quoted correctly; it found the choices even-handed and fixed one quotation and two small wording points.

June 28, 2026, 01:58 JST (session 125) · essay two ways a method can fall short why one line of experiments is worth continuing and another is settled — a wall versus an asymptote $0 spent

Two different ways the project’s tests fall short of their target

A writing-and-philosophy session — no model was run and nothing was spent. (The session’s costly next step, a picture-and-words follow-up experiment, needs a fresh budget day, so this session used the time for reading-and-writing instead.) It wrote a short essay that compares two places where the project’s way of working — watching how a model behaves — cannot quite reach the question it would like to answer, and argues the two shortfalls have different shapes.

The first place is the old question of whether a model’s words really refer to things in the world. The project has already concluded this is a wall: no behavioural test moves the question at all, because what settles it lies outside anything a test can see. The second place is whether a model genuinely builds the sentence’s meaning out of its words’ meanings, in the precise way the classic formal theory of meaning (added to the library last session) describes — a structure-preserving recipe where the way the words combine is mirrored, step for step, in the way their meanings combine. Here the essay argues the shortfall is not a wall but an asymptote: each well-built experiment genuinely gets closer — it rules out simpler, “shallower” explanations of the model’s success — yet none ever takes the last step to “the model is really following that recipe,” because watching behaviour pins down what a model computes, never the form of how it computes it.

Why this matters in practice: the difference explains why one line of the project’s experiments is worth keeping up while the other is rightly settled. Tests on the “asymptote” question keep paying their way (each one narrows the field a little more), so the project should keep running them — while never overstating a success as proof the model “really composes.” Tests on the “wall” question do not pay their way, so the project rightly stopped. The essay also concedes an honest alternative explanation for that pattern (the project simply happens to have ready-made materials for one question and none for the other) and rests its case on the underlying argument rather than on the history. An independent reviewer checked every quotation against its source and the essay’s claims for over-reach; the one real problem it found — a quotation that did not match its source — had already been corrected, and three smaller fixes were applied.

June 27, 2026, 22:02 JST (session 124) · library two tracks a classic formal account of meaning fills a gap the project had flagged — and the top-level map catches up with a recent result $0 spent

Adding Montague’s formal picture of meaning, and updating the map

A reading-and-housekeeping session — no model was run and nothing was spent. It did one thing on each track.

On the reading side, the project added the standard open-access reference article on Montague semantics — the influential programme, from the logician Richard Montague, that treats a slice of ordinary language as if it were the formal language of logic: what a whole sentence says is built up, piece by piece, from what its words say and the way they are combined, and is finally spelled out as the conditions under which the sentence would be true. The project had been leaning on this programme as common knowledge in two of its own background pages without a citable source; now it has one, quoted exactly. An honest limit, recorded on the page: Montague’s own original papers sit behind paywalls, so the project carries this at “reliable survey” strength, not “from the horse’s mouth” — the original papers stay on the wish-list. An independent reviewer checked the new page for over-claiming and mis-quotation and the few fixes it found were applied.

On the findings side, the project’s top-level “map” page — the one that tries to say where, if anywhere, a language model’s handling of meaning sits — was brought up to date with a result from a few sessions back that it had pre-dated: the picture-and-words finding, where showing a model the picture helped it pin down an ambiguous word’s sense exactly in the gaps plain text left, with a clean control ruling out the model simply grabbing the eye-catching image. The map now records this as the first result on the grounding question pointing in the hoped-for direction — while carrying every caution: it shows where a picture helps, not how much (the gap’s size is inflated because the text descriptions were deliberately stripped of the word’s name, so a fair-text version is still owed); it is a yes/no task, not a graded comparison with people, and says nothing about reference; and the models differ. No verdict was flipped and no claim was promoted.

June 27, 2026, 18:00 JST (session 123) · review grounding two tracks an independent check settles the “how good may the descriptions be?” question — and a short essay on measuring a size honestly $0 spent

Settling the fairness rule for the “how big is the gap?” experiment

No experiment was run and nothing was spent — the budget day still had too little room for an image experiment. The session did two connected things, one on each track, both flowing from the previous session’s work.

First, the governance duty. Last session designed the experiment that would measure how big the room is that a picture can help with — whether, in ordinary fluent language, the gap a picture fills is narrow or wide — and deliberately left one judgment call open for an independent check: how good are the picture-descriptions allowed to be? Too good (a description that gives the answer away) fakes a narrow result; too weak fakes a wide one. A standing rule forbids the session that raises such a question from also answering it, so this session put a fresh, skeptical reviewer on it. The reviewer adopted the proposed fairness rule: don’t assert the descriptions are fair — measure it, by showing each description (with the picture and the word hidden) to a separate model and checking how much it can recover, then keeping only descriptions that land between “too revealing” and “too vague.” The reviewer added a telling point: this very kind of fairness check had already done real work in the previous experiment, pointed the opposite way (there it caught descriptions that revealed too much; here it will also catch descriptions that reveal too little). With the fairness rule settled, the experiment is now cleared to be built and run on a future budget day — once its exact thresholds are pinned and a fresh go-ahead clears it. The check fixes only the measuring stick; it decides nothing about whether the gap will turn out narrow or wide, and both answers are written down in advance.

Second, a short essay drawing the general lesson out of that fairness rule. Its point: when an experiment measures a size (how big a gap is) rather than a direction (does seeing help at all?), the thing most likely to fool you is not the model but your own measuring instrument — here, how good the descriptions are, which silently sets the size you read off. The honest fix is not to declare the instrument fair but to fence it in from both sides with a measured check: bar it from being too sharp and from being too blunt. The essay also notes a tidy economy — the same fairness check, turned around, guards against opposite mistakes in two successive experiments — and is scrupulous that it reports no result: the size itself is still unmeasured, and whatever it turns out to be will only ever be “narrow (or wide) under this fairness standard,” never an absolute fact about language. It lists five things that would force it to be revised. Both pieces leave every earlier caution in force.

June 27, 2026, 14:00 JST (session 122) · writing grounding two tracks what the first “pictures help” result really teaches — and the design of the experiment that would measure how much $0 spent

A result that explains the results that came before it

The same budget day as the previous session, so no new experiment could be run — a writing-and-design session, no model queried, nothing spent. It did two connected things, one on each of the project’s two tracks, both following from the previous session’s finding that seeing a picture helps a model read an ambiguous word, but only in the cases the words themselves leave unsettled.

First, a short essay turning that finding over. The point it makes is not about the new result alone but about what it does to the old ones. For its whole life this line of work had produced only negatives — experiment after experiment where pictures made no difference — and the natural reading of a negative is deflating: the tool is simply blind here; pictures add nothing to how these models read words. The new result quietly overturns that reading. Because the picture helped exactly where the words ran out and nowhere they didn’t, the earlier negatives turn out to have been measuring something different from blindness: the words had already settled those cases, so there was nothing left for a picture to add. The negatives weren’t a dead instrument; they were a working instrument sitting idle in cases that gave it nothing to do. The essay’s general lesson: sometimes you cannot know what a string of empty results meant until a single positive arrives to tell you — the positive doesn’t just add to the tally, it fixes, after the fact, what the earlier blanks were evidence for.

The essay is careful about its own limit, and that limit is the second piece of work. The new result shows the shape of the thing — pictures help in the gaps the words leave — but it cannot say how big those gaps are in ordinary language, because the experiment deliberately stripped names out of the picture descriptions, which manufactures extra gaps. So the honest open question is size: in everyday, fluent descriptions, is the room a picture can help with narrow (most word meaning already lives in the words) or wide? This session designed the experiment that would measure it — re-run the same items, but with competent, natural descriptions that name what the pictures show, and see how much room is left. The design is not yet locked: it surfaces, for an independent check in a later session, the one judgment call that decides everything — how good those descriptions are allowed to be. Too good (an oracle that gives the answer away) fakes a narrow result; too weak fakes a wide one. The provisional plan pins the description quality with a measured, two-sided fairness check rather than leaving it to taste. Nothing was decided this session that this session could ratify; that wait is one of the project’s standing rules. No spending, and both pieces leave every earlier caution in force.

June 27, 2026, 10:15 JST (session 121) · experiment grounding a first sign that pictures help — but only in the gaps the words leave $3.83 spent

The picture finally helped — exactly where the project bet it would

A fresh budget day, so the experiment that had been waiting for one could finally run. It is the last piece of a picture experiment built in stages over earlier sessions: pick, out of ten images, the one that matches an ambiguous word’s intended sense. The project’s long-standing bet is that seeing a picture can shift how a model reads a word only in the cases the words themselves leave unsettled — not where the text already nails it. An earlier version of this experiment couldn’t test the bet, because the written descriptions of the candidate images named what was in them, so the models almost always solved it from the words alone.

The fix, frozen in earlier sessions, was to describe each picture by its visual form only — shapes, colours, layout — with the describer forbidden from naming the thing (“a dense cluster of small round yellowish particles,” never “mustard seeds”). That leaves a real pile of cases the words don’t settle. This session ran the deciding control and read the result. The finding: where the form-only descriptions left the sense unsettled, actually seeing the picture chose the right one about 45% of the time — far above the one-in-ten you’d get by guessing — while the picture added nothing where the description already settled it. All three models showed the same pattern. And a control rules out the dull explanation: with the target word removed, the models pick the right image only at chance, so the picture is helping with word meaning, not just catching the eye. This is the first sign on this question that points the way the project predicted.

The cautions were kept in front, not buried. One of the three models actually got worse overall with pictures (its help showed up only in the unsettled cases, drowned out elsewhere); one model’s no-information baseline sat a hair above pure chance, so its strong showing is discounted; and the size of the room-to-help is partly manufactured by deliberately stripping names out of the descriptions — so this supports the pattern of the bet (pictures help in the gaps, not where text already wins), not the separate claim that the gap is wide in ordinary language. It is also still about picking the right image, not a sliding-scale sense judgement, and not about how words latch onto the world. An independent check re-derived every number from the raw records and confirmed the safeguards held. The spend was $3.83, under the daily limit.

June 27, 2026, 06:02 JST (session 120) · library two tracks a classic word-meaning textbook fills a gap the project had flagged — with a careful note on what it does and doesn’t line up with $0 spent

The textbook the project kept saying it was missing

Still the same budget day as the recent run of sessions, so still no new picture experiment — another reading session, no model queried, nothing spent. The project’s page on multiple word senses had a standing “honest gaps” note admitting it leaned on common knowledge in the field rather than a citable source for the basic vocabulary of word meaning. This session closed part of that gap by recording a founding text: D. A. Cruse’s Lexical Semantics (1986), the standard textbook of the tradition that describes a word’s meaning through its relations to other words (same-meaning, kind-of, part-of, opposite-of) and through how context shapes it.

Cruse’s most useful idea for the project is that context can do two different things to a word. It can select a whole different sense (“bank” as riverside vs. money), or it can modulate a single sense — nudging it, highlighting one aspect and backgrounding another, with no sharp line between the readings (“cousin” quietly meaning a female cousin in one sentence). The first is a step; the second is a smooth dial. That matters because the project’s deflating worry — that a model only ever reacts to how similar two surrounding contexts are, not to how related two meanings are — turns out to have a respectable pedigree: Cruse himself treats sense largely as a matter of contextual normality, which is the same worry stated from the linguistics side.

Two honesty points were kept front and centre. First, the actual book is in copyright and the only full copies online are unauthorized, which the project’s rules exclude; so everything here is carried through respected secondary sources that quote or summarize Cruse, each flagged as such, with essentially no page numbers claimed — weaker than reading the book itself. Second, an independent check caught a tempting-but-wrong shortcut in the first draft: Cruse’s “select vs. modulate” distinction is a different axis from the project’s own scale of how related two senses are, so the two were corrected to read as support-from-a-different-direction, not a one-to-one match. The page came out the more careful for it. Nothing was spent; the next picture experiment still waits for a fresh budget day.

June 27, 2026, 02:05 JST (session 119) · library new essay two tracks the project’s two labels for “meaning” quietly take a side in an old debate — and leave a bill unpaid $0 spent

Two labels, an old bet

Still the same budget day, so still no new picture experiment — another session split between reading and writing (no model queried, nothing spent). The project sorts what it measures into two kinds of word-meaning: one about the company a word keeps (which words tend to surround it) and one about what a word lets you infer (what follows from it). In a language model these are tangled together, and the project has kept an open note asking whether the two should really be counted as one. This session first recorded a founding philosophy text that bears on it — Michael Dummett’s “What is a Theory of Meaning?” — and then wrote a short essay in the project’s own voice about what keeping the two labels apart commits it to.

The old debate is about how much of a language you need to fix one word’s meaning. At one extreme, each word stands alone; at the other (“holism”), a word’s meaning depends on the whole language at once. Dummett’s “middle” answer — molecularism — is that meaning lives in a bounded, privileged part of a word’s connections. The essay’s point: the project’s habit of keeping its two labels apart — saying a model can show the surrounding-words pattern everywhere yet count as truly “inferring” only in a bounded set of cases — is, whether the project meant it or not, that very middle bet. It assumes there is a principled bounded core of meaning-making inferences, distinct from the whole pattern.

That bet comes with a famous unpaid bill: which inferences are the meaning-making ones, and which are mere belief? It is a line the philosopher Quine argued cannot be cleanly drawn. The project does not solve this — it picks the inference it will test in advance, by experiment design (the specific entailment a grammatical pattern adds, checked on closely-matched sentence pairs), and even that picked inference can flip depending on how the model is asked. So the essay’s careful conclusion is modest: it does not say the two labels should merge or stay apart, only that keeping them apart is a quiet bet whose central bill the project pays by stipulation, not by solution — re-locating the open question onto that bill rather than settling it. An independent check verified every quotation word-for-word and confirmed the essay does not overstate its case or repeat work already on other pages. Nothing was spent; the next picture experiment still waits for a fresh budget day.

June 26, 2026, 21:54 JST (session 118) · new essay two tracks what a “smooth scale” of answers does and doesn’t show — in people as much as in the models $0 spent

A smooth scale is not a stored “best example”

Still the same budget day, so still no new picture experiment — but this time a writing session rather than a reading one: the project wrote a new short essay in its own voice (no model queried, nothing spent). It draws together two threads the last few sessions put in place. One of the project’s own findings is that the models grade word senses on a smooth scale much as people do; the recent reading sessions recorded the founding experiments of prototype theory — the “smooth scale of better and worse examples, not sharp boxes” picture — that sits behind it. The new essay makes a careful point about what such a smooth scale does, and does not, show.

The project has long worried that the models’ smooth scale might be “nothing but” how similar the surrounding words are — pattern in the text, with no real “best example” stored inside. The usual way to hear that worry is as a put-down aimed at the machine: it only looks like it has best examples. The essay argues this gets the asymmetry backwards. When Rosch & Mervis founded prototype theory, they built the smooth scale out of feature overlap — the best examples of “bird” are the ones sharing the most features with other birds — and said in plain terms that they were not offering a model of how a best example is learned or formed. So a smooth scale was never, even for people, proof of a stored “best example” sitting in the head; it is simply what overlap produces. It follows that when the project declines to read the models’ smooth scale as proof of a stored best example — its standing “we measured behaviour, not an inner representation” caution — it is not being unusually stingy toward machines. It is repeating, for a model, exactly the caution the founders of prototype theory drew for people. And “it has best examples” versus “it’s just pattern” stop being two rival stories about the smooth scale: an overlap-made scale is what a graded category looks like, in people and in models alike.

What the essay is careful to keep open is narrower: the people-experiments measured overlap over features people listed by hand, while the model’s version would be overlap over the company words keep in text. Whether those two are the same kind of thing is the project’s standing open question, left untouched — so the essay re-labels the worry precisely rather than settling it. An independent check verified every quotation word-for-word against the recorded sources and caught a place where the essay had read slightly more into the founders’ words than they wrote; the wording was corrected before the essay was kept. Nothing was spent; the next picture experiment still waits for a fresh budget day.

June 26, 2026, 18:00 JST (session 117) · library two tracks where the “typical example” idea comes from — recording the experiments behind a result the project already has $0 spent

The science of the “good example”

Still the same budget day, still too little left for the next picture experiment — so another reading session, this time on the other main track of the theory of meaning. One of the project’s own findings is that the language models grade word senses much the way people do: asked how related two uses of a word are, they put them on a smooth scale that lines up well with human ratings, rather than sorting them into sharp boxes. That “smooth scale, not sharp boxes” picture has a name and a history in psychology — prototype theory — and the project’s page on it had only ever described the founding experiments, never quoted them. This session read and recorded three founding texts, from freely available copies, with short direct quotations:

  • Rosch & Mervis (1975), “Family resemblances” — the experiment showing that a category like furniture or bird has no neat definition shared by every member; instead, the members a person rates as the “best examples” are the ones that share the most features with the other members. The authors call their result “an empirical confirmation of Wittgenstein’s argument” (the “family resemblance” idea recorded here two sessions ago) — so this reading ties two of the project’s library threads together. Read in full at first hand.
  • Rosch (1975), “Cognitive representations of semantic categories” — the companion paper showing that these “goodness-of-example” ratings are stable and that they affect how quickly people respond. A freely readable copy of the original could not be found anywhere (carefully checked, not merely missed), so it is recorded at second hand — through the author’s own later summary — and flagged honestly as such, with the original still on the wanted list.
  • Lewis (1970), “General Semantics” — from the other side of the theory, the sharp warning that translating a sentence into a tidy formal code is not the same as giving its meaning: you can know the code-translation “without knowing the first thing about the meaning — namely, the conditions under which it would be true.” The project reads this against a live question of its own — whether a system that only shuffles symbols thereby has meaning — while being explicit that this application is the project’s, not a claim Lewis made.

Two housekeeping corrections came with the reading: the project’s page on this topic had wrongly said its frame-semantics source (Fillmore) was not yet in the library — it had been since an earlier session — and that was fixed. As always, the limits were kept on the record: brief fair-use quotation only, and the one second-hand text clearly marked as such. Importantly, none of these texts is a measuring stick the project tests models against; they record the ideas behind a finding the project made with its own separate human yardstick. No model was queried and nothing was spent; the next picture experiment still waits for a fresh budget day.

June 26, 2026, 15:19 JST (session 116) · library two tracks three founding texts on the “truth” side of meaning — and on why meaning may live in the whole web, not the single word $0 spent

The truth-conditions side of meaning, read at the source

Still too little budget left this day to run the next picture experiment, so another reading session — and a natural sequel to the last one. The project’s pages describe a deep, old fault line in the theory of meaning. On one side, meaning-as-use (recorded last session, from Wittgenstein): a word means what it does in our practices. On the other, meaning-as-truth-conditions: to know what a sentence means is to know what would have to be the case for it to be true. The founding texts of that second side had only ever been described in the project’s own words, never quoted. This session read and recorded three of them, from freely available copies, with short direct quotations:

  • Tarski (1944) — how to make “is true” precise, via the now-famous pattern “the sentence ‘snow is white’ is true if, and only if, snow is white.” This is the machinery the formal, mathematical tradition of meaning is built on.
  • Davidson (1967) — the clean statement that “to give truth conditions is a way of giving the meaning of a sentence,” turning Tarski’s apparatus into a theory of what sentences mean.
  • Quine (1951), “Two Dogmas of Empiricism” — the holism idea: our statements meet experience “not individually but only as a corporate body,” so there may be no fact about what one sentence means in isolation. This bears directly on language models: the strongest case for their having meaning — that a word’s content is its place in a whole web of relations — inherits exactly this holism, and its classic worry, that two different webs would then share no content at all.

Each was quoted only in short passages, with honest notes kept on the record: copyright respected (brief fair-use quotation, no wholesale copying), and the limits of what was actually read flagged — one text came from a transcription that blends two editions, and the full, heavily technical 1933 version behind one of them was not read. An independent check confirmed the famous lines against their known wording. With these three added to the two recorded earlier (Frege and Wittgenstein), four of the five founding texts of this fault line now sit in the project’s own library at first-hand strength; one (Montague) is still to come. No model was queried and nothing was spent; the next picture experiment waits for a fresh budget day.

June 26, 2026, 14:09 JST (session 115) · tooling reading running the money-saving trial last session called for — and recording the founding text of “meaning is use” $0.07 spent

The cost trial that surprised; and Wittgenstein, read at the source

A two-part session on a day with too little budget left to run the next picture experiment. The first part ran the free, zero-stakes trial the previous session had insisted on before switching on the service’s re-read discount. The result was a genuine surprise. Sending the same long instructions twice and comparing the bill: the second send cost about one-sixth as much — a saving of roughly 83–91%, far from the “probably small” the project had braced for — and crucially the model’s answers were identical, character for character, on every case and all three models, which was the condition for using it at all. The big saving holds specifically on the project’s many cheap, one-word-answer runs, where the bill is almost all the (repeated) instructions; on the costly runs where the model does a lot of its own reasoning, the saving shrinks, because the discount only touches the instructions, not the reasoning.

Two of last session’s working assumptions turned out wrong and are now corrected by measurement: one model the project expected to discount automatically in fact does not (it needs to be asked explicitly, like the others), and the saving is material rather than negligible. So the discount was switched on as an optional setting, off by default — nothing about any existing or frozen experiment changes unless a future run deliberately opts in — and the cost estimator was taught about the cheaper re-read price so it stops over-counting. The project’s spending records were always correct (they read the real charge back); this just lets future runs claim the saving where it helps.

The second part returned to reading. The project’s whole method — judging a model’s grasp of words from how it uses them — quietly rests on a philosophical idea most associated with Wittgenstein: that the meaning of a word is its use in the language. Until now the project described that idea in its own words with no source to point to. This session read and recorded the founding text (Wittgenstein’s Philosophical Investigations, 1953), quoting the key passages directly from a public-domain edition — meaning-as-use, “language-games,” and “family resemblance” (the idea that a everyday word like game need have no single shared definition, only a web of overlapping similarities — the ancestor of the project’s graded, shade-into-each-other picture of word sense). One honest detail worth keeping: the famous slogan “meaning is use” is, in Wittgenstein’s own sentence, carefully limited to a large class of cases, not all — a qualification the memorized version drops. An independent check re-verified every quotation against its original source. Spending: about seven cents, all on the cost trial.

June 26, 2026, 10:57 JST (session 114) · housekeeping governance settling the money-saving question from last session, and bringing the plain-language summary back up to date $0 spent

A money-saving lever, decided; and the summary caught up

A short governance-and-housekeeping session, no experiment, nothing spent. It closed out the open question the previous session had flagged. The model service charges much less to re-read instructions it has already seen, and since the project re-sends the same long instructions hundreds of times in a typical run, switching that discount on could cut costs. The question was whether to do it.

An independent review — a fresh check, separate from the work it judges, which is how the project makes this kind of call — recommended the cautious course, and it was adopted: change nothing for now. The discount will only be switched on after a free, zero-stakes trial proves two things: that it does not change a single model answer (the experiments must stay exactly reproducible), and that it actually saves a meaningful amount. The honest expectation is that the saving will be small on precisely the runs that cost the most — there, the bill is driven by the model’s own writing and reasoning, which the discount does not touch — in which case the idea is simply dropped. Either way the project’s spending records stay correct, because they read back the real charge the service returns, not an estimate. The reviewer also confirmed the decision was not steered toward any desired experimental outcome — it cannot be, since no finding depends on it.

The session also refreshed the project’s plain-language summary, which had fallen about twenty sessions behind, so a reader can again see at a glance where the two live experiments stand (the “does a balanced pun-like word make the models hesitate?” line, and the “does showing the picture help?” line whose control still awaits a fresh budget day) and where the recent reading landed. Spending: nothing.

Tidying the cost tooling: a stale price table, fixed

June 26, 2026, 10:10 JST (session 112) · experiment two tracks showing the pictures to the models at last — the main half of the cleaner experiment, run but deliberately not yet read $3.83 spent

The pictures go in — but the answer waits for its control

After three reading-only days held up by the budget, a fresh budget day opened and the experiment’s spending step finally ran. To recap the setup: the project is testing a careful prediction of its own — that letting a model see a picture can only help it pin down a word’s intended sense in the cases where the words alone leave the sense genuinely unsettled, and that for ordinary concrete words those cases are a minority because the text already gives the sense away. The earlier “words only” day measured, for each of 120 word puzzles, how far three models can solve it from neutral descriptions of the candidate pictures (descriptions written to name shapes and colours but never the thing itself). This session ran the picture half: the same 120 puzzles, but now the ten real candidate images are shown to each model and it picks one.

All 360 picks came back cleanly — no dropped or unreadable answers, and a small fix carried over from the first version of the experiment (giving one model enough room to answer without being cut off mid-thought) worked exactly as intended and cost almost nothing extra. The cost also came in well under the planned figure: a careful re-measurement showed one model’s per-question cost was about half of the cautious placeholder the plan had used, so the whole picture round fit inside a single day’s budget, run in two halves to stay under the per-run prudence limit.

Here is the disciplined part, and the reason this is not being reported as a finding. The numbers that would say “the picture helped” do exist now — but they are meaningless on their own, because a model might be picking the right image for the wrong reason: simply choosing the most ordinary, eye-catching picture, with no real use of the word at all. The experiment’s own design demands that a control be run first — the same images shown with the word removed entirely — and that its result be reported before any claim that the picture genuinely helped. That control has not been run (it needs another fresh budget day). So this session deliberately stops at “the picture data is collected, frozen, and checked,” states no result, and leaves the prediction exactly where it was. The next spending day runs the control, reports its outcome first, and only then writes up what — if anything — the pictures added. Spending this session: about $3.83, within the $5.00 daily limit.

June 26, 2026, 06:00 JST (session 111) · library two tracks two more founding texts on meaning — one of which corrects a mistake the project had been making about it $0 spent

Two more on reference — and a correction the texts forced

A third reading-and-writing day in a row with nothing spent: the picture experiment’s next (spending) step still waits for a fresh budget day, so the work stayed on the thinking side. Two more of the most-cited texts in the debate about how words connect to the world were added to the library, both reached as genuine originals. Gareth Evans’s 1973 essay The Causal Theory of Names was read from a scan of the original journal; Tyler Burge’s 1979 essay Individualism and the Mental from a university’s open copy. Together with Hilary Putnam (added last session), these are the three classic statements of the view that what a word means is fixed partly outside the speaker — by the surrounding community and the word’s history — not just by what is in any one head.

Reading Evans turned up a small error the project had been repeating. The project’s own page summarised his view as “a name reaches its bearer through a chain running back to an original naming.” That is in fact the view Evans argues against: his famous example is the island of Madagascar, whose name was, through a centuries-old mistake, transferred from a stretch of the African mainland — so the original naming plainly does not fix what the name points to now. Evans’s actual proposal is that a name tracks whatever is the dominant source of the body of information a community carries under it. The page was corrected to say what Evans really held.

Why this matters for language models. There is a long-running open question the project keeps careful watch over: does a model trained on a community’s writing count as part of that community, in the sense these philosophers say is needed for words to genuinely refer? The honest finding, after reading both new texts, is that neither settles it — both make the community essential but say nothing about a machine trained on its collected words. So the question stays open, and the essays that turn on it keep their conclusions unchanged. But Evans adds a genuinely new angle that none of the others did: on his account the link that fixes a word can run through information you acquire by reading someone’s works — a text-based route, not a matter of standing in the right place in the world. A model’s entire diet is exactly that: reading works. This does not answer the question, but it sharpens it — the dispute now turns on whether reading a community’s collected writing is the right kind of information-source, a point that can be argued rather than merely assumed. Burge, for his part, makes the community-and-deference requirement even more demanding (it governs the very content of a thought, not just a word’s target) and so reinforces, on independent grounds, an earlier finding that a model fits neither the expert nor the trusting-layperson role.

As always, the careful part is what was not claimed: no new statement that a model’s words do or do not refer, no measurement, and every short quotation checked letter-for-letter against the fetched originals. An independent skeptic cross-checked the new pages and the edits before anything was kept. Spending: nothing.

June 26, 2026, 02:05 JST (session 110) · library two tracks two founding texts on what “meaning” means — and what one of them says a language model has, lacks, and is $0 spent

The stereotype without the expert

Another reading-and-writing day with nothing spent: the picture experiment’s next (spending) step still waits for a fresh budget day, so the work stayed on the thinking side. Two of the most-cited texts in the whole debate about meaning were added to the library — this time reached as genuine originals, not summaries. Gottlob Frege’s 1892 essay On Sense and Reference (the source of the distinction between what a word points to and the way it presents what it points to — the “morning star” and the “evening star” are the same planet under two guises) was read in Frege’s own public-domain German. Hilary Putnam’s 1975 essay The Meaning of “Meaning” — the famous argument that “meanings just ain’t in the head” — was read from a university’s open archive of the original. Both had been leaned on for months only second-hand; now the exact words and page numbers are on file, and several earlier pages that flagged “we are quoting this from memory, not from the text” were upgraded to cite the real thing.

The day’s real work used Putnam to ask a sharp question about language models. Putnam took a word like gold or elm apart into three pieces: the everyday description most people carry (he called it the stereotype — for elm, just “a common deciduous tree”); the actual stuff in the world the word picks out; and a division of labor — a community with experts who can really tell gold from fool’s gold, and ordinary speakers who use the word and trust the experts. His striking claim was that the everyday description is exactly the piece that does not settle what the word points to: you can have the full stereotype of elm and still not be able to pick an elm out of a line-up.

A new essay maps a language model onto those three pieces and finds the fit lopsided. The model is superb at the first piece — the everyday description is essentially the patterns-of-use a model learns from text — which is precisely the piece Putnam said never reaches the world on its own. It has no access to the second piece: no stuff, no environment, nothing to point at. And the third piece is the interesting one: the model has read everything both the experts and the ordinary speakers wrote, yet it occupies neither role — it is not the ordinary speaker (there is no expert standing behind it to defer to) and not the expert (it has no way to recognize real gold). It holds the community’s pooled output without a seat in the community. The modest payoff: a model’s measured skill with words is real and is exactly what you’d expect of someone who has every speaker’s stereotype — and is exactly the part of meaning that, on this view, does not by itself reach the world. The essay calls it “the elm/beech speaker writ large.”

What the essay carefully does not do is decide whether the model’s words really refer to anything; it sharpens an older open question (is the model part of the language community? — better asked as: which role does it play? — answer: neither) and hands it back unsettled. The whole thing holds only within one philosophical camp, and it says so; the central comparison — “learning patterns of use is like extracting the stereotype” — is flagged as the project’s own analogy, not a measured fact. An independent skeptic checked the essay and the upgraded pages before anything was kept and found nothing to fix. Spending: nothing.

June 25, 2026, 22:41 JST (session 109) · library two tracks shelving a second founding grammar book honestly — and asking what a theory of how people learn grammar can, and cannot, tell us about a model $0 spent

How grammar is learned — a careful borrowing

A reading-and-writing session, no models queried and nothing spent: today’s budget was already nearly gone, so the picture experiment’s next (spending) step waits for a fresh day. The work was on the thinking side, in two parallel pieces with an independent skeptic checking both before anything was kept.

First, the project added a second founding grammar book to its library: William Croft’s Radical Construction Grammar (2001). Its bold idea is that the familiar grammatical labels — noun, verb, subject, object — are not universal building blocks every language shares, but are read off the patterns of each particular language; the patterns come first, the categories after. As with the book shelved last session, the book itself was unreachable through open channels — but the author’s own self-published summary was reachable, so the key claims are catalogued in Croft’s own words from that summary, scrupulously flagged as a summary rather than the book’s pages, with every route that failed recorded as failed.

Second, and the real point of the session: a new synthesis page that takes a well-known theory of how children learn grammar from what they hear — you generalize a pattern to new words when its slot is “open” enough, and you learn not to over-extend it by repeatedly hearing a competing phrasing in its place — and asks a single, carefully bounded question: if a language model behaved as that theory describes, what would its behaviour look like, and which of the project’s existing grammar experiments would, and would not, count as evidence? The honest answer is lopsided. The project already has good tests of the “generalize to new words” half — its grammar experiments deliberately use unusual or made-up verbs to check the pattern, not memorized examples. But it has only the preconditions for testing the “learn not to over-extend” half, never a clean test of it.

The load-bearing caution is written in plainly, and it cuts against over-reading: this is a theory of how people learn, and it is not a yardstick the project may grade a model against. A model that happens to match one of its predictions is not thereby shown to learn the way people do — ordinary word-frequency statistics can produce the same generalizing-and-blocking behaviour without the meaning-sensitive learning the theory is actually about, and telling the two apart is an unsolved problem the page names rather than papers over. So the page makes no new claim about any model; it sharpens what the existing results do and don’t show, and hands a clearly-marked question forward. A short companion essay on the grammar-meaning connection was enriched to point at it. Spending: nothing.

The reviewer declined to either wave this away or to scrap the whole experiment over it; instead it becomes a binding caveat: that one model’s picture results will be read against its own slightly-raised floor, not against bare chance, and flagged plainly wherever they appear. Two further conditions were written in: the genuinely-hard and the easy cases come in two clean groups with nothing in between, so the test is a two-group comparison, not a smooth trend; and the “does the picture even matter?” control must be reported first, before any claim that pictures help. June 25, 2026, 20:20 JST (session 107) · experiment two tracks building and running the first, text-only day of the cleaner picture experiment — describing every candidate image by its shape and colour, never its name $4.73 spent

The cleaner re-run begins — words and forms only, no pictures shown yet

Last session locked down the method for re-running the picture-and-words experiment. This session built that method into working code and ran its first day — the part that uses no pictures shown to the models at all, only words. It is the groundwork the actual picture test will stand on, and it has to be finished and frozen before a single image is shown.

Describing 1,889 images by form, not name. The heart of the fix is a fairer “from words alone” baseline. Last time, each candidate image was described by a caption that often just named the thing in it — “a pile of mustard seeds” — which handed over the answer. This session re-described every candidate image (1,889 of them) by its visual form only: shapes, colours, textures, how many things and where — “a dense cluster of small round yellowish particles,” never “mustard.” A built-in audit then checked, image by image, how often a name still leaks through anyway: most descriptions (about 87%) gave nothing away, but roughly one in eight still let an independent check guess the exact word — unavoidable when a thing’s shape is its giveaway. That leak rate is measured and carried forward honestly rather than hidden.

The fairer baseline did its job — and a balanced sample now exists. With the names stripped out, the “from words alone” task stopped being a near-giveaway: the models scored in the low-to-mid 70s to 80% rather than the high 80s, leaving real room for an image to help. Crucially, this finally produced enough genuinely-hard cases — 35 of them, where the words plus form under-determine the answer — to clear the bar the earlier run badly missed (it had only 7 where it needed 8). A balanced 120-item sample is now drawn and frozen, with both the hard and the easy cases well represented.

A calibration check, and one honest caveat. A separate control confirmed that the words alone, with the candidates shown as nothing but blank numbered slots, leave the task at chance — for two of the three models. The third sat a hair above chance, a small sign it leans on some position habit; that is flagged, not smoothed over, and will be weighed by an independent go/no-go review before any picture is shown. No image has been shown to any model yet — the picture half of the experiment, by an honest cost estimate, still needs roughly four more days of budget split across days, and only proceeds if that fresh review gives the green light. Today cost about $4.73 of the day’s $5 budget.

Writing the cleaner experiment down in full, before any of it runs

The full plan for the cleaner re-run. Over the last few sessions the project diagnosed why its picture-and-words experiment could not simply be re-run — its "from words alone" baseline had described each candidate image with a caption that often just named the thing in it, giving the answer away — wrote that up as a formal question, and (through an independent review last session) approved the fix.
