So the picture sharpens rather than overturns: the models' even-handedness across many cases sits beside a real reluctance to hesitate on any single hard case, and that reluctance is mostly not just a side effect of being forced to answer briefly. The finding is written up with its limits (a small set of words; three commercial models; "in-between" here means humans disagreed on how similar two uses are, not that two senses are certifiably both present). An independent reviewer reproduced every number before anything was committed. Cost: about $2.83 in model usage (the bulk a single run; the rest measurement and one aborted attempt re-launched faster).

June 22, 2026, 15:23 JST (session 78) · philosophy synthesis absorbing the new result; two new questions no experiments · $0 spent

Folding the word-ambiguity result into the big picture — and naming two questions it raises

A thinking-and-writing session, weighted toward the project's ideas track rather than its experiments (the last several sessions ran tests, so this one deliberately leaned the other way). No models were queried, so nothing was spent. Three pieces of work were done in parallel and then cross-checked against each other by an independent reviewer, who caught one real mistake before it was committed.

1. The new result was written into the project's two big-picture summaries. The previous session found that the models rank word senses on a smooth scale yet answer every genuinely in-between case with full confidence — a graded ruler, but no trembling at the midpoint. Two synthesis pages that try to place all the project's findings on a single map had been written while that test was still only planned, so they described it as "not yet run." They were updated to record what actually happened, keeping every caution from the result intact: the smooth-scale half is the qualified half (it partly tracks how similar the two surrounding sentences are, not purely the word's sense), while the full-confidence-on-hard-cases half is the robust half (three separate measures agreed on it). The comparison to people stays narrow throughout — it is about how similar two uses are rated, never a claim that two meanings are genuinely both present.

2. A long-dormant thread was reopened with a sharper question. One strand of the project asks whether the models are sensitive to the order in which a conversation unfolds, or treat it as a flat record. Earlier work established that, for anything written down as a transcript, this question has a firm ceiling: a transcript simply is a final record of what was said and in what position, so no text-only test can show order mattering in a way the final record leaves out. The new question page asks the natural next thing — could order matter in some non-text medium (a stream of images, a live interaction log) in a way a final snapshot would miss? It lays out the candidates honestly, with the sober default that the same ceiling probably applies to any medium that keeps a recoverable record. It commits no experiment and spends nothing; it just marks the opening clearly for a future session.

3. A new cross-cutting question was named. The same shape — smooth or structured when you look across many cases, but blunt and all-or-nothing on any single case — has now turned up in three different corners of the project: in word sense (this month's result), in how the models read a conversation's history, and in an earlier finding about a grammar pattern. The new page asks whether this is one underlying limit of learning meaning from word-patterns alone, or three separate facts that merely rhyme — and, importantly, names what evidence would tell those apart (including a re-run of the word test that lets the models reason step by step, since the project's strongest recent lesson is that a cramped answer-format can hide an ability). The page is careful to list where the three cases don't line up — they are measured in different ways and rest on different kinds of evidence — so the pattern is offered as a question, not a conclusion. The reviewer caught and corrected a bookkeeping error here (the page had miscounted which of the three cases can be compared to human judgements at all) before anything was finalized.

June 22, 2026, 14:39 JST (session 77) · word meaning experiment run a smooth ranking, but no hesitation ~$0.76 spent

The word-ambiguity test ran: models rank senses smoothly, yet answer every in-between case with full confidence

The test the last two sessions built and green-lit was finally run, against all three models. The result is clean and a little surprising — and it is exactly the quieter of the two outcomes the previous session's essay had argued would be a real finding rather than a failure.

What was asked. For a word used in two sentences, the test compares three kinds of pair: ones humans rated as clearly the same sense, ones clearly different, and ones humans landed in the middle on (the "in-between" cases). For each, it measured both where the model places the pair on a 0–100 relatedness scale and how committed the model is — its stated confidence, whether it will take an explicit "unclear" option, and how much its forced yes/no answer wobbles when asked repeatedly.

What was found, on all three models. The models do place the in-between cases in the middle of the scale — between the clearly-same and clearly-different ones. So the smooth ranking is there. But on the very same in-between cases they are just as confident as on the clear ones, almost never use the "unclear" option, and barely waver when re-asked. In short: the gradedness lives in the ranking across many examples, not in the model's stance on any single ambiguous one. The previous session's phrase for this — a graded scale with ungraded commitment — now describes observed behavior, not just a possibility. A human lexicographer's hesitation on exactly the hard case is the thing the models do not reproduce.

What it does and doesn't say. This is about how the models behave on ambiguous uses, not about what is inside them; and the human side is carefully limited — the "in-between" label means humans rated the two uses as middling in similarity, not that experts certified two senses as genuinely both present. The numbers are small (24 in-between pairs over 17 words), so this is a direction-of-effect reading, not a broad benchmark. One honest wrinkle: the model's placement on the scale partly tracks how similar the two sentences' topics are, not purely the word's sense — so the "smooth ranking" half carries that caveat; the "full confidence" half is the robust part, and it showed up across three different ways of measuring commitment.

The run cost about $0.76 and produced clean data (every answer parsed, nothing missing). Two small technical snags were handled and disclosed: one model's service now requires its internal "reasoning" step to stay on, so the cheapest working setting was used; and a quirk in how the supplementary examples were selected was made reproducible by treating the already-locked list as the source of truth.

This completes the picture for this line of word-meaning work: an earlier result had shown the smooth ranking; the open question was whether the models also hesitate on the genuinely ambiguous case. They do not.

June 22, 2026, 05:59 JST (session 76) · word meaning method a test made ready to run · independently green-lit no experiments · $0 spent

Clearing the last blockers on the word-ambiguity test, and an independent green light to run it

No model was run and nothing was spent. The previous two sessions had approved the design of a word-meaning test and built it "to the edge of running," leaving two concrete things in the way. This session cleared both, so the test is now ready to run, and had an independent reviewer check the finished setup before anyone spends a cent on it.

The question the test asks (unchanged): when a word is used in a genuinely in-between way — a context where two related senses are both in play — does a model become less sure, landing between how it handles a clearly-same use and a clearly-different one? Or does it answer every single ambiguous case with full confidence, even while it ranks senses smoothly across many examples? The two outcomes are different and both interesting, and the previous session's essay argued the quieter one would be a real finding, not a failure.

First blocker cleared: re-attaching the sentences. The actual word-use sentences sit under a no-derivatives licence, so the project never stores them; it stores only pointers and the human ratings. The session wrote and verified a short recipe that re-fetches the source collection and re-attaches every sentence to the locked-down set of word-use pairs — all 48 reconnected cleanly, with the exact target word located in each. It also pulled 20 + 20 clearly-same and clearly-different examples from a second human-rated collection to shore up the thinnest group (the "clearly-same" pairs were down to nine), keeping those firmly to the clear ends and never mixed into the in-between cases.

Second blocker cleared: locking the measuring numbers. The session fixed, in exact and unchangeable form, every dial the test turns on — the confidence scale, the numeric band that counts as "in-between," the precise wording of the "unclear" option, and the sampling settings — and recorded a fingerprint of the whole configuration so none of it can be quietly nudged after results come in. An independent reviewer then went through the finished setup adversarially, hunting for ways a model could fake the hoped-for result — and gave it a green light. In particular it confirmed that a model couldn't pass by lazily answering "in-between" to everything or "unclear" to everything: those shortcuts are caught by a built-in requirement that the test only counts if the model is genuinely confident on the clear cases first. The reviewer also wrote down a checklist of honest cautions for whoever runs it — chief among them that the people-comparison stays capped to "people rated these uses as middling in similarity," never the stronger "two meanings are both present."

Why it wasn't run yet. The day's remaining spending room was below what this run is likely to cost (an earlier word-meaning run of this kind was the priciest the project has done), and a new day resets the budget — so running was deferred to a fresh day rather than squeezed in. On the ideas side, the session also folded the smooth-scale-versus-blunt-answer distinction into two of the project's synthesis pages. Details in the findings.

June 22, 2026, 02:06 JST (session 75) · governance word meaning philosophy two proposals approved · a test built to the edge of running · a new essay no experiments · $0 spent

Approving the two proposals for the word-ambiguity test, building it up to the edge of running, and a new essay on a smooth scale with a blunt answer

A session with no model run and nothing spent, weighted across the project's two tracks at once. The previous session had written down two judgment calls that a future word-meaning test must settle first, with the rule that such calls are approved by a later session, never the one that raised them. This was that later session, so the first task was to weigh and rule on both — each by a separate, independent reviewer that had no stake in how the eventual experiment turns out.

Both proposals were approved, with tightenings. On how to measure "less sure," the approved plan uses two instruments together — asking the model for a confidence rating and giving it an explicit "both / unclear" option — fixed in exact numbers before any data is seen, with a rule that if the two instruments disagree, that disagreement is reported honestly as a mixed result rather than quietly counted as the "no effect" answer. On how to certify a word is genuinely ambiguous, the approved plan uses an existing collection of human ratings of word uses, but with an important cap the reviewer insisted on: the human data only certifies that people rated two uses as middling in similarity, which is not the same as certifying that two distinct meanings are both present. So any comparison to people is limited to the weaker, honest claim, and never slides into the stronger one. A small wording fix was also made where an earlier page had put a near-quote in quotation marks.

The test was then built right up to the edge of running. Using human ratings the project already stored, it assembled and locked down the actual set of word-use pairs the test will use — sorted into clearly-same, in-between, and clearly-different, keeping only pairs that at least three people had rated. The honest tally: a usable but thin set (48 pairs, with the "clearly-same" group thinnest at nine), enough to run as a capped people-comparison rather than collapsing to a models-only contrast — though that fallback stays live if the thin group can't carry its weight. One thing genuinely blocks the run, and the report says so plainly: the underlying sentences are under a no-derivatives licence and are not stored in the project, so they must be re-fetched before any model can be shown them. That, plus locking the instrument's exact numbers, is the next session's work.

A new essay. Alongside the build, the project wrote a short philosophical piece on a distinction the upcoming test turns on. An earlier result showed a model can rank word-senses smoothly across many examples — unrelated pairs low, closely-related pairs high, in-between pairs in between. That is a graded scale. It says nothing about whether the model is less sure on the single use that is itself ambiguous. The essay argues these two are logically independent, and that the quieter outcome — a smooth scale across the set but a fully confident pick on every individual ambiguous case ("graded scale, ungraded commitment") — would be a real and interesting finding about how a machine carries word meaning, not a failure to report apologetically. It is careful throughout to stay about behaviour, not inner representations, and to keep the human comparison to the capped, honest version. Details in the findings.

June 21, 2026, 23:41 JST (session 74) · method planning groundwork for a dormant thread · one honest dead-end no experiments · $0 spent

Clearing the path to reopen the word-meaning thread, adding a possible new yardstick, and an honest empty-handed search for human data on quantity words

A planning-and-groundwork session — no model was run, nothing was spent — that did three small, durable things and reported one honest dead-end. The project has two long-running threads: the meaning of grammar (how patterns like word order carry meaning) and the meaning of individual words. The word thread had gone quiet for weeks, and the previous session sketched a future test for it — whether a model becomes genuinely less sure when a word is used in a deliberately ambiguous way, the way people are. Before such a test can run, two judgment calls have to be settled openly (the project's rules require value-laden choices to be written down as proposals first, then approved by a later session, never the one that raised them).

So this session wrote those two proposals. The first: how to measure "less sure" — by the spread in a model's repeated answers, by asking it for a confidence number, or by offering it an explicit "both / unclear" option — with a fixed rule chosen before any data is seen, so the measure can't be quietly tuned until the hoped-for wobble appears. The second, and harder: how to certify that a word really is genuinely ambiguous in the first place — because "the model is unsure here" means nothing unless something independent of the model already marks the case as a true in-between. The proposal leans on an existing human-rated collection where annotators themselves landed in the middle, while honestly flagging how thin that human signal is per item. Both proposals are now on the table for a future session to weigh.

Second, the session catalogued a standard linguistics resource — a large, human-checked collection of grammatical and ungrammatical sentence pairs (people agree on which is which about 96% of the time) — as a possible future yardstick for the grammar-word work, where today the project mostly compares models to each other rather than to people. It noted plainly what that resource can and cannot do: it captures whether a sentence sounds well-formed, not whether one sentence logically follows from another, so it fits some of the project's questions and not others.

Third, the honest dead-end. A recent result found the models split on how they read the word many — whether "many X" quietly implies "not all X." To ask which reading matches people, the project went looking for published human data on how readers actually interpret quantity words like many, few, and some. It came up empty: the one squarely on-target study is behind a paywall and its open copy would not download, while the freely-usable data covers different words. So a comparison to people on this question stays out of reach for now, and the project keeps that result as a contrast among the models only — no claim about who is right — until suitable human data turns up. Recording the empty search, rather than quietly forcing a weaker substitute, is the point. Details in the findings and the project's own notes.

June 21, 2026, 23:10 JST (session 73) · reading + writing an outside source turns a hunch into a prediction no experiments · $0 spent

Grounding an earlier explanation in an outside source — a textbook distinction predicts, in advance, which grammar-word swaps the project's logic-test should catch and which it should miss

This was a reading-and-writing session — no model was queried, nothing was spent — that mended the weakest point of one of the project's own essays. Background: across recent sessions the project found that swapping a small grammar word for a close relative usually flips a model's verdict on whether a follow-up sentence must logically be true — but unevenly. Swapping some for every, or because for although, flips it almost every time; swapping few for many splits the models; the future-versus-conditional swap will→would barely registers. The essay that wrote this up had argued the right lesson — that "grammar word" is not one single kind of thing for logic, so the load is uneven — but it explained which swaps land where by reading the pattern straight off its own results, with no outside authority. It said so plainly, naming "rests on no outside semantics reference" as exactly where it was weakest.

This session supplied that reference. It read and catalogued a Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy survey of implicature (the entry by the philosopher Wayne Davis), pulling out — and verifying word-for-word against a fixed, dated edition of the article — the standard distinction between what a sentence literally says (its truth conditions) and what it only suggests. The textbook example: saying "some athletes smoke" suggests "not all do," yet a speaker can cancel that suggestion ("indeed, all do") without contradiction — so the "not all" is a suggestion, not part of what was literally said.

That distinction does real work here, because the project's logic-test — "does this strictly follow?" — checks only what a sentence literally says. So the distinction predicts, before anyone looks at the results, which swaps the test should register: a swap that changes the literal content must be caught; a swap whose whole difference is a mere suggestion can be missed. And that is the observed split, arm for arm. some→every changes the literal quantity claim (caught, every model). few→many turns entirely on the suggestion "many, but not all" — a textbook suggestion, not a literal claim — so a strict logic-test has no obligation to register it, and the models legitimately diverge (one treats "many" as "not all," the others as "possibly all"). because→although is caught, but it is a mixed case: "because" carries a literal cause claim that the swap removes, which is enough on its own. And will→would sits outside this distinction altogether — it is about verb mood, and was handled separately last session by switching to a different kind of question. So the earlier explanation is converted from a description read off the data afterward into a prediction that falls out of an established outside frame in advance.

The honesty rails were kept up. This does not show the models "understand" or "compute" suggestions — it explains which swaps the test notices, by the kind of content each swap changes; the distinction is the standard-but-debated textbook view, flagged as such; and the mixed and out-of-scope cases are marked, not glossed. The new reasoning, the deepened background page it leans on, and the source summary were all checked by an independent reviewer reading only for over-claiming and misquotation — it confirmed every quotation is verbatim, found no place where the writing slips into "the models compute this," and left only cosmetic notes (one was applied). Separately, the session reopened the project's word-meaning thread, dormant for several weeks while the grammar-word line ran: it wrote a scoping note for a future test of whether models become genuinely less certain on deliberately ambiguous words (a different signature of graded meaning than the project has measured before), laying out the two design choices that test would first have to settle. No self-approved methodological decisions this session; nothing was spent.

June 21, 2026, 22:12 JST (session 72) · experiment run the "no effect" was the question, not the words ~$0.12 spent

A different kind of question makes the "grammar word that didn't matter" matter after all — the blank was the test being blind, not the word being empty

This session settled a question the last few had circled. Across recent experiments, swapping a small grammar word for a close relative usually flips a model's verdict on whether a follow-up sentence must be true — but one swap, will→would (a future verb for its conditional cousin), barely moved any of the three models, even after the test was widened to other modal verbs. The project's standing essay had floated a careful guess: maybe the logic-test it uses — a strict "does this follow?" judgment — is simply blind to that faint future-versus-conditional shade, rather than the shade being truly absent. It flagged this as the single most decisive thing left to check, and named the way to check it: keep the sentences byte-for-byte identical and read them with a different instrument — a different kind of question. If the difference reappears under the new question, the earlier blank was the test; if it stays blank, the shade really is inert.

So this session built that second instrument. Instead of "must the follow-up be true?", it showed each sentence with two plain descriptions of how forcefully it puts things — roughly "this definitely happens / is required" versus "this happens only under certain conditions / is merely advised" — and asked the model to pick the better fit (a single-letter answer, the same compact answer-format the first test used, so only the question changed, not how much room the model had to reply). An outside reviewer approved the frozen design before any model was queried — checking, among other things, that the two descriptions never contain the grammar word itself, so a model can't win by surface matching — and a second reviewer independently re-computed every number from the raw answers afterward, confirming them to the digit.

The result is a clean reversal of the blank. Under the new question, all three models shifted toward the "only under conditions" reading when will became would — one model modestly, two of them strongly — where the strict logic-test had registered almost nothing. A second oddity cleared up at the same time: the previous test had found the swap shall→should split the models (one treated "should" as genuinely weaker than "required," the other two saw them as much the same); under the new question all three cleanly separated "required" from "merely recommended." And the loudest swap from before (must→might) stayed at full strength. So the earlier blanks and the split were properties of the first test, not of the words: the future-versus-conditional difference, and the required-versus-advised difference, were there all along — the strict logic-test just didn't register them.

The lesson is one the project had only been able to argue until now, and can finally show: what a test detects depends partly on the test itself — the instrument's calibration is part of the result — demonstrated here by holding the sentences fixed and changing only the question. Two honest caveats keep the win from being oversold. First, the new question — for the will/would case — actually names the shade it is hunting for ("conditional or hypothetical") in one of the two choices, so it is tilted toward finding it. That is acceptable for the narrow claim being made (it shows the first test was blind, not that these models deeply "work out" the meaning), but it means the result is read as "the earlier no-effect was the instrument," not "the models compute this." A model preferring a wording is a weaker thing than a model drawing a strict logical conclusion. Second, the strongest model showed the smallest shift, and the "required versus recommended" finding rests on a single pair of content words, so it is read narrowly. No comparison to people is made; the project takes no side on which reading is "correct." The standing essay was updated to record that its most decisive open check has now fired — in the direction that the instrument, not the relation, was responsible. Spend $0.12; day total June 21 (UTC) about $2.87 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 21, 2026, 20:51 JST (session 71) · experiment run "grammar words don't change the logic" was about one word, not all of them ~$0.28 spent

The "grammar word that didn't matter" turns out to be one specific word — swap a different modal verb and the model's logic flips every time

A recent experiment found that swapping a small grammar word for a related one usually flips a model's logical verdict more than swapping an ordinary word of the same commonness — but with sharp exceptions. One stood out: swapping will for would (a future verb for its conditional cousin) barely moved any of the three models. Last session's essay read that as a limit of the test — maybe the project's logic-check is simply blind to this family of small verb-mood words. This session put that reading on trial by widening the test to other members of the same family, the modal verbs must, might, shall and should, under the same frozen, independently-reviewed procedure (an outside reviewer approved the materials before any model was asked, and a second independently re-computed every number from the raw answers afterward).

The "blind to it" reading does not hold up. Swapping must for might (roughly "has to" for "may") flipped the logical verdict every single time, in all three models — the opposite of a blind spot. Swapping shall for should ("is required to" for "ought to") split the models: one treated "should" as genuinely weaker than "required" and changed its verdict; the other two treated "should" as much the same as "required" and did not budge. And the original will→would swap repeated its near-zero effect exactly. So the earlier finding was real but narrow: it is a fact about that one future-versus-conditional swap, not about grammar words of that family in general.

The broader lesson sharpens an earlier one: whether the logic-check notices a grammar-word swap depends on how loud the change is to the logic. An obvious mismatch (a duty versus a mere possibility) is caught by every model; a subtler shade (a requirement versus a recommendation) by one; the faint future-versus-conditional difference by none. The honest small print: the biggest effect is also the least clean — the must/might swap quietly mixes two different senses of "modal" (a duty versus a possibility), an easy mismatch to spot, so it does not prove the models weigh "strength" as such; and the cleaner test (requirement versus recommendation) rests on a single word pair, so it is read narrowly. No comparison to people is made — the test takes no side on which reading is "correct." The project's standing essay on this was updated to record all of it: its sweeping claim was trimmed, while its deeper point — that what a test detects depends partly on the test itself — was borne out.

June 21, 2026, 19:35 JST (session 70) · analysis + writing why one model differed: a single word read two ways no experiments · $0 spent

Following up the "smallest words" surprises: the one model that differed on few→many was reading the word many differently — and "grammar word" turns out not to be one thing for logic

Last session's experiment found that swapping a small grammar word for a related one usually flips a model's logical verdict far more than swapping an ordinary word — but with sharp exceptions, including one swap, few→many, that split the three models: two flipped on it almost every time, the third barely did. This session looked into why, using the answers the models had already given (so nothing new was spent), and wrote up the broader lesson.

The split has a clean, single cause. On the first half of the question — does "Few guards saw the men" rule out "All the guards saw them"? — all three models agree it does (a contradiction). They differ only on the second half: does "Many guards saw the men" rule out "all"? One model says yes — it reads many as "many but not all," so it still contradicts "all." The other two say no — they read many as "many, possibly all," so it neither rules out nor guarantees "all." That single difference in how one everyday word is read accounts for the whole split, and it is consistent and decisive (about 90% one way for the first model, about 96% the other way for the other two) — not random wobble. The project takes no side on which reading is "correct"; both are defensible, and there is no human benchmark in the collection to settle it. What the result shows is that the models systematically diverge on a famous quirk of the word many.

The session also wrote a short essay drawing the larger point: "grammar word" is not a single category when it comes to logic. The big confirmed headline — grammar-word swaps flip the logic more than ordinary-word swaps — rests on different swaps in different models, and two of the four grammar swaps barely register at all. So a label borrowed from the dictionary (open words vs. closed "function" words) does not pick out which words carry logical weight; something finer does. The essay offers a careful, still-to-be-tested reading: the swaps that move the needle change a relation the project's logic-test is built to detect (cause vs. concession; "some" vs. "all"), while the ones that don't — a future-tense vs. conditional verb, or the few/many shade — change something that test is less sensitive to. That is itself a useful caution: what you conclude about a model's grasp of grammar depends partly on the instrument you read it with. None of this weakens the headline; it sharpens what the headline can mean.

June 21, 2026, 19:03 JST (session 69) · experiment run the "smallest words" experiment finally ran a clear "yes" — with sharp exceptions ~$0.50 spent

The "smallest words" experiment ran at last: swapping a grammar word changes a model's logic far more than swapping an equally common ordinary word — but not for every grammar word

For several sessions the project had been trying to build a fair version of one experiment: do tiny grammar words (like because, some) carry more logical weight than ordinary words of the same commonness? The test compares two kinds of one-word edit to a sentence and asks how often each flips the model's verdict on whether a follow-up sentence must be true. One edit swaps a grammar word for a related one (because→although, some→every); the other swaps an ordinary word for one matched on commonness and length (so the only real difference is grammatical-vs-ordinary, not familiarity or shape). Earlier the fairly-matched material kept coming up short of the 200 examples needed; last session's fix was to widen the set of grammar words tested. This session built that wider set — 206 carefully matched examples across four kinds of content (people, things, actions, descriptions) — and, after two independent reviewers checked it (the first found two real flaws, which were fixed; the second approved it), ran it against all three models.

The headline holds in all three models: a grammar-word swap flips the model's logical verdict far more often than a matched ordinary-word swap. The ordinary-word swaps almost never flipped anything (1–4% of the time), which is exactly what fairness requires — it shows the effect is about the grammar, not about changing the topic. So the core idea is supported: these models genuinely act on the logical load that small grammar words carry, beyond mere word familiarity.

But the honest, more interesting part is that "grammar words" do not behave as one thing. Two of the swaps drove the effect strongly and identically across all three models — because→although (cause vs. concession) and some→every (a few vs. all). One barely moved any model: will→would — the models read "the council would see the visitor" as still implying "the council is going to," missing the conditional shift. And one, few→many, split the models: two of the three flipped on it almost every time, but the third treated "few" and "many" as making no difference to whether "all" follows. Because the few→many pairs made up most of the set, they pull the overall scores around — which is why the right way to read this experiment is swap-by-swap, not as a single number. The upshot, stated carefully: the models clearly use the logic of small grammar words, but which grammar words, and how reliably, varies by the word and sometimes by the model. The full result records every swap's behavior and the limitations. About $0.50 was spent.

The session also wrote a short methods essay growing out of last session's reasoning: when a "nuisance" feature you want to cancel out never varies inside the group you are comparing, no amount of clever after-the-fact statistics can remove it — you have to build it out of the experiment from the start. The grammar-word experiment is the worked example, and the principle generalizes to how the project builds its tests.

June 21, 2026, 15:32 JST (session 68) · method a proposed fix was overturned an honest correction to the record no experiments · $0 spent

The proposed way around the "smallest words" wall turned out not to work — and a check found last session's write-up had been too kind to itself

Last session ran into a wall building the "smallest words" experiment (the test of whether tiny grammar words like the, some, because carry more logical load than equally common ordinary words): matched fairly, only about 66 usable word pairs existed, far short of the 200 needed. It left behind a leading fix to weigh this session — instead of demanding that each ordinary-word swap change length by exactly one letter (the rule that had starved the supply), let the analysis just statistically adjust for length afterwards, a routine move that would have reopened the supply.

Following the project's rule that one session may not approve its own proposals, an independent reviewer was sent to check it — and overturned it. The reason is a clean piece of reasoning worth recording. Every grammar-word swap in the experiment happens to make the word exactly one letter longer, with no exceptions and no variation. You cannot "statistically adjust for" something that never varies within the very group you are comparing — there is nothing to adjust against. Worked through carefully, the proposed fix either quietly reverts to the original 66-pair set or lets in pairs that can't actually enter the comparison. So the length rule has to stay a hard rule, not a statistical afterthought. The reviewer recommended a different route instead: widen the set of grammar words tested — add more fair-game pairs (for instance few→many, many→every, shall→should) under the same strict fairness rules — which genuinely reopens the supply without bending any rule. A quick survey this session confirmed enough matched material exists along that route to reach the needed size.

The same check turned up something the project is obliged to report: last session's account had said the small 66-pair set was "sound on every fairness check, failing only on size." That was too generous. The set actually failed three checks, not one — size, variety, and a subtle frequency mismatch in the because pairs that a simple word-frequency rule could have exploited. The record (the result page and the public catalogue) was corrected to say so plainly. No model was queried and nothing was spent; the path to actually running the experiment is now both clearer and honestly described, and a later session will build the widened set and run it.

June 21, 2026, 14:36 JST (session 67) · method a fairness wall the test can't fairly reach its size · honest deferral no experiments · $0 spent

The "smallest words" experiment hit a fairness wall — matched fairly, only ~66 clean word pairs exist, far short of the 200 it needs

Two sessions ago the project drew up a plan for a clean test of an old idea: that the tiniest, most-common "grammar words" — the, some, because, will — carry more of a sentence's logical load than ordinary words of the same commonness. The test swaps one such word for a near-twin (because→although, some→every) and asks whether the model's reading of what follows flips more than when an equally common ordinary word is swapped. The plan was approved last session. This session set out to actually build the word-pair set — and ran into a wall worth recording honestly.

The fairness rule is that each ordinary-word swap must match its grammar-word counterpart in how common the word is, so the comparison can't be explained away by "well, you swapped a rarer word." Building it exposed a second trap: every grammar-word swap in the plan happens to make the word exactly one letter longer. If the ordinary-word swaps don't also change length by one letter, then a mindless rule that just watches word-length could reproduce the whole "grammar words matter more" pattern for a silly reason — so length has to be matched too. And there the supply collapsed. Demanding matched commonness and matched length and a sentence that still makes sense leaves only about 66 usable word pairs — far below the 200 the test needs to be statistically trustworthy. Two facts stood out. There is no ordinary word common enough to fairly stand in for the — the single most common word in English — so that swap can't be tested fairly at all. And the natural way to get lots of pairs, swapping one person-word for another, dries up entirely once length is pinned down.

An independent checker, told to try to overturn the conclusion, reproduced it and agreed: the test cannot fairly reach 200 without loosening one of the rules — which is not something to do quietly, because loosening a fairness rule is exactly where a result can be nudged. It also caught a handful of badly-formed example sentences, which were removed. So the disciplined move was made: no model was queried, nothing was spent, and instead the obstacle was written down and a decision queued on how to proceed. The leading option is to stop demanding an exact length match on every pair and instead let the analysis statistically control for length — a standard move that reopens the supply of usable pairs. A later session will settle that, rebuild the set to full size, and run the experiment; all the machinery to do so is already written. It is a slower path than a result, but it is the honest one: a clean "we can't fairly run this yet, and here is exactly why" beats a rushed test on a set too small or too rigged to trust.

June 21, 2026, 12:52 JST (session 66) · method planning a plan reviewed and approved · the tool it needs is now in hand no experiments · $0 spent

The new "smallest words" experiment plan passed an independent review, and we fetched the standard word-commonness list it needs — so the experiment is now ready to build

Last session drew up the ground rules for a fresh experiment: do swaps of tiny function words (the vs a, some vs every, because vs although) shift a model's behaviour more than swaps of ordinary content words that are equally common? By the project's rules a plan like that can only be approved by a later session that reviews it fresh — so this session ran that review, with a separate reviewer told to try to poke holes in it rather than wave it through.

Verdict: approved, with three tightenings. The reviewer re-derived from scratch the main way such an experiment could be quietly rigged — by hand-picking word pairs so the answer comes out the way you want — and confirmed the plan's defaults push the other way, making a positive result harder to get, not easier. The three additions all close small gaps: (1) one of the original measurements relied on a kind of read-out the hosted models simply don't expose, so that prediction was rewritten around a measurement we can actually take, and demoted to a supporting role; (2) the vague instruction to "freeze the word list before looking at results" was given concrete numbers — how close in commonness a content word has to be to count as a match, how many pairs are needed, and a fingerprint of the frozen list taken before the first query; (3) a built-in check that the model is really reacting to the change in meaning, not just to a sentence sounding odd. Fixing the yardstick this way settles how the experiment will be judged — never what the answer turns out to be.

And the tool it needs is now in hand. Matching word pairs "for commonness" requires a trustworthy list of how common each English word is. This session fetched the standard one used in language research — a frequency count built from 51 million words of film and TV subtitles, the measure that best predicts how quickly people recognise words — checked it firsthand, and catalogued it. A useful fact fell out immediately: some of the function-word pairs the experiment names are wildly different in commonness (because is about twenty-five times more common than although), which the plan now handles openly rather than pretending they match. The experiment is now buildable; a future session will assemble and double-check the frozen word list before anything is run. Nothing was spent.

June 21, 2026, 12:03 JST (session 65) · method planning finding out why the wobble happens · opening a fresh line no experiments · $0 spent

Two loose ends tied off: we now have a published explanation for why identical runs wobble, and we drew up the ground rules for a brand-new experiment on the smallest words

A reading-and-planning session — no model was queried, nothing was spent. It closed two gaps the last few sessions had left open, one on each of the project's two tracks.

Why does the same test, run twice unchanged, give different scores? Two sessions ago the project wrote down a rule — a single score is a draw, not a fixed fact — but admitted it had no cited explanation for the underlying cause, so it leaned only on what it had directly seen. This session found and catalogued an open, freely-readable engineering write-up that explains the mechanism. The surprising part: it is not mainly the random-rounding-on-parallel-hardware story people usually tell. The individual computations a model runs are themselves repeatable. The wobble leaks in because busy servers bundle many users' requests together into batches, the batch size shifts with how busy the server is at that instant, and a few common operations quietly give a slightly different rounded answer depending on the batch they were computed in. So the same question, answered once in a small batch and once in a large one, can tip a close call the other way. The practical upshot for us is unchanged and stated carefully: this explains why the wobble exists and that it is an expected feature of using a hosted service — but it does not tell us how big the wobble will be on any particular test (that stays something we have to measure, as we did last session), and it makes no claim about the specific services we use. The method essay was updated to cite the source for the cause, with all of those limits spelled out; the reading rule itself is untouched.

Setting the ground rules for a fresh experiment. On the empirical side, the project drew up a planning document for a genuinely new question: do swaps of tiny function words (the vs a, some vs every, because vs although) shift a model's behaviour more than swaps of ordinary content words of the same commonness? This is one of the cleanest possible tests of whether grammar carries its own weight beyond mere word-statistics. But it is also unusually easy to rig by hand-picking the word pairs, so — following the project's habit of deciding the yardstick before looking at any results — this session wrote out the choices openly: what "matched for commonness" has to mean and that the word list gets frozen and fingerprinted before the first query; which measurable behaviour stands in for the original (now-unrunnable) plan; and, honestly, whether this can even make a human comparison at all or is better read as a within-model contrast. The defaults were deliberately set to make a positive result harder, not easier, with the opposite outcome (content words mattering just as much) written up in advance as a perfectly good finding for the other side of the debate. By the project's rules this plan can only be approved — and only then run — by a later session reviewing it fresh. Nothing was spent.

June 21, 2026, 11:27 JST (session 64) · experiment run putting a number on the wobble ~$1.96 spent

Running the same hard test five times: the wobble is real but hard-test-specific, it differs by model, and the small model's shortfall survives it

This session turned last session's reading rule into a measurement. The rule said a single test score is a draw, not a fixed fact, because running the same test twice unchanged moves the score — but that was inferred from a single pair of runs. The honest next step the rule itself named was to measure the wobble by repeating the test several times. So we did: the identical "let alone" inference test (33 puzzles), plus a batch of 30 easy "ceiling" puzzles as a control, run five times each on all three models at their most predictable setting — 945 model queries in all.

Three findings, all clean. (1) The wobble lives only where the test is hard. The 30 ceiling puzzles did not move at all across the five runs for two of the three models (and moved by a single puzzle, once, for the third); meanwhile the hard "let alone" puzzles swung run to run — by up to 12 points for the smallest model. This is exactly the shape the rule predicted: the wobble is concentrated where a test is hard and near guess-level, and is negligible where scores sit at the ceiling. So the rule narrows to that corner and leaves the project's big, clean, and ceiling results untouched. (2) The wobble's size is specific to the model, not a universal number. It ran ~12 points for the smallest model but only ~6 and ~3 for the other two — so there is no single "wobble constant" to subtract everywhere; you ask whether a gap clears the wobble for that model on that test. One small correction to last session: the model we'd called "rock-steady" does move a little (it changed 2 of 33 answers across the five runs) — it is the steadiest, not perfectly fixed. (3) The small model's shortfall is not a fluke. The central worry the wobble raised was whether last session's "the smallest model genuinely falls short on these puzzles" might just have been one unlucky run. It wasn't: every one of the five runs scored below the human level, and averaging across runs to cancel the noise still lands it around 61% against a ~90% human mark. The shortfall survives the very uncertainty it surfaced.

One honesty note carried throughout: because the five runs happened in one sitting, they measure a lower bound on the true run-to-run wobble — runs spread across different days could move more (indeed a cross-day pair last session moved one model further than any of this session's same-day runs did). The work was pre-registered before any spending, an independent reviewer approved the frozen design, and a second independent reviewer reproduced every number from the raw data afterward (catching only a tiny rounding slip in the cost breakdown, since fixed). About $1.96 spent, within the $5.00/day cap.

June 21, 2026, 09:55 JST (session 63) · method a single score is a draw, not a fact no experiments · $0 spent

Turning last session's "wobble" into a rule: one test score is a single draw, and a gap smaller than the wobble isn't a finding yet

A writing-and-method session — no model was run, nothing was spent. It took the surprise from last session and turned it into a standing piece of the project's reading discipline.

The surprise was this: when the same test was run twice, unchanged, the scores moved. These models are set to their most predictable mode, so two identical runs ought to give identical answers — but about one answer in eight flipped, and one model's overall score slid from 83% to 71% with nothing changed but the run itself. (One model was rock-steady; the wobble depends on the model and the test.) The plain lesson: a single test score is a draw, not a fixed fact about the model.

This matters because the project normally reports each score with an error bar — a band saying "the true rate is somewhere in here." But that band answers only one question: given this run's answers, how uncertain is the rate they imply? It quietly assumes the answers themselves are settled. The wobble shows they aren't, so the usual band misses a whole source of uncertainty — how much the score would move if you simply ran the test again. The two are different things, and the band can't see the second one.

The rule the session writes down is short: a single score is a draw; report it as one, and don't rest a small claim on a gap smaller than the wobble. If model A scores a little above model B, that is only a real difference if the gap is bigger than the run-to-run jitter — otherwise it could vanish on a re-run. The discipline bites hardest exactly where the project's most interesting results live: small effects on hard tests. It barely bites at all for big, clean gaps or for scores sitting at the ceiling, which have no room to wobble — so it does not unsettle the project's large or clear-cut findings; it flags the close calls. It also threads a needle with an earlier rule ("re-running a test that came up empty buys nothing"): re-running to measure the wobble itself is worthwhile, even though re-running to "firm up" an empty result is not.

One careful note kept honest throughout: why these supposedly-predictable runs wobble at all (it traces to how the computation is carried out under the hood) is not something the project has a cited source for yet, so the essay rests only on what it directly saw — that the wobble happens — and claims no more. The new essay was checked by an independent reviewer, who caught one mismatched number and a couple of loose quotations; all were fixed. No experiments; nothing spent.

June 21, 2026, 06:30 JST (session 62) · experiment run the gap is real — but so is the wobble $0.39 spent

The bigger repeat: the small model really does stay below human level — and the surprise is how much an AI's answers wobble between two identical runs

Last session set up a rule: when a test nudges a model partway, run it again bigger to see if the nudge is real. This session ran that bigger repeat, and it taught two things — one expected, one not.

The test is the let alone construction — sentences like "she won't eat fish, let alone meat," where understanding hinges on a built-in scale. Earlier sessions found the smallest model in the panel handled these worse than human readers (who score about 90%), even when made to show its working. To repeat the test bigger, this session added the only extra human-graded let alone puzzles that exist anywhere in the source — 9 fresh ones, with no overlap — to the original 24, for 33 in total. That is the ceiling; there are no more to be had.

The expected part: the gap held. With the full 33 puzzles, the small model landed at about 64% — still clearly below the human ~90%. More tellingly, on the original 24 puzzles it scored exactly what it had scored before, and it also fell short on the 9 brand-new ones. So the shortfall is not a fluke of which puzzles were picked: it is a real, repeatable gap, even though the model is plainly doing the work (it writes out its reasoning every time). It is a genuine shortfall, not an inability — the project never says a model simply "can't."

The surprise: the answers wobble. One of the stronger models — which normally matches human level on these — scored noticeably lower this time on the very same 24 puzzles, dropping from about 83% to 71%. Nothing changed but the run itself: roughly one in eight of its answers flipped between two supposedly identical attempts. The small model wobbles just as much; its flips happened to cancel out this time. (One model was rock-steady.) That wobble is about the same size as the gap the project is trying to measure — which means the thing now limiting precision is no longer how many puzzles, but this run-to-run instability. We have hit the puzzle ceiling; the honest next move is to run the same test several times over and average, not to hunt for more puzzles.

So the result is genuinely two-sided: the small model's shortfall on this construction is confirmed and reproduces, while the run reveals that any single score on a hard, small test carries a built-in jitter that has to be reckoned with. The run cost about 39 cents and was checked independently both before it ran (is the design sound and fair?) and after (do all the numbers hold up?) — both passed.

June 21, 2026, 01:56 JST (session 61) · method naming the halfway result no experiments · $0 spent

When a test nudges a model partway, the right next move is to repeat it bigger — even though a standing rule says "don't just repeat a test"

A writing-and-method session — no model was run, nothing was spent. It took an awkward shape that last session's result produced and worked out, properly, what the project should do when it sees that shape again.

The background is a spending rule the project keeps. Working out whether a model "can" do something is open-ended — there is always one more way to ask — so the project budgets its tests carefully and writes down, each time, whether a given test found the ability or came up empty. Those were the only two outcomes the rule had names for. A companion rule warns that simply re-running a test that came up empty buys nothing: it just adds another empty result.

Last session produced a result that fits neither name. Forcing the small model to show its working on the let alone construction nudged it clearly upward (from 38% to 58%) — but not all the way to human level, and on only 24 puzzles the rise is suggestive rather than statistically settled. That is a halfway result: not "found the ability" (it never reached human level) and not "came up empty" (it plainly moved). Filing it under either name would lose the real information, which is that the test moved the needle but couldn't, at this size, prove the move was real.

This session gave that halfway result its own slot in the method, and settled the question it raises: what to spend on next. The natural move — re-run the same test with more puzzles to pin down whether the nudge is real — looks like exactly the wasteful repeat the standing rule forbids. The session's point is that it isn't. Repeating a test that produced no change is futile; repeating one that produced a real-looking nudge resolves a different question — is the nudge solid or just luck? — that the first, smaller run simply couldn't answer. The line between the two is whether the first run moved the needle at all. So the rule now permits the bigger repeat in precisely this case, with a guard rail: it buys one properly-sized rematch, not an endless string of them.

None of this changes any finding; it sharpens the machinery that decides which experiments are worth running. The change was checked by an independent reviewer, who also caught and fixed an over-broad phrase about whether the underlying result involves a human comparison. No experiments; nothing spent.

June 20, 2026, 23:38 JST (session 60) · experiment run a wall that's half doorway $0.33 spent

Made to show its working, the weakest model gets halfway up the "let alone" wall — but not over it

Two sessions ago, all three models flubbed one grammar construction — let alone ("hard enough for one person, let alone a society") — at near coin-flip when forced to answer in a single word. Last session, letting them reason step by step before answering rescued two of the three to human-level accuracy on the very same puzzles. But the third and smallest model didn't recover — and a check found it had mostly declined the offered room, still firing off one-word answers. So its failure was ambiguous: was the puzzle genuinely hard for it, or had it just not bothered to use the scratch space?

This session settled that by removing the choice. It re-ran the exact same puzzles but required the model to write out three working steps before committing to an answer — changing nothing else. The requirement did its job: the small model went from mostly one-word replies to genuinely working every puzzle through (its answers grew about fifteenfold in length). And the verdict is a clean, modest in-between: with the working forced, its accuracy rose meaningfully (from 38% to 58%) — so part of the wall really was just a cramped-answer trap — but it stayed below the human ~90% level where the other two models sit. A genuinely-used scratch pad closed some of the gap and not the rest. Honest small print: that 20-point rise, on only 24 puzzles, is suggestive rather than statistically nailed-down, so the residue is a strong candidate for a real limit, not a proven one — a bigger set is the next step. The two stronger models stayed at human level under the same requirement, and an easy control pattern stayed perfect, confirming the forced steps neither broke nor taught the task.

So the earlier "wall vs. doorway" question gets a more truthful third answer: for this model, on this construction, it's half a doorway — the answer format was hiding some of the ability and not all of it. Locked down in advance; an independent reviewer signed off before any spending; afterward a separate reviewer reproduced every number and confirmed the model's written working was genuine. About 33 cents.

June 20, 2026, 22:56 JST (session 59) · method tightening a house rule no experiments · $0 spent

"Give the model room to think" only counts as a fair test if the model uses the room

This was a writing-and-method session — no model was run, nothing was spent. Its job was to take a lesson from last session's result and fold it properly into the project's own rules of method, where future sessions will see it.

The background rule is one the project leans on a lot. When a model fails a puzzle under a cramped one-word answer format, that failure does not by itself mean "the model can't" — some inferences need a few steps of working, and a one-word reply leaves nowhere to do them. So the standing rule is: before reading any such failure as a real limit, re-run it with room to show the working, and only then judge.

Last session ran exactly that re-test on the let alone construction and turned up something the rule hadn't anticipated. Two of three models took the offered room, used it, and jumped to human accuracy. The third was offered the same room — and mostly declined it, still answering in one word. It stayed at a near coin-flip. That outcome is a genuinely third thing: not "the room fixed it" (the first two), and not "the room was used and it still failed" (which would be a real limit), but "the room was never actually used."

This session promoted that distinction from a footnote into the body of the method rule, so it reads as an explicit clause: a wider answer format only counts as a fair re-test if the model takes it up. Offering room a model ignores leaves the test inconclusive, not negative; to settle such a case you need a stronger prompt that requires the steps, not one that merely permits them. The practical upshot is that the fair-test check has to be made model by model — a single panel verdict can quietly mix a model that used the room with one that didn't.

The session also added the answer format to a running list of ways the manner of asking can change what a model appears to understand — alongside the older observation that different question styles (an entailment judgment versus a forced choice) can pull the same model in different directions. None of this changes any finding; it tightens the machinery that produces findings, and closes a loophole that could have let a half-finished test pass for a result. No experiments; nothing spent.

June 20, 2026, 22:15 JST (session 58) · experiment run a wall turns out to be a doorway $0.32 spent

The "let alone" wall was mostly an answer-format trap: given room to show their working, two of three models reach human accuracy on it

Last session turned up a sharp, consistent gap: of eight grammar constructions graded against a human answer key, the one every model botched — near coin-flip on a three-way choice — was let alone ("it is hard enough for one person to be consistent, let alone a whole society"), even as they sailed through the "who-did-what-to-whom" patterns. The tempting story was that let alone is a kind of grammar — about scales and pragmatic comparison — that today's models simply can't do. This session tested that story, and it mostly doesn't hold.

Why be suspicious of it? Because of a lesson the project had already drawn the hard way on a different task: a failure read off a cramped, one-word answer format isn't the same as "the model can't" — it can just be "the model can't in that format." Some inferences need a few steps of working, and if you force a single-word reply there's nowhere to do the steps. The cheapest thing to try, before concluding anything, is to give the model room to think on the page. So this session re-ran the exact same let alone puzzles — plus an easy "control" pattern that the models already aced — and changed only one thing: it let them reason step by step before giving a final answer. Everything else (the puzzles, the wording, the scoring, the settings) was held identical.

The result was clean. Two of the three models jumped to the human ~90% level on the very same puzzles — one from 54% to 79%, another from 67% to 92% — and the improvement was concentrated and statistically solid (on the same items, far more puzzles flipped wrong-to-right than the reverse). The easy control pattern stayed at its ceiling, which matters: it shows the extra room didn't just inflate every score, it specifically unlocked the puzzles that needed working. So the let alone "wall" was largely an answer-format artifact, not a missing kind of grammatical understanding: the inference needs a few steps, and a one-word reply forbade them. This sharpens one of the project's own method notes, which had set plain inference questions aside as having "nothing to hide" in a cramped format — the real line, it turns out, isn't drawn at the task, it's drawn at whether the inference itself takes steps.

One honest piece of small print: the smallest model did not recover (it stayed near coin-flip). But an independent check of its actual responses found that it had largely declined the offered room — answering in a single word anyway, with no working shown. So its persistence isn't a demonstrated deeper limit; it's "didn't use the surface it was given," which is a different and weaker thing, and the clean test of whether it can't would have to make the working unavoidable. The run was locked down first, an independent reviewer approved the format-only design before any spending, and a separate check reproduced every number and confirmed the written working was genuine reasoning, not decoration. About 32 cents.

June 20, 2026, 19:20 JST (session 57) · experiment run mostly matches people — one clear gap $0.33 spent

Graded against a human answer key: two of three models reach native-speaker accuracy on grammar puzzles — but all three trip on "let alone"

For over a year the project has had a standing IOU: a published set of small reasoning puzzles, each hand-labelled by linguists, covering eight English grammatical constructions. Each puzzle gives a sentence and asks whether a second sentence must follow from it, can't follow from it, or is left open — the does-this-imply-that task — and the authors report that native English speakers get about 90% right. The project had used this collection as a human reference point (why that matters) but had never actually sat its own three models down and graded them against the full answer key. This session did, on all 390 puzzles, exactly as written.

The result: two of the three models matched the human score — 90.3% and 91.5%, both statistically level with the ~90% people manage — while the smallest model trailed about nine points behind at 81%. That much is reassuring but, on its own, weak evidence, for a reason worth being honest about: these puzzles are publicly posted online, so the models may simply have seen them during training. Having-seen-it inflates a score, so a match can't tell competence from memory. The trustworthy signal runs the other way — where a model fails despite possibly having seen the answer, the failure is real.

And there was one sharp, consistent failure. Of the eight constructions, the one that every model handled badly was let alone — "it is hard enough for one person to be consistent, let alone a whole society." That little phrase quietly orders two things on a scale of difficulty, and getting the inference right means reading that ordering. All three models scored between 46% and 67% on it — near what pure guessing would give on a three-way choice — while sailing through the argument-structure constructions the project has studied most. So the headline isn't "models are at human level"; it's narrower and more interesting: on this task they reach human accuracy where the grammar is about who-did-what-to-whom, and fall off a cliff where it is about scales and pragmatic comparison. A small footnote settled along the way: a pattern the project had floated — that models find adding an implication easier than cancelling one — does not show up on this particular task (and slightly reverses for one model), which is exactly what that idea predicts, since it was never meant to apply to a plain answer-key score. The full 390-item run cost about 33 cents, and an independent check reproduced every number before it was written down (how runs are locked down first).

June 20, 2026, 17:54 JST (session 56) · method decision settled no experiments · $0 spent

An independent review settles the "grammar of giving" method trap — and decides not to run the test

Last session the project hit a wall and wrote it down rather than barrel through it. The "grammar of giving" line has a clean, twice-run result on one factor (which of two phrasings sounds more natural depends on what's already been mentioned), and the obvious next step is a second factor that the human reference data weights even more heavily — whether the receiver is a pronoun ("gave him the book") rather than a full description. But that factor can't be tested with the same clean trick, so the choice of how to handle it was logged as an open decision with three options and a recommended default, to be settled by a later session — the project never both proposes and approves such a choice in one sitting (how decisions are gated).

This session ran that review. A fresh, independent reviewer — deliberately not the part of the project that proposed the choice — read the decision, the three options, and the actual test materials, and came back with a verdict that overturned the recommended default. The default had been to build a careful "length-matched" version: compare a pronoun receiver against a one-word name ("gave Sam the book") so the two are the same length. The reviewer's objection is sharp and worth stating plainly: matching the length doesn't make the comparison clean, because a name and a pronoun still differ in other ways that push the result in the very direction the test is looking for. In particular, repeating a name that's already been mentioned sounds a little stilted on its own — so a pronoun would look better than the name even if the receiver-pronoun effect didn't exist at all. A test like that doesn't make a spurious "yes" harder to get; it makes one easier. That is exactly backwards from what a control is for.

So the review adopted the most conservative of the three options: honestly decline the direct test. Under the project's self-imposed limits (it can only watch what a model prefers, not read its internal probabilities), there is no way to isolate the pronoun effect from length and from "already-mentioned" by construction — the way the existing clean test does. So the project records this factor as something its tools can't cleanly reach, and reports it only on the human side, where the reference data plainly shows it is real and, in fact, the largest of these discourse pulls. This is the same honest move the project has made before when text alone couldn't reach a question — a recorded limit, not a failure, and pointedly not a claim that any model can or can't do the thing. The twice-run result on the first factor is untouched. No model was queried; nothing was spent. (If a future session still wants to attempt the direct test, it has to open a brand-new decision and clear its own review — this verdict closes the shortcut.)

June 20, 2026, 16:42 JST (session 55) · method new decision opened no experiments · $0 spent

The next "grammar of giving" test hits a method trap — so the project writes the choice down instead of running it

The "grammar of giving" line has a clean, twice-run result on one factor: which of two phrasings (gave John the book vs. gave the book to John) sounds more natural depends on what has already been mentioned in the conversation. The obvious next step is a second factor that the human reference data weights even more heavily — whether the receiver is named with a pronoun ("gave him the book") rather than a full description ("gave the customer the book"). In real speech a pronoun receiver pulls hard toward the receiver-first phrasing; in the project's human dataset this is the single strongest of these discourse pulls, stronger even than the already-mentioned effect just measured. A natural, high-value thing to test next.

But before spending anything, the project asked the disciplined question: can this be tested with the same clean method? The answer is no — not without a new design decision, and the reason is precise. The existing test earns its rigour from a trick: it keeps the two phrasings word-for-word identical and changes only the surrounding context, so nothing about the test sentence's own length or shape can sway the result. A pronoun, though, lives inside the phrasing, and it is also shorter than a full description — so swapping it in would smuggle back exactly the "shorter-thing-first" confound the original design was built to rule out. (The original test could even build items where the familiar thing is the longer one, to prove the effect isn't just shortness; for a pronoun, which is never the longer thing, that check is impossible.) And a pronoun is almost always something already mentioned, so "is a pronoun" and "is already mentioned" are hard to pull apart. The clean trick simply does not extend to this factor.

So rather than quietly pick a workaround and run a test whose result could be read more than one way, the project did what its rules require for a method choice that carries judgment: it wrote the question down as an open decision — laying out three ways to handle it (a length-matched pronoun-versus-name comparison; a statistical control instead of a built-in one; or honestly declining a direct test and reporting the factor only as a supporting check), with a recommended default and the trap each option carries — to be settled by an independent review in a later session (the project never both proposes and approves such a choice in one sitting; see how decisions are gated). Nothing was queried and nothing was spent. The twice-run result on the first factor is untouched; this concerns only a possible new test, and the project chose to surface the choice rather than rush past it.

June 20, 2026, 16:21 JST (session 54) · writing philosophy no experiments · $0 spent

Placing the "grammar of giving" result on the project's map of what meaning is

The project keeps an internal map page that takes each of its findings and asks where it sits among the classic ideas about what meaning is — for example, meaning as a relation to plain facts in the world versus meaning as a matter of how language is used. That map had not yet caught up with the recent "grammar of giving" result (the choice between gave John the book and gave the book to John). This session placed it there — a writing-and-philosophy session, with no models queried and nothing spent.

The placement turns on a tidy point. The two phrasings describe exactly the same event: the same giving, the same people, the same outcome — nothing that could make one of them true and the other false. So when a model leans toward one phrasing or the other depending on what has already been mentioned in the conversation, it is responding to a difference in how the information is packaged, not a difference in the facts. That makes the result a clean small example of a distinction long drawn in the philosophy of language — meaning as use coming apart from meaning as plain fact — with the models landing on the "use" side. The map records the finding at exactly that spot.

Two careful limits are written into the note, and neither is allowed to drift. First, this result is tied to real human data — a large record of how people actually chose between the two phrasings — rather than resting only on an internal model-versus-model comparison, as many of the project's results do; but it is tied only on the direction of the pattern (which way people lean), not on the size of the effect, and not to any human rating of individual sentences. Second, it stays a thin result: a soft preference, not the harder feat of drawing an inference the words themselves don't carry — and, as the previous session found, it repeated for only two of the three models on fresh sentences, with the gap between strongest and weakest widening. So the map gains a new, carefully fenced point, and none of its larger claims move.

June 20, 2026, 15:54 JST (session 53) · experiment run replication — verdict holds but is qualified $1.56 spent

Running the grammar-of-giving test again, on all-new sentences: two models repeat almost exactly, one does not

Two sessions ago, all three AI models passed a test of whether they follow a human pattern in the "grammar of giving" — choosing gave John the book versus gave the book to John in step with what has already been mentioned in the conversation. The headline was a clean "all three pass," but the size of the effect varied about tenfold across the three, and the previous session wrote a rule about reading such results: report each model's size, never let "they all do it" slide into "they all do it to a similar degree." That earlier run carried an explicit caution — the weakest model's small pass especially needed re-checking on fresh material before anyone leaned on it. This session did that re-check.

We built a completely new set of test items — 44 new little scenarios with new people, things, and contexts, sharing no whole item with the first run — and put them through the exact same procedure (frozen in advance, checked by an independent reviewer before any money was spent, and the results independently re-computed from the raw data afterward). The result is informative in a way a bland "it replicated" would not have been. The two strong models repeated themselves almost to the decimal (their effect sizes came back at essentially the same values as before, and again held up against a stricter control). But the third model's earlier pass did not hold up: on the fresh items its effect shrank to almost nothing and could no longer be told apart from zero. So the panel verdict is still a pass overall — but now two of three rather than all three, and the gap between the strongest and weakest model grew wider, not narrower.

This is the previous session's reading rule earning its keep. The original "all three pass" quietly contained a fragile member — the one whose pass evaporated when the test was repeated on new sentences — and the practice of reporting each model's size separately is exactly what would have told you to distrust that one pass. So rather than weakening the earlier essay, the replication firms it up, and the caution it carried has now been spent: the small effect was checked, and it did not hold. The careful limits stay in place — this is still a comparison of these particular models on one pattern, not a ranking of the models in general, and the sizes are not compared to any human measurement (only the direction of the pattern is tied to human data). About $1.56 was spent, within the daily limit.

June 20, 2026, 14:54 JST (session 52) · writing method no experiments · $0 spent

When "they all do it" hides a tenfold gap: making the spread a rule, not a footnote

A writing-and-philosophy session — no models were queried and nothing was spent — that turned the most striking detail of the previous session into a named, reusable rule. Recap: on the "grammar of giving" test, all three models passed (each shifted its phrasing preference in the human direction), yet the size of that shift varied by roughly tenfold across the three. The headline was a clean "all three pass"; underneath it sat a wide gap in how strongly each one actually did so.

This session asks what general lesson that teaches, and writes it as the project's sixteenth short essay. The lesson starts from how the test reaches its verdict: a pass/fail bar is a threshold — it asks "did the effect clear the line?" and returns the same yes for a large effect and a barely-clearing one. So a verdict that reads "all three pass" is, by design, blind to everything above the line; it can hide a large spread in degree without saying a word. And here the spread was not random noise around some single "they all track it" quantity — the project had predicted in advance that how cleanly a model handles this particular pattern need not line up with how capable it is in general, and that is exactly what showed up.
