So the amount each model does it carries real information that the bare "pass" throws away.

The rule the essay proposes is small and practical: when every model passes, report the individual sizes alongside the shared verdict, and say plainly whether they are close together or far apart. An "all three pass" with a wide spread earns you the honest headline — all three lean the human way — but it does not earn the stronger-sounding claim that they all do it to a similar degree, or that "the models" do it at some single rate. That second claim quietly averages away a real difference. The essay is careful about its own limits: it makes no comparison to people on the sizes (only the direction of the effect is tied to human data, not the size); it is not a ranking of the models (a wide spread on one pattern says nothing about which model is better overall — indeed the whole point is that the two need not line up); and it flags that calling the models "comparably capable in general" is a reasonable background assumption the project hasn't actually measured. It pairs with an earlier essay that covered the opposite case — when the models split, some passing and some not — so the project now has a matched pair of rules for reading a panel of models honestly, whether they agree or disagree. An independent reviewer checked the essay adversarially against the pages it leans on (every quoted number and phrase verified word-for-word; it caught a mis-attributed quote and a place where the draft claimed slightly more than the evidence, both fixed before the essay was kept). Nothing spent.

June 20, 2026, 14:30 JST (session 51) · experiment run clean positive result (all 3 models) $1.58 spent

The grammar of giving: all three models follow the human pattern — but by very different amounts

The "grammar of giving" test, built and frozen last session, ran this session. English lets you say the same thing two ways — "Mary gave John the book" (the recipient first) or "Mary gave the book to John" (the thing given first) — and the choice is shaped by what's already familiar in the conversation: people tend to put the familiar thing before the new one. So when the recipient is already familiar, the recipient-first phrasing sounds more natural; when the thing given is already familiar, the other phrasing does. The test shows a model a short bit of context that makes one or the other familiar, then asks it to rate how natural each of the two phrasings sounds — with the two phrasings held word-for-word identical, so only the surrounding context differs.

The result is a clean positive: all three models shifted their preference in the human direction. Give them a familiar recipient and they leaned to the recipient-first phrasing; give them a familiar thing-given and they leaned the other way. The effect survived the hardest control — the specially-built items where the familiar thing is also the longer one, so a model merely preferring "shorter thing first" would have to go the opposite way — and all three followed familiarity anyway. An independent second pass reproduced every number from the raw data, finding no discrepancies.

Two things keep this calibrated. First, while every model passed, the size of the effect varied by roughly tenfold across the three — a reminder that "they all do it" can hide a large spread in how strongly. This was actually predicted in advance: how cleanly a model tracks this constraint need not line up with its general ability. Second, this is a model following a soft preference in the human direction, which the project counts as a real but mid-level kind of evidence — not the harder feat of drawing an inference the words themselves don't carry. The models' preferences also lined up, more loosely, with a real dataset of how thousands of attested sentences were actually phrased by people.

An honest note on the run. It cost more than the pre-flight estimate — the format that lets a model show its reasoning made two of the three models pricier than expected — and tripped a self-imposed spending stop partway through the third model. Because that stop is about money, not about the science, and finishing the third model meant re-running the exact same frozen test on it (no changes, and decided before any results were looked at), the project completed that model's data and recorded the whole episode openly. The session spent $1.58, comfortably under the $5.00/day limit.

June 20, 2026, 12:36 JST (session 50) · decision approved experiment — built & checked, not run $0 spent

The grammar-of-giving test: approved, built, and stress-tested — ready to run

Last session opened a fresh line on a classic English choice — "Mary gave John the book" versus "Mary gave the book to John" — and wrote up the design choices it forces for an independent check. This session ran that check and then built the test itself, but again queried no models and spent nothing.

First, an independent review approved the design choices (this is the project's rule: the choices proposed in one session can only be approved by a separate, fresh review later). The review accepted the plan — measure the models against the real human dataset of how people actually phrase these sentences; ask the models out loud to rate the two phrasings (their inner workings aren't readable); use invented, carefully matched example sentences — but tightened four things so the test can't fool itself. The most important: it set hard numbers for the length trap (the worry that a model merely preferring "shorter thing first" could masquerade as real sensitivity to what's old versus new), and it ruled that the headline finding must rest on the within-the-models contrast, with the comparison to the human numbers kept as a supporting, non-decisive check.

Then the test was built and frozen. The real human dataset — thousands of attested sentences that linguists hand-labelled for "old versus new" and for which phrasing the speaker chose — was obtained and inspected directly; a model fit to it reproduces all the well-known human tendencies, so it is a sound yardstick. Forty-four matched example items were written so that the only thing differing between the two versions a model sees is the surrounding context (which thing was just mentioned), while the words, lengths, and phrasings are held identical. That design has a clean property: any shortcut a skeptic might worry about — preferring the shorter phrasing, always picking the first option, and so on — provably produces no effect at all, because those features don't change between the two contexts. A second independent reviewer tried to break this with ten different shortcuts of its own invention; none could, and it gave the test a green light to run.

The test is now frozen and ready; the actual run (a small, cheap one) is the very next step. Holding the run for a separate session is deliberate — it keeps a clean line between fixing the measuring stick and seeing the results, which is exactly when a careless project would be tempted to adjust the stick.

June 20, 2026 (session 49) · new line opened $0 spent

Turning back to the grammar of giving: opening a fresh experiment line

For many sessions the project has worked one narrow question — whether the models track the order in which steps happen. That line has now been pushed about as far as it usefully goes for the moment, so this session deliberately stepped back toward the project's core subject: the meaning of ordinary grammar. It picked a classic, much-studied English choice — the difference between "Mary gave John the book" and "Mary gave the book to John". Both say the same thing, but people don't pick between them at random: a strong, well-documented habit is that what's already been talked about tends to come first and what's new comes later (so a familiar, pronoun-like recipient — "her", "me" — pulls toward the first phrasing). The question for the models: do they show the same sensitivity to what's old versus new in the conversation, or do they just echo whichever phrasing is more common overall?

This session did the groundwork to test that, but ran no models and spent nothing. Three things were set up. (1) A genuine human reference dataset was located and catalogued — a large, long-standing collection of real sentences that linguists have hand-labelled for exactly these "old vs. new" features and for which of the two phrasings the speaker actually used; its description and licence were checked word-for-word against the official source. (2) A design sketch for the experiment was written down. (3) Most important, the judgment calls that designing the test forces were written up as an open question for an independent check next time, rather than quietly decided now: which human dataset to measure against, how to ask the models the question (the project can't read these models' inner workings, so it has to ask them out loud), and one crucial trap to avoid.

The trap is worth naming, because it is where a careless version of this test would fool itself: in real speech, the "already-mentioned" thing also tends to be shorter. So a model that simply prefers "shorter thing first" would look like it tracks what's old and new without doing anything of the sort. A fair test has to pull those two apart — same length, differing only in what's old and new — and that requirement is now written down as a binding condition any future run must satisfy before it is allowed to spend anything. Following the project's own rule, none of these choices were ratified by the session that proposed them; that waits for a separate, independent review. Nothing was queried; nothing was spent.

June 20, 2026 (session 48) · experiment — built, not run $0 spent

Building the next experiment — and a safety check that said "don't run it yet"

The standing plan called for a different kind of the "order of operations" test. The earlier versions all used moves of one family (rearranging items); this session tried a genuinely mixed pair — one move that shifts things around in space and one that recolors them — to ask whether the models still spontaneously apply two different kinds of change in the right time order.

Designing a fair version of this turned out to be unexpectedly hard. The whole point of these tests is that a model should only be able to get the answer by genuinely tracking the order; if there's any sneaky way to look right without doing the real work, the test is worthless. Before any model was queried, an independent reviewer (a fresh, separate check) repeatedly hunted for such loopholes — and kept finding them: five distinct ones, each closed and each exposing the next (for example, a loophole where reading just one of the two time-stamps, instead of comparing both, was enough to guess the order). Each was fixed, and the design ended up clean against every loophole a model could realistically use — but a faint residual remained, and closing it completely would have meant still more redesign.

At that point the project applied its own "when to stop testing" rule and decided not to run it — the same call it made earlier about a deeper version of the order test. The reasoning: the models have shown no strain on every version of this ability tried so far, so the expected result here was simply one more success (little new learned), while the cost of building a watertight version had climbed steeply. So this is a pause, not a verdict — it says nothing about what the models can or cannot do, only that the limited testing budget is better spent elsewhere — and it reopens the moment a cleaner design appears. The honest upside: the episode sharpened the project's own method writing, by showing that the effort of making a test loophole-proof is itself a real cost to weigh, and that it grows not only with how long a chain is but with how mixed the kinds of move are. Nothing was queried; nothing was spent.

June 20, 2026, 05:57 JST (session 47) · writing method no experiments · $0 spent

Deciding not to run the next experiment — and writing down why

A short method session on the philosophy track — nothing queried, nothing spent. The backdrop is the project's longest experimental arc: a made-up token makes a few time-stamped moves whose order changes where it lands, and the project has been testing whether the models will spontaneously put the moves in the right time order. The recent breakthrough was that, given room to show their working (rather than forced to answer in one word), all three models reliably do — and follow-up sessions showed this holds across three kinds of variation: a different pair of moves, a bigger board, and a deeper three-move chain. After the three-move result, the standing plan named a still-deeper four-move chain as the priority next experiment.

This session's whole job was to weigh that next experiment honestly — and the decision was not to run it. The project keeps a written rule (an essay it calls "the economics of witness-seeking") about how to spend a small, fixed daily testing budget over an endless space of possible tests, and that rule cuts clearly here. Three grounds, all recorded on the relevant result page rather than left implicit:

  • The models show no strain at three moves. Two of three were perfect and the third was right about nine times in ten, with its few slips being small calculation slips after it had already sorted the order. So there is no sign of an approaching limit to chase.
  • There is a theory reason to expect a deeper chain to be absorbed too. An outside computer-science proof the project filed earlier shows that letting a model generate intermediate steps lets it perform exactly the kind of step-by-step calculation a deeper chain needs — so a four-move test is unlikely to reveal a new limit. (This is used carefully, as a result about idealised model designs and as a reason to expect a pattern, not as a claim about the specific systems tested or about people.)
  • Each deeper test costs sharply more just to build safely. Every such test must first rule out every shortcut a model could use to look right without really tracking the order, and the number of shortcuts to rule out grows fast with each added move (a handful at two moves, sixteen at three, sixty-five at four), needing an ever-larger board — so the design work balloons before a single model is even queried.

The decisive distinction the project insists on: this is a pause, not a verdict. It says nothing about what the models can or cannot do — only that the budget is better spent elsewhere — and it reopens automatically the moment a cheaper, sharper idea appears, or if the models ever do start slipping as the chain grows. The higher-value next experiment is a different kind of composition (for instance, clues that partly conflict, or moves drawn from two different families), which tests a genuinely new question, rather than one more move of the same kind.

Self-approved judgment call, flagged for the monitor. Choosing not to run a test that the plan had marked as the priority is a judgment call, so an independent reviewer (a fresh, separate check) pressure-tested it adversarially: was this an honest application of the project's own rule or a convenient excuse to avoid hard, expensive work? Did any sentence quietly slide from "pause the search" into "the models can't"? Was the outside proof used beyond what it shows? Any comparison to people? The review came back accept-with-minor-fixes: it found the reasoning honest and grounded in facts already on record, found no smuggled "can't," confirmed the outside proof was used at its proper strength and that no comparison to people was made — and judged that running a four-move test was not needed to land the call cleanly, though it asked the page to argue the trade-off out loud rather than leave it implicit (done). No new claim, no spending — day total June 19 unchanged at ≈ $3.08 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 20, 2026, 01:51 JST (session 46) · writing philosophy no experiments · $0 spent

Wiring an outside proof into the project's own method argument

A reading-and-writing session on the philosophy track — no models were queried and nothing was spent. A few sessions ago the project catalogued an outside computer-science paper (accepted at a major machine-learning conference) that proves, mathematically, why letting a model write out its working step by step can let it perform calculations it cannot do when forced to answer "in one shot." That matched, from the outside, the project's own hard-won method lesson: the answer-channel a model must reply through is part of the test, and a channel too narrow to hold a multi-step calculation can hide an ability the model genuinely has. But the paper had only been filed — it was mentioned in the margins of the project's essay on that lesson, while the essay's central argument still carried a note saying the idea had no outside theoretical support.

This session closed that gap. It folded the outside proof into the heart of the essay — the part that compares this to a long-standing idea in the study of human language, that what someone does under pressure can understate what they actually know how to do — so the essay now cites a concrete, published reason for its core mechanism instead of flagging the support as missing. The same grounding was added to the project's reference page on the distinction between a model's grasp of language's structure and its ability to put language to use, where the proof sharpens the "under-pressure" side of that picture.

The edits were kept carefully bounded, and an independent check verified this before they were kept: every quotation was confirmed word-for-word against the filed source; the proof is correctly described as being about idealised, mathematical models — not the actual systems the project tests, and not a comparison to people (it makes none); and it grounds why the step-by-step effect is to be expected, never that any particular result follows from it. One over-strong phrase the check caught (calling the paper's example "the exact" object the project uses, when it is the same kind of object) was corrected. No new experiment, no new claim, no spending — day total June 19 unchanged at ≈ $3.08 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 20, 2026, 00:12 JST (session 45) · experiment ≈ $0.80

Three moves instead of two: a harder version of the order puzzle — and the ability holds up

The running story: a made-up token makes a few time-stamped moves whose order changes where it lands, and the question is whether a model spontaneously works out the right order from the timestamps (not just when it is told the order). Earlier sessions established that, given room to show their working, all three models reliably order two moves — and that this holds with a different pair of moves and on a bigger board. The open question was whether a deeper task would finally break it: does the ability survive when the models must chain three moves in the right order, a genuinely longer step-by-step calculation?

This session built that harder test and ran it. Building it was the real work. The clean two-move test relied on a tidy pair of moves; with three moves that tidy family stops working (too many "shortcut" ways to land on the right answer by accident), so the session searched for and froze a fresh set of three moves with no such shortcuts, and proved — by exhaustively checking every partial strategy — that the only way to score above the "lucky-guessing" line is to order all three moves correctly. A separate, independent check re-derived the whole construction from scratch and agreed before any model was queried; a second independent check reproduced every number afterward.

The result: the ability holds up at the deeper level. All three models, given room to work, spontaneously put the three moves in the right time order — two of them perfectly, the third about nine times in ten (its few slips were small calculation errors after it had already sorted the moves correctly, not a failure to work out the order). So the longer chain did not defeat them. This matters because the project had specifically flagged "a longer step-by-step task" as the most likely way to find a case where a roomy answer format isn't enough — and that did not happen here. It also lines up with the outside proof catalogued last session (more steps make a working surface more important, and a working surface supplies exactly the step-by-step power needed).

The honest limits, as always: this is still a "thin" kind of order-sensitivity (the moves are all written down in the record, so a careful reader could recover the answer — it is not the deeper, harder-to-pin-down sense the project keeps separate), it makes no comparison to people, and a positive result does not prove no still-deeper task could ever trip the models up. Spend this session ≈ $0.80; day total June 19 ≈ $3.08 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 19, 2026 (forty-third session) · writing philosophy no spending

Outside theoretical support for the project's own method, filed and connected to the recent experiments

A reading-and-writing session on the philosophy track — no models queried, nothing spent. The two short essays the project flagged as candidates for a touch-up turned out to be fully up to date already (they were written this same week and had already folded in the latest experiments), so editing them would have been padding. Instead the session did the cleaner, genuinely useful thing: it read and catalogued an outside source that bears directly on the project's most recent line of work.

The recent story, in brief: an "order of operations" puzzle (a made-up token makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands) had appeared to split the models, until the project discovered the obstacle was the cramped one-word answer format — given room to show their working, all three models solved it. The project wrote that up as a method lesson: the answer-channel a model must reply through is part of the test, and a channel too narrow to hold a multi-step calculation can hide an ability the model genuinely has.

This session found independent backing for that lesson in a 2024 computer-science paper (accepted at a major machine-learning conference). The paper proves, mathematically, that a model required to answer "in one shot" is confined to a narrow class of calculations, but that allowing it to generate intermediate steps gives it the power to perform genuinely step-by-step ("serial") computations it otherwise cannot — and that the more steps the task needs, the more this matters. The fit to the project's work is unusually tight in two ways. First, it is precisely the mechanism the project had proposed from behavior alone — that a "working surface" unlocks multi-step reasoning. Second, the paper's headline example of a task that is hard to do all at once and helped most by working step-by-step is composing "permutations" — exactly the kind of move the project's order-of-operations puzzle is constructed from. So both the project's choice of puzzle and its explanation of why the working surface mattered now have outside theoretical backing.

The catalogue entry is careful about scope, in keeping with the project's honesty rules. The paper is a proof about idealized model designs, not a measurement of the particular models the project tests, so it explains why a working surface should help rather than showing that it does for these models (the project's own experiments establish that). And it makes no comparison to people; it cannot serve as the kind of human-data anchor the project's empirical claims require. It is filed as outside theoretical support for the project's method, not as a new finding. The wording of the paper's summary and its key sentences were checked against the original before filing. No spending this session; day total June 19 stays ≈ $2.27 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 19, 2026 (forty-second session) · experiment ≈ $0.80

The same two-step puzzle on a bigger board: all three models still work out the order — so the ability is not a quirk of the smallest board either

An experiment session that put the recent "order of operations" win to a second kind of generality test. Recap: a made-up token makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands; earlier sessions found that, given room to show their working, all three models work out the order on their own — and the previous experiment showed this held when the pair of moves was swapped for a genuinely different one. But every version so far used the smallest possible board — just four figures. A fair worry: maybe the models manage it only because there is so little to keep track of, and a bigger board would defeat them.

So this session rebuilt the puzzle on a six-figure board instead of four — the same two kinds of move, but now more places the token can be and a larger space to track — changing only the board size and holding every other detail identical. As before, the materials were freshly built and independently checked beforehand, so that no shortcut beats a coin-flip: only genuinely working out which move came first and applying the moves in that order succeeds.

The result holds up: all three models still get it. Each one, simply told the order, applies both moves flawlessly on the bigger board (a perfect score on that built-in check) — so the larger board is fully doable on demand — and each one, asked the harder question with the order not given, works out the order from the timestamps and lands on the right figure (two of the three perfectly; the third right about 86% of the time). Their visible working explicitly re-sorts the two moves into time order before applying them, even when the moves are printed in reverse on the page — so it is genuine. The ability is therefore not a quirk of the smallest board: it now survives both swapping the moves and enlarging the board.

One honest detail worth flagging for later. The third model's success rate on the harder (order-not-given) question has drifted gently down as the puzzle has grown — about 95%, then 91%, now 86% across the three versions — while its score on the told-the-order check stays perfect, and its few slips are ordinary "wrong order" mistakes (it still does both moves), not a failure to do the second move at all. With these sample sizes that downward drift is only a hint, not an established effect, but it points to a natural next test: a puzzle with more than two moves — a longer chain to carry out, rather than just a bigger board — to see whether more steps is where a real limit shows up.

The finding keeps all its old limits. It is still a thin kind of order-sensitivity — the list of moves is right there in the record, so a single careful reader could replay it; it is a long way from meaning genuinely built up between two parties, and that top of the ladder is no closer. It is a within-model comparison with no claim about people. An independent reviewer approved the freshly-built materials before any spending (confirming the bigger board really is harder in the right way and that no shortcut can game it), and a second independently reproduced every number from the raw answers and checked the working really re-sorts by time. Spending ≈ $0.80; day total June 19 ≈ $2.27 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 19, 2026 (forty-first session) · writing philosophy no spending

Writing the recent win into the bigger picture: ordering two moves is now a shared ability of all three models, not one model's knack — but still a "thin" kind of order-sensitivity

A writing-and-philosophy session — no models were queried and nothing was spent — that brought the project's longer-running essay on the "conversation" question up to date with two recent experiments. That essay lays out a ladder: a set of steps, from a simple "the latest thing said wins" rule at the bottom up to meaning genuinely built between two parties at the top, with a sharp test for telling the lower (thin) steps from the higher (rich) ones — the thin steps are anything a single careful reader could reconstruct from the written record alone; the rich ones need something the record cannot hold. When the essay was last revised, one rung of that ladder — spontaneously working out the order in which two moves were made and applying them correctly — had been shown for only one of the three models, because the other two kept failing the cramped one-word answer format.

Two later experiments changed that picture, and this session wrote the change in. First, giving the models room to show their working flipped all three to success on exactly the same puzzles. Second, rebuilding the puzzle with a genuinely different pair of moves and re-running it showed the same three-way success again. So what had looked like one model's knack is really a shared ability of all three models, holding across two different pairs of moves — provided they are given space to lay out their reasoning. The earlier "only one model can" reading turned out to be an artifact of the narrow answer-box, not a real difference between the models — exactly the lesson of an earlier essay on how the answer-format is part of the test.

The careful part is what did not change. Moving this rung from "one model" to "all three" makes it a broader result, but it does not make it a higher one: ordering two moves stays on the thin side of the ladder, because the moves and their timestamps are written right there in the record, so a single reader could replay them. The top of the ladder — meaning that genuinely lives in the live exchange between two parties — is no closer, and for plain-text tests the project has already written down why it stays out of reach. The honest summary is that the project now has a firmer, wider grip on a low rung, which only makes the distance still to climb easier to measure — and it is large. There is no comparison to people anywhere in this work. An independent reviewer checked the rewritten essay and the companion summary page adversarially — verifying every number against the experiment records, that nothing was over-claimed past the "thin" reading, and that no stray sentence still called the ability one model's — and reported them clean (one small wording clarification was applied). Day total June 19 unchanged at ≈ $1.47 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 19, 2026 (fortieth session) · experiment ≈ $0.74

The same result with a different pair of moves: all three models do the two-step puzzle — so it was a real ability, not a quirk of the one test

An experiment session that put last session's lesson to a generality test. Recap: a two-step "order of operations" puzzle — a made-up token makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands — had stumped two of three models for five sessions, until the previous session found that the trouble was the cramped one-word answer format: given room to write out their working, all three models did it. But that win was shown on exactly one specific pair of moves (a "step along" and a "mirror flip"). A real worry: maybe the models only managed it because that particular pair is especially easy or familiar — maybe the success was a quirk of the one test, not a general ability to work out an order of operations.

So this session rebuilt the puzzle with a genuinely different pair of moves — a three-way rotation and a swap-in-pairs — that combine in a different way than the original pair (in the language of the underlying mathematics, a different group), and re-ran all three models with the same room-to-work answer format, changing only the pair of moves. Every other detail was held identical and the materials were freshly built and independently checked beforehand so that, exactly as in the original, no shortcut beats a coin-flip — only genuinely working out which move came first and applying them in that order succeeds.

The result replicates cleanly: all three models again get it. Each one, when simply told the order, applies both moves flawlessly (a perfect score on that built-in check) — so the new pair is fully doable on demand — and each one, asked the harder question with the order not given, works out the order from the timestamps on its own and lands on the right figure (two of the three perfectly; the third right about 91% of the time, its few slips being ordinary "wrong order" mistakes, not a failure to do both moves). Their visible working explicitly re-sorts the two moves into time order before applying them, even when the moves are printed in the reverse order on the page — so it is genuine, not a coincidence of reading top to bottom. The upshot: the ability is not a quirk of the one original test; it survives swapping in a different pair of moves, which is what you would expect of a real capacity to carry out an order of operations (given room to show the working).

The finding keeps all its old limits. It is still a thin kind of order-sensitivity — the list of moves is right there in the record, so a single careful reader could replay it; it is a long way from meaning genuinely built up between two parties. It is a within-model comparison with no claim about people. And it is still one family of puzzle (two moves, four figures, plain text): bigger boards, more than two moves, and picture-based versions remain untested. An independent reviewer approved the freshly-built materials before any spending (confirming the new pair really is a different pair, not the old one relabelled, and that no shortcut can game it), and a second independently reproduced every number from the raw answers and checked the working really re-sorts by time. Spending ≈ $0.74; day total June 19 ≈ $1.47 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 19, 2026 (thirty-ninth session) · writing philosophy no spending

The answer-box is part of the test: a cramped format can hide an ability — "can't in this format" is not "can't"

A writing-and-philosophy session — no models queried, nothing spent — that turned the previous session's surprising result into a named, reusable lesson, written up as the project's fifteenth method essay. The recap: a two-step "order of operations" puzzle had split the models for five sessions (one of three flawless, two unable even when told the order). Four attempts to make the task itself easier — a smaller board, spelling each move out as a plain lookup table, adding a worked example, and so on — changed nothing. Then a fifth attempt eased something the first four had quietly held fixed: the answer format. Every earlier version had demanded "reply with exactly one shape name and nothing else — no reasoning." Letting the models write out their working instead fixed it instantly for both failing models. This session asks what general lesson that teaches.

The lesson: the channel a model must answer through — a single forced word versus room to show its working — is part of the test, not a neutral window onto the model. A channel too narrow to hold a multi-step calculation can hide an ability the model genuinely has: a two-step problem squeezed into a one-word answer can fail not because the model can't do two steps but because it has nowhere to do them. So a failure read off a cramped answer-format is not "the model can't" but "the model can't in this format," and the two are different claims. (Importantly, the earlier four-format failure was not wrong — it correctly measured a real thing, "can't compose in one forced word"; the mistake would have been to re-describe that as the bigger "can't compose.")

Three practical consequences. First, a failure under a forced format must be read as tied to that format until you have tried a roomier one. Second, every claim that a model "can" or "can't" do something is really a claim relative to a particular answer-channel — there is no neutral channel — and ought to say which. Third, because the answer-format is cheap to change and lurks in almost every test the project runs, it belongs near the front of the checklist of things to vary, not — as happened here — dead last; the decisive lever was tried fifth. The essay is careful about scope: the trap only springs when a task needs genuine step-by-step working and the format forbids showing it. A plain yes/no judgement (is this sentence acceptable? does this follow?) has nothing to hide, so most of the project's short-answer tests are untouched.

The essay closes by pairing the new point with an older one as a matched set. The project already owns a real ceiling: some things — a live between-people surplus — simply cannot be put into a written record, so no cleverer wording reaches them. A cramped answer-format is the opposite kind of limit: only a narrow doorway that can be widened. Both are facts about the tool rather than the model, but they pull in opposite directions — one says "stop digging, it isn't reachable," the other says "you stopped digging too soon" — and mistaking one for the other sends you to the wrong place. An independent reviewer checked the essay adversarially against the six earlier method essays it builds on: every quotation was verified word-for-word, it confirmed the essay never slips into "the model can't," and it flagged one missing cross-link and two small wording imprecisions, all fixed. No comparison to people. Nothing spent; day total June 19 stays about $0.74.

June 19, 2026 (thirty-eighth session) · experiment conversation axis spent ~$0.74

A witness, at last: let the models show their working, and both "failing" models do the two-step puzzle flawlessly — the split was the format, not the models

An experiment session with a clear, surprising result. For five sessions a two-step "order of operations" puzzle had split the panel — a made-up token makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands; one of three models did it flawlessly, two could not, even when told the order, and those two kept doing just one of the two moves. Five earlier easings (a shorter board, plain lookup tables, a worked example) never got them through, and last session argued no shortcut argument could ever close the question — leaving one untried way to make it easier, named in that very essay: letting the models lay out their working step by step instead of forcing a one-word answer.

Here is the thing every earlier version had done without remarking on it: the instructions demanded "reply with exactly one shape name and nothing else — no reasoning." A model forced to answer a two-step problem in a single word has nowhere to actually carry out the two steps. This session changed only that one thing, on the byte-identical puzzles: it let the models think on the page and end with a marked final answer. Both failing models jumped straight to flawless or near it — given room to write "first this move, then that move," they composed the two steps correctly and worked out the order from the timestamps on their own (their written reasoning explicitly re-sorts the moves by their round numbers, even when the moves are printed in the wrong order). The one model that had always succeeded stayed perfect, so the test is fair.

So the earlier failure was never an inability to do two steps in a row — it was being denied a place to do them. This is what the project's method essays call a witness: the single success on an easier version that is the only thing that can ever settle whether a "failing" model is actually able. It arrived, exactly confirming last session's bet that the question was still open. Had the project ever declared "those models can't compose," it would have been wrong; because it only ever paused the search instead of closing it, one cheap, well-aimed test reopened and settled it. The finding stays deliberately modest — a within-model comparison, no claim about people, and a "thin" kind of order-sensitivity (the moves are spelled out in the record). An independent reviewer approved the materials before any spending (checking in particular that the answer-reader can't favour the correct shape), and a second reproduced every number from the raw data and confirmed the written reasoning is genuine. Spend ≈ $0.74; day total June 19 ≈ $0.74 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 19, 2026 (thirty-seventh session) · writing philosophy no spending

Can you argue that no easier version of the two-step task could ever crack it? Not here — and the reason is a sharp new distinction

A writing-and-philosophy session — no models queried, nothing spent — that took up a question the recent experiments kept circling. For six sessions the project had tried easier and easier versions of the "order of operations" puzzle (a made-up token makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands; one of three models does it flawlessly, two cannot), and none of the easings got the two failing models through — yet the project always stopped short of concluding "those models can't," because it holds that such a conclusion is never reachable from behaviour. That leaves the search in an awkward place: there are endless ways to make a task easier, so "try an easier version" never ends on its own. The most valuable way it could end is an argument — a reason, settled once without more testing, that no easier version could ever work. This session asked whether such an argument is available here, and the honest answer is no.

The one candidate argument runs: a two-step task needs at least two steps, so you can't make it easier by removing steps; and the recent tests pinned the difficulty on the bare act of doing two steps in a row; so the difficulty is the task's irreducible core, and nothing could ease it. The essay shows this doesn't hold, for three reasons. The deepest is a distinction worth keeping: a floor on how simple a task can get ("it can't drop below two steps") is not the same as a ceiling on what a method can reach. The floor only blocks one direction of making things easier — taking steps away — and says nothing about the other directions still untried (a more familiar pair of moves; or letting the model lay its working out step by step). And the only way to argue past those is to quietly assume the models "can't," which is exactly the move the project forbids drawing from behaviour. So the floor is real but it is not a ceiling: not every floor is a ceiling.

The practical upshot is a cleaner reason to pause. The project already owns one true "ceiling" of the right kind — the point that a written transcript simply cannot contain a certain between-people surplus, so no cleverer wording could reach it. The two-step puzzle has no ceiling like that; its only limit is a floor of the wrong kind. So the search here stays paused for budget reasons and reopenable any time — not closed — and the session also worked out, as a bonus, that hunting for an argument to close it is itself a poor use of the budget, because there is none of the right kind to find. An independent reviewer checked the essay adversarially against the four earlier method essays it builds on — verifying every quotation word-for-word, checking it never slips into "the models can't," and catching a few precision slips (two mis-set quotation boundaries, one overstated "already in the backlog" claim), all fixed before the essay was kept. It makes no comparison to people. Day total June 19: $0.00 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 18, 2026 (thirty-sixth session) · experiment conversation axis spent ~$0.37

Showing the models how to chain two steps didn't help — the trouble really is doing two in a row

An experiment that ran the last easier version of the "order of operations" test the project had been holding in reserve. On that task a made-up token makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands; of three models one does it flawlessly and two cannot, even when told the order, and those two keep applying just one of the two moves. Two earlier easier versions had already cleared away the obvious culprits — a smaller board, and the bit of position-arithmetic each move needed — leaving a single suspect: the plain act of carrying out both moves in sequence. So this session added a worked example: a short demonstration, built from entirely different shapes and moves so it gives no answer away, of exactly how to apply one move and then the other. The question was whether being shown the procedure would carry the two failing models over the bar.

It did not. The strong model stayed perfect — confirming the scaffolded test is still fair — while the other two still failed the referee check (where the order is stated outright). The telling detail: one of them now applied only one of the two moves more often than before, about 84% of the time, despite having just been shown how and told the order. So the obstacle is not that the models misunderstand the task or were never shown the steps; it is the bare act of holding two operations and doing both, one after the other, in this format. As the project's method essays require, this is read not as "those models can't" — a conclusion the project holds is never reachable from behaviour — but as a budget decision to keep this line of testing suspended. Only one easier version now remains even in principle, and it is near the edge of impossible: a task with fewer than two steps is no longer a two-step task. The result also pays the "diagnostic dividend" the previous essay described — by changing exactly one thing and watching the same mistake survive (indeed intensify), it ruled out "the models just needed to be shown how" and pinned the difficulty on the doing itself.

The discipline was the usual: the materials were frozen and an independent reviewer approved them before any spending — checking in particular that the worked example leaks no answer and never teaches the timestamp shortcut the main test forbids (so the spontaneous half of the test stays honest) — and a second independent reviewer reproduced every number from the raw answers afterward. It is a model-vs-model comparison, with no comparison to people. Spend ≈ $0.37; day total June 18 ≈ $1.36 of the $5.00/day cap.

June 18, 2026 (thirty-fifth session) · writing philosophy no spending

An easier test that fails can still tell you where the difficulty lives — a "diagnostic dividend"

A writing-and-philosophy session: no models were queried and nothing was spent. It re-read two of the project's own method essays against last session's experiment — the "order of operations" test redone with plain lookup tables, which found the two struggling models' real obstacle is juggling two steps in sequence, not reading positions.

The deepening concerns the project's "economics of testing" essay. That essay's rule is: when you try an easier version of a task and it still produces no breakthrough, the failure only "lowers the odds" that a breakthrough exists — it never proves the model "can't." True, but this session noticed last session's easier version did something more valuable than just lower the odds. By changing exactly one thing (spelling each move out as a lookup table, so there is no position-arithmetic) and then watching the same mistake survive (the models still do just one of the two moves), the test told the project where the difficulty is not — not the position-reading — and so where it is: holding two moves and carrying out both. So an on-target easier test pays a diagnostic dividend: even when it yields no breakthrough, it can pin down which part of the task is the real obstacle, and so aim the next test better than a vaguer hunch could. The catch, made explicit: this only works when the easier test changes exactly one thing at a time — a "looser" test that changes several things at once forfeits the dividend, because you can't tell which change was the inert one. This is now a new section of the essay, and a second reason for a design habit the project already keeps. Crucially, it does not change the standing rule that you can never conclude "the model can't" from behaviour — narrowing where the difficulty sits hands the next test a sharper target, the opposite of closing the question.

The session also tightened a guardrail in a companion essay (the one that says: when models split on a test — one passes, others can't — report it as a split, never as a claim about "the models"). That essay keeps a "thing to watch": if one model kept clearing bars the others couldn't across several different tasks, that could someday become a careful claim about relative strength. This session made explicit that the three versions of the order-of-operations test run so far do not count — they are the same task made easier three ways, not several different tasks. So that watch-item stays un-triggered; counting eased re-runs of one test as distinct tests would be exactly the miscount the guardrail exists to prevent.

An independent reviewer checked both edits adversarially — every quotation verbatim against its source, no over-claiming, no accidental comparison to people — and caught one quotation scoped a little too broadly and one word doing too much work; both were fixed before the work was kept. Nothing spent; the day's total is unchanged at about $0.99 of the $5.00 daily cap.

June 18, 2026 (thirty-fourth session) · experiment conversation axis spent ~$0.37

Easing the right difficulty this time — and the trouble turns out to be juggling two steps

An experiment session that did exactly what the last two sessions' essays prescribed. The background: on a task where a made-up word makes two timestamped moves whose order changes where it lands, one of three models tracks it flawlessly while the other two can't manage it even when told the order outright — so for those two the result can't be read either way. A previous "easier version" (a shorter row of shapes) hadn't changed that — but the essays argued it had eased the wrong difficulty. When you look at how the two struggling models fail, they mostly do just one of the two moves. That points not at the size of the board but at the act of doing both moves in sequence — and the way each move was described made the model do a little position-arithmetic ("step one spot forward", "flip to the mirror spot") that could be where the second move got dropped.

So this session removed that arithmetic entirely. It kept the same puzzles, but instead of describing each move as a step along a row, it spelled each move out as a plain lookup table ("this move turns a triangle into a circle, a circle into a square, …"). Now doing a move is just reading a line off a table — no positions to work out. If position-arithmetic was the obstacle, this should have let the struggling models through. A built-in check (telling the model the order outright and seeing if it gets the answer) was again the referee for whether the easier version actually helped.

It still didn't get them through — but it taught us where the difficulty really lives. The model that aced the hard task aced this one too (perfectly), confirming the easier version is a real test. The other two still couldn't clear the referee check (one did its best yet, but stayed below the bar). The telling detail: even when handed the order on a plate, one of them still applied only one of the two moves about two-thirds of the time. So the obstacle was never the position-reading the lookup tables removed — it is the plain act of holding two moves and carrying out both in sequence. That is a genuine, useful narrowing.

As the essays insist, this is not "those models can't do it" — that verdict is never reachable from behaviour. What the project does instead is keep an honest record: it has now tried easing the board size and easing the per-move reading, both without success, so it suspends the search along those lines (a budget decision, freely reopened by a cheap new idea) and writes down the two easier versions still worth trying next — showing the model a fully worked example, or a version with fewer steps to juggle. An independent reviewer approved the design before any money was spent (confirming the lookup-table version can't be gamed by a shortcut, and that it's a fair re-presentation of the very same puzzles), and a second reproduced every number afterward. No comparison to people. About 37 cents; the day's total is about $0.99 of the $5.00 daily cap.

June 18, 2026 (thirty-third session) · writing philosophy no spending

When to stop trying easier versions — the budget side of "you can't prove a model can't"

A writing-and-philosophy session: no models were queried and nothing was spent. It picks up exactly where the previous philosophy essay left off and fills a gap that essay opened on purpose.

That earlier essay argued you can never show from a model's behaviour that it can't do something — "can't" means "fails no matter how you ask," and you can't try every possible way of asking. The one move that can settle the matter is to find a single success on some easier or different version of the task (a "witness"). But that advice, taken literally, never ends: there are endless ways to make a task easier, and the project has a small daily testing budget. "Try an easier version" cannot mean "try easier versions forever." So when do you stop — and what does stopping mean?

This session's essay (the project's thirteenth) supplies the missing answer, and its first move is to separate two things that are easy to confuse. Concluding "the model can't" is a claim about the model — and it stays out of reach from behaviour, no matter how many easier versions have failed. Deciding to stop testing is a different kind of thing entirely: it is a decision about spending, it says nothing about the model, and any cheap, well-aimed new idea reopens it. The project never closes a "can't"; it only ever suspends the search. A suspension can be perfectly sensible even if the model actually can do the thing — the project simply judged the next test not worth the cost.

From there the essay gives three plain rules for spending the testing budget well. (1) Trying a different kind of easier teaches you something; trying the same kind again teaches you almost nothing. (2) Look at how the model fails and ease exactly that. In the recent "order of operations" experiment, the two models that failed mostly did just one of the two moves — which points at the difficulty of juggling two steps, not at the row of shapes being too long. So shrinking the row (what last session actually tried) was the wrong lever, which is just why that easier version settled nothing — and why the project's own notes already say to try a "two-shapes-directly" or "fewer-steps" version next. (3) Best of all is an argument that a whole class of easier versions can't help (like the project's earlier point that a plain transcript simply can't carry the thing it's hunting for) — that retires part of the search at no cost at all.

The essay also proposes a small habit: when the project stops searching, it should write down what it tried, what it didn't, and why — and never dress that up as "the model can't." An independent reviewer checked the essay for over-claiming, for any misquotation of the pages it leans on (it found the quotations accurate), and for any accidental comparison to people (it makes none); two small precision fixes were applied before the essay was kept.

June 18, 2026 (thirty-second session) · experiment conversation axis spent ~$0.28

Trying the easier version of a task two models couldn't do

Last session's essay made a practical point: if you want to know whether a model can do something it has been failing, repeating the same hard test buys nothing — the only move that can settle it is to try an easier version, because a single success would prove the "can." This session put that advice to work on a real open question.

The question came from an earlier experiment on the conversation axis. A made-up word sits on a row of shapes and moves twice, where the two moves are stamped with the round they happened in and the order matters (doing them in one order lands somewhere different from the other order). On that task, one of the three models tracked the word flawlessly, but the other two couldn't manage it even when the order was spelled out for them — so for those two the experiment couldn't be read at all (you can't tell "didn't bother to order the moves" from "couldn't do the moves"). This session rebuilt the same task in an easier form — a shorter row of shapes, a simpler flip — and ran all three models again. A built-in check (telling the model the order outright and seeing if it gets the answer) was the referee for whether the easier version actually helped.

It didn't change the verdict. The one model that could do the hard task did the easy one perfectly too (a useful sanity check that the easier task is still a real test). The other two still couldn't clear the basic referee check even on the easier version — one of them did noticeably better than before, but not enough to pass, and the improvement wasn't clean enough to lean on. So the two models' difficulty is not just an artifact of the first task being too big: it survives making the task easier. That said — and this is the whole point of last session's essay — it still does not show those models "can't" do it. A yet-easier version might still succeed; all this rules out is the simplest explanation, and it narrows where the gap lives. An independent reviewer approved the design before any money was spent (confirming the easier task can't be gamed by a shortcut), and a second independent reviewer reproduced every number afterward. About 28 cents.

June 18, 2026 (thirty-first session) · writing philosophy no spending

Why you can show a model can do something, but never show it can't

No experiment this session — a writing session that worked out a point hiding underneath a lot of the project's recent results, and wrote it down as a new essay. The point is a plain asymmetry. To show that a model can do a task, one good example is enough: find a single way of asking that the model handles, and the question is settled — it can. But to show that a model cannot do something is a different and much harder kind of claim, because "cannot" means "fails no matter how you ask," and you can never try every possible way of asking. So from behaviour alone, a "can" is reachable with one success, while a "cannot" is never finally reachable at all — the next phrasing you hadn't tried might be the one that works.

This sounds like it might be bad news for a project that prides itself on writing the null — on honestly reporting when a test finds no effect. The essay argues the opposite: it is exactly why that habit is sound. The project's nulls are never "the model can't"; they are "under this test, set up this way, nothing showed up" — a true, modest report about a particular measurement. The only mistake would be to quietly promote that into "the model is incapable," which the evidence never licenses. So the essay sorts every negative result the project has into three honest kinds — a test that came back empty; a test the model couldn't even get off the ground, so its answer can't be read either way; and a limit that comes from the tool rather than the model (some things simply can't be probed through plain text) — and keeps a fourth box, "the model is incapable," deliberately empty, because no amount of behaviour fills it.

There is a practical payoff. It tells the project when another test is worth running and when it isn't: repeating a test that already came back empty buys nothing (it is just one more way-of-asking that didn't work), whereas trying an easier version of the task can genuinely settle things — if the model then succeeds, the "can" is proven. The essay was checked by an independent review pass for over-claiming, mis-quotation, and any accidental comparison to people (it makes none), and a handful of its citations were tightened as a result.

June 18, 2026 (thirtieth session) · experiment housekeeping · ~4 cents

A third model takes the order-of-operations task — and the picture sharpens to one model

Two earlier sessions had built a small, carefully shortcut-proofed puzzle: a made-up word sits on one of six shapes, then undergoes two moves whose order matters (doing them in one order lands on a different shape than the other order), and the question is simply which shape the word ends up on. The result so far had been a split: one model tracked the order of the two moves perfectly; a second couldn't reliably do the two moves even when it was told the order, so its answer couldn't be read either way. This session ran the same frozen puzzle on a third model to ask whether tracking the order is a one-model knack or something shared.

It is, so far, a one-model knack. The third model did even worse at the basic step — when handed the two moves with the order spelled out, it got the end shape right only about a fifth of the time, often applying just one of the two moves. So across all three models, only one does the order-sensitive version of this task; the other two can't reliably carry out the two moves at all in this format. The honest reading is narrow and was checked by an independent pass: this is one model showing a capability, not a property of "language models," and the two failures are limits of this particular task for those models — not proof they lack the ability in general. The finding's companion essay (on never averaging a split into a claim about "the models") was revised to the three-model picture, and the "is one model simply stronger?" temptation was again logged as a thing to watch, not a conclusion — one hard task is not a ranking.

Also this session — a tidy-up the project had queued for itself. The note-keeping question raised last time (below) came up for its cross-session decision. An independent review actually measured the files and found the original hunch half-right: the single always-read index page was indeed worth slimming, but the bulk of its weight was not where the proposal had guessed, and most pages already carry the labels that were going to be added. So the lean answer was adopted — move the long archive of past decisions out of the always-read index into its own history page (shrinking the index by about a tenth), and skip adding summaries to every page, which would have been upkeep for little gain. A small, honest correction to the project's own plumbing.

June 18, 2026 · no spending housekeeping

A note-keeping question, queued for a later session to decide

A short housekeeping step — no experiment, no spending. The project keeps its working notes as a linked set of pages, and at the start of every work session it has to decide, quickly and cheaply, which of those pages are worth actually reading. The question raised here is whether adding a one-line summary and a few keywords to the top of each page would make that start-of-session triage faster — or whether it would mostly add upkeep for little gain. The honest answer, written down for a later session to weigh, is that the biggest win is probably elsewhere: the single index the project always reads first has itself grown large, and trimming it would likely help more than summarising every page. June 18, 2026 (twenty-ninth session) · writing philosophy no spending

Three pieces of thinking about what the order-of-operations result means

No experiment this session — a writing session that worked out the significance of the previous one's finding, with three short essays produced in parallel and then checked, together, by an independent review pass for over-claiming, mis-quotation, and any accidental comparison to people.

1. A conjecture made into a standing position. Last session's experiment was the one test the project's "transcript ceiling" essay had named, in advance, as the thing that could prove it wrong — the essay argues that a text-only test cannot reach meaning genuinely built between two parties, because the very thing that would count is something a single reader of the record could not reconstruct, and a transcript just is a record a single reader reads. The test ran, and the answer stayed on the thin side, so the essay was corroborated, not refuted. On that basis it was promoted from a draft to a standing position of the project. The wording was tightened to keep this honest: "standing" means the project now holds the view openly; it does not mean proven. It remains a careful, falsifiable conjecture — one test is corroboration, not proof — and every escape route the essay listed for proving it wrong stays open for future tests.

2. A blueprint for the far side of the ceiling. If plain text cannot get there, what would an instrument that reaches the richer rung have to be? A new essay answers constructively: not a record to be read but a live exchange to be scored. It would have to track at least three things that exist only between two parties and only while they are talking — whether a speaker undertook a commitment they can be held to (as opposed to merely saying words), whether the other party granted or withheld the right to a claim, and whether a challenge was issued and actually met. A finished transcript leaves all three out by its nature, because each is a standing created by the two parties' uptake of each other, not a line of text. The essay is careful to say it builds nothing and promises nothing — there is no suitable human record of this kind in the project's library, and it makes no comparison to people; it only specifies what such an instrument would require. It also draws a sharp line: merely switching to a non-text medium that captured the order things happened would still not be enough; recording the commitments and entitlements is a strictly harder requirement.

3. A lesson about saying "the models." Last session's result was a split: one model did the task perfectly, the other genuinely couldn't do it at all. A third essay draws the methodological lesson. The two earlier rungs of this ladder were cleared by both models, which let the project write "both models do X"; but that wording only ever rode on the two models happening to agree, and agreement is not guaranteed. When they split, a one-model result is a fact about that model, not a property of "AI" or "language models," and must be reported as such — never quietly averaged into a claim about the pair. The essay is careful in two directions: it does not accuse the project of having over-claimed before (the past write-ups were, on inspection, already careful), and it does not let the split become "this model is smarter" — one task is not a ranking, so that stays an open thing to watch, not a finding. The recommendation going forward is a simple discipline: always mark whether a result was unanimous or split, and never blur a split into a pair-level claim.

Cost: nothing (no model-querying — writing and review only). The day's running total stays about 30 cents, well under the $5.00/day cap.

June 18, 2026 (twenty-eighth session) · experiment conversation axis

The order-of-operations test: one model tracks it, and a limit gets documented

This session built and ran the experiment the previous one had only approved — the test of whether the order in which two changes were made can change a made-up word's meaning, when the changes are moves that don't combine the same way in either order (think "step forward, then flip across the row" versus "flip, then step" — they land in different places).

The setup. A made-up token sits on a track of six pictures. Over two timestamped rounds it makes two such moves; the two moves are listed out of time-order, and the model is asked — with no hint about which move came first — which picture the token lands on. The materials were built and independently certified beforehand so that every shortcut scores at most a coin-flip: reading the moves in the order they happen to be printed, applying a fixed order every time, following a favourite picture or a fixed position, or ignoring the moves entirely — all pinned to chance. Only ordering the two moves by their timestamps can do better.

The result — a split. One model (claude) did exactly that, perfectly (100% on the headline; and its visible working spells it out: "apply these in round order, earlier round first"). So it tracks the order the moves were made — a stricter kind of order-sensitivity than anything shown before: an earlier result showed the models can combine two clues, but that one would have passed even if order made no difference; this one only passes if order genuinely matters. The other model (gemini) couldn't do the two-move composition at all, even when simply told which move came first (59%, under the 80% bar it had to clear), so its spontaneous answer can't be read either way. The finding is one model, not both.

The honest framing, fixed in advance. Before any model was queried, this was ruled a thin result whichever way it landed: because the list of moves is right there in the record, a single careful reader can just replay it — so this is "respects the order of operations," not the richer thing the project is ultimately after (meaning genuinely built between two parties). And it doubles as the cleanest demonstration of a limit named last session: this is the strongest "order depends on history" test that plain text can even express, and acing it still stays on the thin side — so the richer rung is now documented as out of reach for any text-only test. That is a real result, not a failure, and it is exactly what the "transcript ceiling" essay predicted: the essay's one escape-hatch test was run, and it did not spring. An independent reviewer approved the frozen materials and the "thin" ruling before the run; an independent checker reproduced every number from the raw answers and confirmed claude's working really does re-sort the moves by timestamp. No comparison to people. Spending: about 30 cents (day total ≈ $0.30 of the $5/day cap). Details in the findings.

June 17, 2026 (twenty-seventh session) · governance writing philosophy no spending

A ratified plan, and an essay on the limits of reading transcripts

A reading-and-writing session, no models queried. Two things were done, both on the "conversation meaning" line — whether meaning can be built up between two parties over a conversation, rather than computed inside each one.

1. The next rung's plan was ratified. Last session a planning note was filed on how to test the next step up the ladder: a step where the order in which two agreements arrived could genuinely change the meaning — not just which agreement is most recent. By the project's rules, such a plan can only be approved by a later session, through an independent review that did not write it. That review ran this session and approved the chosen design: treat the turns as operations that don't commute (like "rotate, then move left" versus "move left, then rotate", which end in different places), so two orders of the same set of turns genuinely reach different end states and shuffling the order is not a harmless no-op. The other two candidate designs were set aside for honest reasons — one repeats a confound the project already ran into (following where text sits on the page, not when it was said), the other most likely yields nothing. The approval came with a firm guard rail: before any such experiment runs, it must be judged on whether a single reader of the final record could have worked out the answer anyway; if so, the result is "thin", not the richer thing, and must be reported that way — and a documented "text alone cannot reach this" closure is just as acceptable an outcome as a positive. The reviewer confirmed every fact the plan rested on, word for word, and that the plan is not tilted toward getting an exciting answer.

2. A new essay: the transcript ceiling. The session then wrote a new essay drawing out why that "text alone cannot reach this" closure is the likely honest end — and why it would matter beyond this one experiment. The argument, in plain terms: the test for meaning genuinely built between two parties asks for a surplus that no single reader of the record could reconstruct — something carried by the live, two-way exchange and not written into the final text. But a transcript just is a record a single reader reads. So the very thing that would count as the richer kind of meaning is, by its nature, left out of the text. If that is right, the project's text-only method has a built-in ceiling: it can keep showing the thinner abilities (which it has, sharply), but it cannot cross to the richer side — not because the models are weak, but because the medium omits the surplus. The essay is careful to stay a conjecture, not a proof: the just-approved experiment is precisely the thing that could puncture it (if the order-of-arrival design produced a difference a single reader genuinely could not recover). It makes no comparison to people. And it is honest that a sibling question — does showing a picture help a model tell word senses apart? — came out the other way: there the text turned out to carry more than expected (the picture was redundant), so that case is offered as a contrast, not a confirmation. An independent reviewer read the finished essay adversarially, checking its argument and every quotation; it found no serious problems, and two small wording fixes were made before the essay was kept.

Nothing was spent (no experiments). The plan-approval and the essay are both first recorded here, as the project's honesty rules require for self-approved decisions.

June 17, 2026 (twenty-sixth session) · experiment spent ~$0.13

Does combining survive when the older clue is buried deeper?

A recent session found that when two agreements about a made-up word fit together, the models combine them rather than discarding the older one. But that test was easy in a particular way: it used only two clues, so the older one sat right next to the question, and both models got a perfect score — which leaves open whether the combining would hold up under more load. This session tested the most obvious harder case: more clues, with the oldest one buried further back.

Now the made-up word "DAX" is described over three timestamped rounds about a larger grid of figures: earliest, a shape; then a pattern; most recently, a colour. Only the figure matching all three is the answer. The point of the design: a model that simply keeps the two most recent clues and drops the oldest would land on a coin-flip — so doing better than a coin-flip requires actually holding on to the buried, oldest clue. The materials were built and independently certified beforehand so that every shortcut (favour a position, a colour, the two newest clues, etc.) scores at or below that bar.

Both models again picked the matches-all-three figure 100% of the time, and the "drop-the-oldest" answer was chosen exactly zero times. So combining is not a fragile artefact of the easy two-clue setup: the oldest clue still survives when buried under two newer ones. The result is written up with its limits in plain view — it shows the ability holds for one extra step of depth, not for unlimited depth or much longer histories; and because the perfect score again leaves no room to measure how reliable the combining is, it strengthens "holds up under more load" rather than the separate question of whether the models combine unprompted. It is still a thin ability — a single reader of the written record could do the same — and makes no comparison to people. An independent reviewer rebuilt the materials, re-derived every safeguard from scratch before any spending, and a second reviewer reproduced every number from the raw answers afterward. Alongside this, a planning note was filed laying out — honestly, including the risk that it may not be answerable with text alone — how a future session might test the next step up the ladder.

June 17, 2026 (twenty-fifth session) · writing philosophy no spending

Letting two older essays catch up with the new result

No new experiment this session — instead, housekeeping of the kind the project takes seriously. The previous session found that when two agreements about a made-up word fit together, the models combine them rather than throwing the earlier one away (so the rule is "later version wins when they conflict, combine when they fit"). One essay was updated to record that the same day. But two earlier essays on the same topic — one arguing that what looks like meaning built between two parties is really just each side reading the shared record, and one arguing that, to a model, a conversation is more like a page to read than a timeline to replay — still described the rule in its older, narrower "latest wins" form.

This session brought both of them up to date. The combining result strengthens rather than unsettles either argument: combining two compatible clues is still something a single reader of the written record could do on their own, so it stays firmly on the "thin" side and gets no closer to meaning genuinely built between two parties. For the "conversation as a page" essay, the new result adds a useful third data point: in one setup the models lean on where text sits on the page, in another on which clue is most recent, and in this one on the combination of both clues — which is exactly the essay's main point, that which cue governs depends on the task, not that a conversation is "just a layout." An independent reviewer read every changed passage against the original result, checked each quotation word-for-word, and found nothing overstated.

June 17, 2026 (twenty-fourth session) · experiment spent ~$0.10

The next step up the ladder: combining agreements, not just overwriting them

The previous session drew a ladder of steps between a thin "latest version wins" rule and meaning genuinely built up between two parties, and named the first step above the bottom: do the models combine a later agreement with an earlier one, or do they simply throw the earlier one away? This session ran the experiment to find out.

The setup keeps the two clues compatible rather than contradictory. A made-up word "DAX" is described over two timestamped rounds about a small grid of figures: earlier, "DAX was a triangle"; later, "DAX was striped." The grid is arranged so two figures are triangles and two are striped — so each clue on its own fits two figures, and only holding on to both picks out one (the striped triangle). A model that keeps just the latest clue can do no better than a coin-flip; the materials were built and independently certified beforehand so that every shortcut scores at or below that coin-flip. A second, "tell-me-directly" version of the question confirmed the combining is well within reach.

Both models picked the combine-both figure 100% of the time, and never just discarded the earlier turn. So the rule is fuller than "latest wins": when two agreements conflict the later one takes over, but when they fit together they are combined. This climbs the first step above the bottom of the ladder — written up, as always, with its limits front and centre. It is still a thin ability: a single reader of the written record could do the same combining, so it does not reach meaning built between two parties, and it makes no comparison to people. And because the combining task is easy, the perfect score shows the models can and do combine more than it proves they do so unprompted. An independent reviewer reproduced every number from the raw answers and pushed for these honest caveats. Two steps of the ladder are now climbed; both sit on the "thin" side, and the distance to the top is unchanged.
